TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE
SYMPTOMS, TREATMENT, AND CURE.
rpHE directions contained in this little treatise will be
found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous
or Sexual Debility, JVo Medicines will be required , as, by
observing the directions given, the patient can Cure Him-
self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the' treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer from the following complaints, viz.:—Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy.
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.
If the complaint is of long standhig and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OP NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike any
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate Ms statements by verifying
tlie testimonials published in his treatise. The treatise
will be forwarded on receipt of two stamps to cover
postage.

Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism.— Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils, lie thinks
i he important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.

Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will

SEND FOR m. PERRY'S FAMOUS WORK ON DISEASES OF THE REPRODUCTIVE ORGANS, FREE ON APPLICATION.
TSr-A-G-SjVCEnSTT.
CONSULT DR. PERRY, M.R.C.S., L.S.A., etc.
(REGISTERED BY THE MEDICAL BOARD OF NEW SOUTH WALES),
Late House Surgeon to the Great Northern Hospital, Caledonian Road, London,
SPECIALIST in the Treatment of Diseases of the
Reproductive Organs and Nervous .
Dr. PERRY was induced to publicly advertise himself as a
Specialist in this branch of the profession in consequence of the number
of persons suffering’ from diseases of this nature remaining uncured ; and
this is not surprising, when it is remembered, in consequence of the
general dislike of the profession to undertake these cases, the public
health has suffered, because .the majority of persons advertising to treat
these diseases, and calling themselves Doctors, Professors, etc.,
Are Neither the One nor the Other,
and exist chiefly because DULY QUALIFIED MEDICAL PRAC-
TITIONERS have not occupied the field.
Patients can, therefore, readily see the advantage of being treated
by a practitioner who has gone through a thorough Medical, Surgical and
Anatomical Training in the best hospitals ; and the Doctor argues that,
although it may be considered contrary to antiquated medical etiquette
to so advertise, yet he is promoting the usefulness and thk dignity of
the profession more by searching out and relieving this class of disease,
than by leaving them severely alone to the care of men who, however good
their intentions, have neither the opportunities or advantages of a regular
gedical training to become acquainted with the best methods of treating
these complex forms of disease.
m

Dr. 3PDDIE3*3r
Will be happy to advise persons suffering from these
diseases, and as, in many cases,
Nothing hut the observance of a few simple directions
as to Self-Management
is required to place the patient in a healthy state, all who
are in any way affected should consult him, and thus have
the benefit of his advice and experience.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER G, 1890.
Strikes.
The very prevalence of strikes during the
century—which has seen their inauguration
as a recourse of discontented Labour—indi-
cates the existence of a peculiar condition,
politically and socially. Labour on strike is
Labour in the sulks. The notable fact is that
Labour dares to sulk, and is permitted to
sulk with impunity—so far as governmental
interference is concerned. 11 is little more
than half a century since Labour was per-
mitted to show temper. Prior to 1825, any
tantrum of this kind ivas visited with per-
emptory and severe punishment. If a single
workman refused his services, nobody cared.
The man simply hurt no one but himself ;
and, as to himself, ho perished. But com-
binations of workmen, by refusing to sell
their services at the rate the capitalist found
it convenient to dole out to them, hurt the
capitalist as well as the labourer. The
earlier ruling authorities of England, being
either feudal lords, priests, or landlords,
looked down with supercilious contempt on
;dl industrials, distinguishing little, in the
hugeness of their arrogance, between master
mid man —base, mechanical persons all. But
/the masters w'ere nearest, the lords and the
I squires and the lords spiritual, and had a
trifle better access to their ear. Hence,
from the very inception of legislation
dealing with employment, its whole trend
and force has been in favour of master
against man. The Statute-book has been
burdened with a load of enactments
strengthening the hands of the former and
hearing down the resistance of the latter.
Combination in especial was assailed by a
series of cruel and drastic law's. In the
reign of Edward YI. a repeated, breach of
Acts aimed against combinations of workmen
involved the offender in a crushing fine,
with the alternative of the loss of an ear and
practical outlawry. Elizabeth repealed this
particular law, but the drift of legislation
continued in the same direction. During
the evil days of the Georges, trade after
trade w 7 as brought under the lash of laws
raised against combinations, and when the
third, and stupidest, George was on the
throne the hands of masters were braced
more strongly than ever by a hypocritical
law which ostensibly held an even balance
iby prohibiting Combinations aihong masters
as well as among workers, and making pro-
vision for arbitration in cases of dispute.
The former provision was, of course,
nugatory. As Adam Smith, wdio wrote his
great work a quarter of a century earlier,
therein remarks : —-“Masters are always and
everywhere in a sort of tacit, but constant
»nd uniform, combination not to raise the
wages of labour above their actual rate . .
Masters, too, sometimes enter into particular
combinations to sink the wages of labour
even below this rate. These are always
conducted with the utmost silence and
secrecy. ... We seldom, indeed, hear
of this combination” (alluding to the former),
“because it is the usual and, as one may
say, the natural state of things which nobody
ever hears of.”
# * *
It must, therefore, have been evident to
the framers of the combination laws of
George 111. that, although ostensibly double-
edged, so as to cut combiners either among
capitalists or workmen, only one edge was
sharp. The entire system, however, broke
down. In 1824, a committee of the House
of Commons had to be appointed to review
the whole situation. This Committee re-
ported that these professedly equal laws
were “instruments of oppression in the
hands of employers, who had the means of
putting them in force against their men,
while no case pros known to the (Jiruim Ittce of
an employer belay punished water them , even
in the most flagrant cases of conspiracy
against the interests of artizans.” Our
quotation is from Harriet Martineau’s
“ History of The Peace. ” The consequence
was fresh legislation, which abolished the
anti-combination laws- between thirty or
forty having to be repealed. But in the
very next year that relieving law 7 was itself
repealed, and replaced by fresh legislation,
which rendered penal, “combinations for con-
trolling employers by moral violence”—what-
ever that may mean. Such associations are
now subject to common law —a convenient
way of placing Labour under the lash of the
courts, without defining the line which
separates their liberty of resistance from
criminal interference with the “ Rights of
Property.”
* 1 * *
Hence the situation of workmen in relation
to combined efforts to improve their condi-
tion remains tq-day very insecure. The
repeal of 80 or 40 penal enactments has by
no means exhausted the resources of the
capitalist class, so long as the common law is
at its disposal, and the Bench is recruited
from its ranks and sympathises with its senti-
ments. But, apart from those advantages,
laws remaining in the Statute-book and not
designed to bear upon strikers or unionists
can he made to serve. After the second
attempt at legislation just referred to,
Capital was desperately scared and rendered
furious by the first attempt ever made by
the mere clodhoppers—the agricultural
labourers—to follow 7 the example of the
artizan class and form unions. This was
about 1834. Sharp lawyers were set to work
to root among decayed and obsolete statutes,
and, in fact, to scrutinise the whole code in
order to discover whether there might not
exist some shred of unrepealed tyranny re-
lating to labour, or some scrap of law 7 bear-
ing on other matters which could be twisted
into a whip for the audacious Hodge. Such
a scrap was found, and complaisant judges
being, of course, on hand for the dirty job,
six agricultural unionists w 7 ere duly indicted
under an obsolete statute, really framed to
deal with cases of mutiny in the navy, and
were sentenced to seven years’ transpor-
tation.
* * ■*'
There is reason to believe that a somew hat
similar process is being resorted to at the
present moment by the Government of New 7
South Wales with reference to the existing
strike. It lias been stated in an evidently
official paragraph in the daily papers, that
the Crown law 7 officers are engaged in in-
vestigating .whether the leaders of the pre-
sent strike cannot be reached by the law.
This has much the air of a groping amidst
the back-alloys of the law's in search.of some
obscene crick by which the known enact-
ments and generally understood common
law may be supplemented by s me worm-
eaten statute or obsolete precedent. The
mere text of the codes is really of less conse-
quence than the spirit of their administra-
tors. If a Government and a bench of
judges write sympathising with one class of
disputants and denouncing the other class,
there will usually he discovered a method of
giving penal effect to their joint inclinations.
But Ministerial and judicial Partingtons
are equally impotent to stem a tide in the
world’s progress. Especially is this true of
the Ministers and judges of a petty province.
The conflict between Capital and Labour will
not be decided by events in New' South
Wales. This is a truth just as significant for
one sideas for the other. 1 f Labour becrushed
and conquered in Great Britain, the greatest
success which could conceivably be accom-
plished by an effort of Union Labour in New 7
South Wales—nay, by federated labour in
Australia —could not avert eventual defeat.
The fugitives from the field of battle in
Great Britain would break in upon the vic-
torious Australian labour and throw them
into miserable disorder. Immigration would
attain such proportions as would overcrow'd
the labour-market and render the employer
master of the situation. Similarly Capital
in New South Wales will have achieved but
a barren triumph should it successfully
emerge from the present crisis, if the masses
get the upper hand in Great Britain.
* # *
There are numerous indications that the
masses will, before long, get the upper hand
there. The present juncture and the social
history of the century point to a peculiar
condition. The continual occurrence of
strikes indicates a period of balance. Labour
is stronger than it was, and is bold enough
and pow erful enough to take up and main-
tain an attitude of nearly passive—although
menacing—resistance. Capital is yet strong
enough to sustain itself, but has almost lost
the supremacy which, till the present half-
century, enabled it to bring the whole force
of the State to bear against its antagonist.
For years the opposing classes have thus
confronted one another, neither being
weighty enough to turn the beam against its
antagonist. Even that attitude marked a
previous gain achieved by Labour. And an
equipoise of this character is never in-
definitely prolonged. One side or other
acquires weight. Now, every recent event
seems to indicate that the masses are about
to gain the advantage. If so, their attitude
of mere resistance will, in the very nature of
things, be changed for one of aggression.
As yet the worker can do no more than refuse
to work, while thereby forfeiting his means
of livelihood. Presently lie may find himself
in a position not only-to refuse to work for a
master, but able to resort to the alternative
most advantageous to himself and most
destructive to his antagonist—of working,
in combination, and with the countenance
and active assistance of the State, for his own
benefit and in direct competition with the
employer class.
# # #
To effect this momentous alteration of con-
ditions, no very extraordinary acceleration
of the present rate of democratic progress
will be required. The first essential is that
a larger proportion of the wage-earning class
shall recognise what their true interest is.
Every disturbance which arrests and con-
centrates attention such as a strike
operates in this direction. It sets men think-
ing who have not before troubled to think.
It sets in agitation a flood of discussion on
the very topics and principles which the
intelligent workmen desire to see debated.
And in reality this is about all that strikes
as at present conducted do achieve for the
worker. They are costly and painful methods,
but in this regard they are effectual ones.
Once a sufficient majority of the working
class is thus educated to the pitch of com-
prehending the essentials of the social pro-
blem, the rest must follow by an easy pro-
gression. The multitude will storm the
legislature, unless it be in a position to take
possession of it by quiet exertion of existing
rights, not previously properly understood
and turned to account. Once in possession
of the Legislature, there is nothing to stop a
complete organisation on co-operative prin-
ciples. The whole machinery and power of
Government will he the servant of Labour,
instead of its master and oppressor. Legis-
lative authority resembles the lamp of
Aladdin. Whoever possesses it is master of
a mighty genius, able and willing to perform
the must difficult undertakings, and prompt
to quell resistance from whatever quarter it
may proceed. Capital at this moment is the
African wizard who has obtained, by fraud,
possession of the lamp. If the Labor-
Aladdin but once regain the lamp, the
wizard’s day of power is ended.
* * *
Every day, indications that the period of
balance and suspense is nearly over, accumu-
late. Strikes multiply, and occur among
bodies of people who hitherto have been the
blind tools used by the classes for suppressing
the masses, whenever the latter thought too
much, and appeared likely to make an effort
to throw off their servitude. In Great
Britain, postmen strike, policemen strike,
and- -most significant of all —soldiers strike !
There is something of almost grotesque
significance in the names of the bodies of
soldiery who have recently “gone out on
strike.” The first to strike were the English
Guards. These have been followed by the
“Queen’s Own ” Hussars, after an interlude
filled by the “Royal” Inniskillens and the
“ Royal ” Artillery. It is worthy of remark
that the earliest phase of the first French
revolution was the defection of the monarch’s
“French Guards.” That the inferences
deducible from these conditions are not mere
vain imaginings of our own, may be learned
by reference to the folio wing bit of moralising
by the London correspondent of the S.M.
Herald in his latest letter ;
The men have been influenced by the general levelling
agitation that is going on throughout the land among
artisans, postmen, and police ; and the fact of this agita-
tion has to be looked in the eye and met. The lowering
of the constitutional fences, the extension of the fran-
chise, and the general “democratising” of our institu-
tions are now bearing fruit. Those who live longest will
see the most; but it looks as if the men of broadcloth are
going to have a rough time of it in England. Mr. Burns,
and such like persevering men, unchecked by law or
order, are sowing dissatisfaction right and left. There is
no knowing how broadcloth may not have to stand up for
its rights.
# # #
Property and Privilege are staggering and
floundering under the series of blows they
are subjected to. They have hitherto
counted on the military and police to crush
the people when desired. But now they
have to grapple with the problem how to
quell the police and the military. A most
inconvenient amount of sympathetic unison
has been exhibited between the people and
the offending soldiery. Attend again to the
correspondent of the S.M. Herald :
The departure of the Guards for Bermuda was a touch-
ing sight. They looked healthy and capable. They
marched as no troops in the world march—firm of foot,
with a superb carriage.
This last bit is, of course, bunkum. The
correspondent has not been able to shake
off his habit of writing Rule-Britannia
blather. It is part of his nature to froth
at the mouth enthusiastic twaddle about
Her Majesty’s Guards, and to lead fools to
fancy that the German Guard regiments, and
the French Guards, and the Russian Guards
are mere goose-step recruits compared with
“our splendid household troops.” He had
forgotten for the moment that these were
mutineers sentenced to transportation, and
that his cue should have been
like this : “ Showing in their shame-faced
aspect and slovenly bearing the evil
effects of resistance to lawful discipline.”
However, let him continue in his own way
Here are some more unconsidered testimonies
from his pen I* ‘ There was a tremendous
crowd to cheer them on their way.” Next
another slice of forgetful slobber: —“ They all
went forth as if inspired by a most perfect
sense of duty !” And listen to another naive
confession : —“Not since the same regiment
marched to sail for the Soudan has so much
sympathetic enthusiasm been evoked.” “The
Coldstream guardsmen turned out in force
to do honour to their comrades”—their com-
rades, the banished mutineers, who had just
previously been told by the Duke of Cam-
bridge, Commander-m-Chief, and'Uncle to
the Queen, that they had disgraced the name
of soldier !
“The “Local Producer.”
In the course of a sorrowful lament upon the
present industrial crisis Sydney Daily Tele,
graph strikes a new and brilliant argument in
favour of foreign-trade. The policy of the
importers who live by the destruction of local
industry is fast wiping-out the resources of
New South Wales, and leaving the country
more and more dependent on foreign supplies •
and consequently prices rise to famine rates
whenever a partial stoppage of communication
leaves the community to rely in a great measure
on its own unaided efforts. The daily bread of
New South Wales is supplied by the protected
colonies which languish in the darkness of
political error, and when these morally and
mentally degraded races are no longer able to
come to her relief serious results immediately
ensue. Like England, but in a less degree, New
South Wales can be pretty well reduced to
starvation and submission by any enemy who
can cut-off her communications with the outer
world; but the Telegraph, in a moment of
frenzied inspiration, propounds the theory that
her difficulties arise, not because her industries
have been squashed, but because they haven’t
been squashed enough ; and now that a limited
measure of Freetrade has left her helplessly
dependent on her protectionist neighbours, it
urges that more Freetrade is the thing that is
wanted to raise her to her proper position.
And not content with putting the cart before
the horse in the customary manner of Free-
trade logicians, it proceeds to pile that vehicle
on top of the unhappy animal in the-following
manner:—
So much for the present aspect of this lamentable crisis.
The effects of it are already becoming manifest, and, as
usual, the heaviest weight of the consequences fall not
upon the particular capitalists whom the unions are fight-
ing, but upon the masses out of whom trades-unionism
draws its membership, and upon the community generally.
The first of its results has been to upset the equilibrium
of the produce and breadstuff market in a manner which
will enrich a few persons while inevitably bringing hard-
ship and hunger to thousands. All the results of a highly
protective tariff can now he temporarily seen in fairly'
good working order in our midst. Outside supplies have
been almost entirely cut off in several lines, and the local
producers are thus enabled to levy high charges upon
consumers for certain of the ordinary necessaries of life.
This is bad enough, hut it is not the worst. At the very
inception of the strike keen-sighted speculators bought up
practically the whole supply of some of the articles and
are still holding for the higher prices which must ulti
mately prevail. It is quite within the scope of probability
that some of the shipowners have “hedged” in this
manner, and that whatever they might lose through the
laying up of their vessels will be recouped in part or ia
full from the consumer’ pockets.
* * *
In the first place, it will be noted that-
according to the Telegraph —it is only the
fiendish “local producer” who has raised the
prices of the necessaries of life, and the Free-
trade party having neglected to hang or other-
wise utterly abolish that degraded individual in
the days of plenty and prosperity, he is now
rising to affluence at the nation’s expense. From
this it may be gathered that the importer who
deals in foreign grain and vegetables has not
raised his prices at all, or otherwise taken a
mean advantage of the country’s extremity. It is
one of the striking characteristics of the Foreign-
trade party that it always curses the local pro-
ducer, and pines for his obliteration. It hates
the manufacturer because he interferes, no
matter in how small a degree, with the sale of
foreign shoddy, and it loathes the farmer
because, even in his decayed and ruined state,
he is still an obstruction to the trade in produce
imported from America, Victoria, South Aus-
tralia, and other countries which, under a
Protectionist regime, are able not only to fill
their own requirements, but to sell their sur-
plus food supply to the unhappy Freetrade
community in Eastern Australia. The dream
of the Freetrade monopolist is to abolish all
this internal rivalry, and leave one-half the
population to live on the production of sheep
and wool in the grassy plains of the interior,
while the other half unloads foreign produce
from nigger-manned ships at Circular Quay.
But so far this golden vision has been only par-
tially realised, and consequently the last rem-
nant of the local producer has raised the price
of the food supply of Sydney. If Freetrade
had been carried to its logical conclusion ten
years ago this catastrophe would have been
averted, for there would have been no local food
supply to raise, and the local producer couldn’t
raise it, because he would have gone to join the
dead C.ksars and other equally extinct public
institutions.
* # #
Still, if the much-cursed local producer were
allowed to raise sufficient to supply the require-
ments of the nation, it is obvious that the
present difficulty would have been avoided. It
is only because N. S. Wales is forced by the
Freetrade system to buy what she would other-
wise produce for herself, that the stoppage of
outside assistance becomes a matter of any im-
portance. The ports of America, of New Zea-
land, or of Victoria might be blockaded till the
day of resurrection, and the only result would
be to cheapen the principal necessaries of life
by forcibly retaining the surplus in the country.
In a low, mean, underhand, protectionist manner
these countries manage to grow their own food-
supply, despite the fact that none of them has
anything like the agricultural resources in pro-
portion to population which are possessed by


New South Wales, and wasted in supporting
fj tr p je of M‘Millan and the other descen-
Unts of the importing horse-leech. The insane
I o wl that Protection is synonymous with scar-
■itv i s merely the repetition of the old cry of
private Murphy that all the rest of the regi-
icnt is out of step. If Protection meant scar-
H, tv and Freetrade were another name for abun-
dance, then Britain and New South Wales
would not be reduced by a Freetrade system to
depend on their Protectionist neighbours for
their daily bread and cabbage. It is entirely
oW i n g to the enlightened policy of the adjacent
provinces that the shortness of supplies now
Prevailing in the markets of Sydney has not
already given place to hunger and absolute
famine. If all Australia were run on the politi-
cal doctrine of Parkks and Gobden and Sydney
Gaily Telegraph, then all Australia would be
equally dependent on imported produce, and
already the worn-out cab-horse would be com-
'pounded into pies, and the cat in the back-yard
would develope into soup, and the old family
do" would be roasted for dinner and served up
j 1 and those citizens who were unable to
afford such luxuries would lie flying from their
homes to lead a precarious existence on rabbit
in the far interior.
# * #
It is the favourite contention of that brand of
imported dry-goods politician, who is the stand-
ing curse of New South Wales, that Freetrade
carries with it the inestimable blessing of a
cheap loaf, and though there is a painful lack
of coherence about this statement, in view of
the conditions now prevailing, and considering
the fact that the cheap loaf, when it is to be
had, comes from a protected country, the point
is of comparatively little importance. Britain,
the shining example and illustration which is
dished up with weary iteration by every
Freetrade orator, has at least obtained a
loaf of some kind despite her suicidal policy,
and she eats it sitting on the verge of a
volcano. So long as Britain commands
the sea her existence as a nation is secure,
hut should the long run of good fortune
which has attended her for centuries at last
<dve out, as such good fortune will and must do,
sooner or later, the full significance of Cobden’s
"real scheme will begin to be appreciated. For
by the logical operation of that same scheme
Britain is the only nation in existence to which
a three-months’ blockade means irredeemable
ruin. Even Mexico or Belgium would not be
half so helpless as the mother country if another
De Ruyter or De Witt should succeed in
closing her seaports for a few short weeks, and
her fleet once destroyed any fifth-rate maritime
Power on earth could starve her into the sur-
render of half her empire, and could reduce
Britain to the meanest rank among European
nations. Freetrade has successfully placed a
nation of 37,000,000 souls in such a situation
that one great disaster may wipe out eight cen-
turies of progress and leave a fallen people to
die like a rat in a hole, or else surrender at
discretion. No doubt such a danger seems
almost incalculably remote, yet the reality may
come at any moment, and the fact that Britain
has held the seas for two long centuries since
the day when the victorious Dutchman block-
aded London and dictated peace in the Thames,
is merely a proof that the catastrophe is two
centuries nearer. New South Wales, by her
servile imitation of Britain’s policy, has suc-
cessfully placed herself, so far as her geographi-
cal position will allow, in the same precarious
situation, and the howl of the Telegraph
against the “local producer” eloquently ex-
presses the indignation of the dominant party
at those circumstances which have left the great
experiment incomplete.
*. # *
The average Australian Freetrader is merely
the pawnbroker and the bill-discounter in dis-
guise. The shoddy has eaten into his soul, and
lie is prepared to sell his country in order that
imported pants may go up, and candles of
foreign manufacture realise an extra halfpenny
per pound. To him the nation is merely a
mouldy trader in a dingy office, and national
progress means an extra 23,- per cent., and
ambition is only another name for a hungry
desire to trade off a crate of German merchan-
dise at a high figure, and collect the proceeds
promptly subject to the usual discount. The
hopes and brains and intelligence of the nation
may demand some surer material resources than
a precarious trade in foreign calico, but then
Providence built the nation that it might buy
foreign calico, and a nation which doesn’t buy
calico isn’t worth possessing. Even the Creator
himself, according to the Freetrade apostle, is
down on protective tariffs, and His mission is
to be a mysterious Power shoving the import
business along with an unseen yet omnipotent
hand. And yet even under this beneficent dis-
pensation there has arisen here and there a
malevolent spectre known as the “ local pro-
ducer,” who has attempted to grow corn and
potatoes contrary to the dictates of the Gospel,
and has opposed himself to that great moral
axiom which says that M‘Millan and his tribe
shall import all that the country requires, and
sell it at a large profit. And the sorrow of the
Freetrade newspaper is that this sinful indi-
vidual has taken advantage of his enemy’s diffi-
culties and raised his prices. It is consoling to
reflect that if the present fiscal policy lasts out
another decade in New South Wales, he will
not be alive to repeat the experiment.
The Reward of Labour.
Under the competitive method of production
the value of all products, according to Karl
Marx, is diverted into three channels. The
first receives the quantity necessary for the
sustenance of the producer, the second the sum
necessary for the replacement of the capital ex-
hausted in reproductive effort, and the third the
surplus value—which may again be subdivided
into rent, interest and profits. Labour is the
first necessity of production, and labour-power
cannot exist without sustenance; therefore,
Labour’s claims must be fully satisfied ere
further apportionment takes place. If Capital,
as exemplified in the instruments of toil, be not
continually replaced, then industry must cease
to be fully remunerative ; therefore, the second
share is fitly devoted to working expenses, while
the balance, if any, is apportioned as has been
already shown. The failure of any industrial
enterprise, as a rule, may be put down either to
bad management or to the fact that the profits
withdrawn by monopoly have been so great as
to encroach on the amount necessary for the re-
placement of used-up capital. The amount of
use-values absolutely necessary for the susten-
ance of the worker must, whether industries be
profitable or not, be equally sustained, and any
attempt to reduce the amount withdrawn for
that purpose must prove disastrous to produc-
tion, by reducing the force and intensity,
physical and intellectual, of labour-power.
* # *
Viewed from a national standpoint, all exer-
tions of labour-power which result in a return
of products sufficient for the support of the
producer and the replacement of capital, are
profitable. Viewed from the capitalistic stand-
point, only those exertions which, in addition,
provide surplus value, can be thus classified.
Now, surplus value can only be realised when
labour produces more than is required for its
support; this it does only when the articles
produced bear a use-value for a lengthier time
than that expended in their production. There-
fore, the average exchange-value of any article,
perishable or imperishable, is mainly determined
by the amount of labour-power expended on its
production. The value of that labour-power is
chiefly determined, not, as is commonly sup-
posed, by supply or demand, but by the quan-
tity and quality of the necessaries of life used
by the average producer. Here it is that we
discover the source of strikes in the difference
of opinion existing between employer and em-
ploye as to the amount of value created by the
latter which he may retain for the mainten-
ance of his comfort and his physical, mental,
and moral well-being.
# # *
What sum is sufficient to support the
“manual-labourer”—the wharf-labourer, for
instance, in comfort ? At present he is paid
Is. per hour during a day of eight hours, and
at a slightly higher rate for overtime. It
must not, however, be imagined that his
average weekly earnings are therefore £2 Bs. or
over, for during the year his periods of en-
forced idleness are many, and it is calculated
by those who ought to know that in every
year the wharf-worker is only employed, on
an average, during nine months. Requiring to
be continually awaiting at the receipt of custom
opportunities of labour, he is thus precluded
from filling up these idle intervals by wage-
earning in other industrial avenues, and, there-
fore, it may be unhesitatingly asserted that—
even if by unremitting toil he is enabled to earn
£5 or £6 in some weeks—his total earnings for
the year do not exceed, even if they always attain
to, a sum of £2 ss. weekly. This sum, according to
the S.M. Herald’s hydrocephalous bantling,
the Echo, is amply sufficient for the mainten-
ance of “ those who have not and will not learn
of the better things of life,” though the writer
might be pleased to consider that if we all
“ had ” and “ learned of these better things ”
among which, we suppose, the writing of the
Echo’s leaderettes is numbered—that there
would be no one left to provide the necessaries
of life. No doubt the capitalist also looks at
the matter through similarly distorting green
goggles ; but as neither former nor latter con-
sider the matter logically and unbiasedly, we
prefer to examine it more closely before accept-
ing their dicta.
# # #
What is the cost of the articles abso-
lutely indispensable to the existence of
the ordinary wharf-worker and his family?
Doubtless even the Echo will admit that a
wharf-labourer is justified in entering into the
married state, if only for the purpose of raising
up aspirants for the vacancy on the wharves
that will eventually be created by his decease.
House-rent is, of course, the most important
item; now cottages, such as they are, may be
rented in the suburbs at Bs. or 10s. weekly, but
it is essential that the wharf-worker shall dwell
near the wharves, and there houses are not to
be obtained except at a higher rental. Even
setting down rent at 10s., this large deduction
from 40s. leaves in hand, say, 355., more or less, to
provide bread, meat, vegetables, milk, groceries,
firing and light, for, perhaps, several growing
and grown persons, to say nothing of clothing
for several backs. Presuming that it is
found equal to these demands, how much may he
expected to remain for schooling, the purchase
of literature (presumably the Echo’s guide to
“better things”), for the medical attendance
sometimes necessary, for rational amusement,
for the replacement of worn-out ‘ ‘ household
gods,” aye, and for the purchase of little odds
and ends of womanly finery, or grog and tobacco
for the wage-earner ? How much ? Well, not
much. But, presuming for the sake of further
enquiry that this miserable sum is equal to all
these demands, with such an income and so
many outlets of expenditure, can it be possible
to lay by anything for the inevitable rainy day,
the hour of sickness, casualty, or calamity, or
to make provision for the time that comes to
every man, who lives long enough, when he no
longer can work ? No, no, a thousand times no.
Would it were possible to condemn for a time
those who prate about the prosperity of the
worker to be ground through the mill of hard
work and home hardships that he is doomed to
endure while life lasts. The survivors of this
ordeal would have found good reason to alter
their opinions.
* * *
It has long been the aim of the employer in
the older countries to reduce tire earnings of
the legitimate bread-winner, and thus depre-
ciate the proletarian standard of living, by the
employment of women and children —in many
cases mere infants—during long hours at starva-
tion wages. This traffic in flesh and blood
having been largely curbed by the passing of the
Factory Acts, it is now his habit to play off the
large masses of the literally-starving, the
uttcrly-helpless and hopeless unemployed
against those whom monopoly is grinding out of
existence more gradually. In new countries
where the man, unjustly treated by an
employer, has some measure of access to
natural opportunities, these methods are to
some extent inapplicable, and monopoly is
forced to have recourse to the utilisation
as labour-forces of the native races, or
the importation of black and yellow skins,
whose notions of comfort are limited, and who
are content to occupy a much lower grade than
that demanded by men of his own colour.
Warned by experience, and the example of
America, where such importations have caused
grave social difficulties, Australian public
opinion has demanded that further introduction
of low-living peoples shall cease, and employers
are now driven to attempt to starve the discon-
tented strikers into submission by employing,
temporarily, inefficient labour (driven into the
cities by the locking-up and misuse of land) at
higher rates than those prevailing prior to the
strike. On the present occasion they may be
successful, nevertheless, at some subsequent
period they will be compelled to accede to the
demands of a labour-union whose ramifications
know ho boundaries.
* * *
The utilisation of any fresh mechanical inven-
tion, no matter how largely it increases produc-
tion, only temporarily, and then in individual
cases, increases surplus values ; for when such
a machine enters into general use—exchange-
value being determined by the amount of labour-
power embodied in an article—a given value is
simply spread over a greater number of products,
and the exchange-value of each article that is
or might be thus produced is greatly reduced.
Monopoly, then, failing to combat an improve-
ment in the workers’ standard of living, which
keeps pace with that increase in productiveness
caused by improvement of appliances, will year
by year be more closely hemmed in between
consumer and producer, until at length
they meet on a plane where the consumer
of one use-value must have already been the
producer of some other use-value ere its acquire-
ment became possible. The entire social economy
of the busy working hive will then contain no
standing-room for drones, and they will be com-
pelled to enlist in the great industrial army
either as producers, administrators, instructors,
or distributors. To-day, the capitalistic method
of production and accumulation almost anni-
hilates all self-earned private property. Then,
the industrial method of production and utilisa-
tion will annihilate that expropriation of use-
values which has given birth to poverty and all
the crimes and miseries that follow in its track,
and has filled earth’s highways with those who
fain would, and those who need not, work. The
onward inarch of mankind towards the Co-
operative Commonwealth which is to be is slow,
but undeniably sure.

A Parson on the Fence.
The “ strike” address delivered at Sydney on
the strike by Rev. Dr. Sevan, of Melbourne,
who preaches the gospel for £30 a week, may
be simply and contemptuously described as a
Jefferisian utterance. In the first place, the
lecturer’s assumption that “the public” form a
third party to the conflict, is an utter fallacy,
for no matter how ignorant or indifferent to the
true interests of their own class a large number of
people may be, the fact remains that every man
in the community is either a capitalist or a
wage-earner. Some people, including a large
percentage of the clergy, are both, and their
leanings are almost universally towards capital.
As to Dr. Sevan’s cheerful story that the masses
“ have all the power in their hands, every man
having a vote,” we need but inform him that,
there being in New South Wales no system of
vote-registration, thousands of men are totally
deprived of the franchise, inasmuch as necessity
forces them to be absent at election-time fi om
the district to which they electorally belong.
Further, even if every man has in theory one
vote, there are hundreds and hundreds of capi-
talists who have, by reason of their landed
property, three or four, or ten votes each, and
regularly poll them, thus effectually antagonising
the votes of as many franchised workmen. Thus,
for instance, Mayor Burdekin has a vote in every
Sydney constituency, and we know that Mr.
George Thornton, of the City Bank, has at
least twelve parliamentary votes in and around
Sydney; also that Mr. F. A. Wright, M.P.,
has 25 votes in New South Wales. Every dude
whose name is on the roll as a member of a club
which owns its own premises, has a vote, in
addition to the one he polls for the district in
which he lives, and to those he enjoys by
virtue of his property ; but where is the
manual - labour elector who possesses more
than one vote? Why should clergymen
travel 600 miles to sit on a fence and talk
slobber? As to Dr. Sevan’s text, “ Take heed
and beware of covetousness, for a man’s life
consisteth not in the abundance of the things
that he possesseth,” it is all nonsense. A man’s
life does consist in abundance, as the personal
appearance of the clergy in general elocpiently
testifies ; also, this extract from Scripture is a
sentence which, as the masses are beginning to
understand, has been used for a thousand years
to establish the privileged classes in secure
possession of the result of generation after
generation of shameless robbery, and to make
the poor “content with the lot to which,” so it
is blasphemously asserted, “ it has pleased God
to call them.”
A Cheeky Minority,
The Sabbatarian party in Victoria lias resolved
to demand a plebiscite to decide whether public
libraries and art galleries are to lie accessible on
the Day of Rest. This is an ingenious device
and worthy of the guileless Children of Light
who invented it. At the polls the Sabbatarians
would have all the advantage. Their opponents
have no organisation to work with, while every
parson and Sunday-school teacher would go out
to bang the gong of war, and the Y.M.C.A.
would rise in all its might and dignity, and the
teetotal fanatic, and the Social Purity howler,
and the fellow who screeches discordant hymns
on the wharves, and the offensive bigot who
sends tracts full of blazing hell to the patients
in the hospital, and the Salvation Army choir,
would all go on the warpath together, and even
the Chinaman would be routed out of the House
of Joss and the stray Mahommedan would be
wakened and shaken up, and if by all these
devices a majority of only one was obtained the
question would be announced as set at rest for
ever. The Sabbatarian party has for years de-
nounced the idea of taking a vote on such a
subject as a vile pandering to Baal and other
unclean gods, but now that a popular vote
seems to be their only hope this old objection
lias been sunk out of sight. Apparently, how-
ever, they forget that the question is
not one to be settled in such a fashion.
A mere majority has no more right to
close a public institution on Sunday than
it has to close a church on the same day.
If a library is to be shut because a trifle more
than half the population has no use for it, then
every religious denomination in Australia should
be closed up at once, because they are all
minorities —every one. And even if the atten-
dants at all the churches are counted in as
belonging to one creed, still they represent less
than a quarter of the population, and, conse-
quently, on the doctrine of majorities, they
want to be wiped out bodily and for ever.
Moreover, the theory of the plebiscite is defec-
tive on another point. If the Sabbatarians win
—no matter how or by what devices —they will
denounce all further agitation on the subject as
infamous treachery; if they lose, they will go
right on and fight the battle to the last gasp
just the same. They always do. The Sunday
bigot with numbers at his back is offensive and
insufferable ; the same bigot when deserted and
alone compares himself to the one righteous
man who was found in Sodom, or to the solitary
prophet who went forth and whooped in Nine-
veh, The habit is fixed and incurable. It is
no earthly use to toss up with a Church that
speculates with a double-headed coin, and is
ready to prove that it has won whichever side
comes uppermost.
“ For Myself and a Woman, for Ever and
Ever, Amen.”
William Ellery, the Sydney letteivsorter,
sentenced last week to 18 months’ hard labour
for having stolen letters containing cheques ami
postage-stamps, represented in extenuation of
his crime that his wife had been long sick and
that as his poor salary had been drawn on in
advance and he could raise no more money, he
turned thief to get funds to send her for change
of air—the only expedient which promised to
keep' her alive. The Law did not admit that
Ellery’s plea extenuated his offence in any
way, and herein the Law was more right
than it generally is. If Ellery had reasoned
he might have realised that letters are of all
forms of property the most sacred. He who
cribs a letter from a mail-bag runs the risk of
stealing, in addition to postage-stamps, tin-
peace and hope not alone of a life, but of lives.
Under the caption of “ Letters that never
Came ” histories of tears might be written and
tales of death be told; and the man who,
while gold chains emblazon the white
waistcoats of usurers and other vampires
steals a letter entrusted to him, is ignorant
of the first principles of morality. But now
comes another aspect of the question. What
Ellery did, ignorantly or otherwise, he did to
keep his wife alive. It was a case of Ellery
and his wdfe against the world, against the
universe. For the sake of Society, for sake ol
Society’s opinions and its laws, should a man
see his wife die ? Assume that the case comes
to this—that on one side a man sees social order
maintained and his wife dying by its mainten-
ance, and, on the other, social order disrupted
and his wife restored to health, what is the
healthy, the brave man’s first impulse ? Does
he not act, if forced into a crime, even as Nora
Helmer acted to save her husband’s life ?Is
it not that he and his wife will fight the world
together, will fight the universe together, and
that law, neither human nor alleged to be
divine, shall be allowed to break the bond?
The lion fights for the lioness, the tiger
for the tigress ; even the sparrow fights for his
wife and her little nest, not against one or two
or a dozen, but against everything and every-
body whose influence tends to disruption. The
man who is not prepared to do that—even
against Society—is only worthy of a society
woman. Rudyard Kipling, the possessor of a
very fresh and clear mind, in a set of verses
recently published, puts it: —■
But till we are built like angels, with hammer and chisel
and pen
We will work for ourself and a woman, forever and ever,
amen.
That idea is the first one of a true social system.
The farther Society departs from it, the less
Society merits regard, the less the laws are res-
pected. Society should not be accepted as any
man’s tyrant. Kipling ’s notion was to show that
the individual man should, or would, break away
from compacts with his brother men for the sake
of his wife, but, as he treated the subject, be
ignored the difference between compacts better
able to help a mail to keep his wife than
existing ones. Marry a woman, keep a woman,
die for a woman—that is Humanity’s shibbo-
leth ; and whatever Law says, whatever Society
says, whatever Religion says, all these are
secondary, if they allow the man and the woman
to choose between death and social dishonour.
The Age of Flabbiness.
Vanity Fair looks with a dark and bilious
eye on the weakness of the Tory Government
in England, inasmuch as that broken-backed
aggregation has neglected to ‘ ‘ put a head ” on
everybody who doesn’t seem quite contented :
The prevailing weakness of the present age is flabbiness.
Flabbiness of thought. Flabbiness of policy. Flabbiness
of action. It is a bad quality and encourages mischief.
Wo said much the same thing when the dockers’ strike
was in the air last year. We say it again now when the
country’s servants are affecting to strike. And if any
honest man who cares for the honour and well-being of
his country will compare in their results all
of any considerable proportions, he will what
we say is right.
We rise to second these observations. *No
Roman centurion ever attempted to go on
strike, so far as history relates, so long as the
empire could raise nails enough to crucify him
with. Neither did the primitive Christian ever
demand higher wages while the tiger iu the
Coliseum was kept fresh and in good working-
order. There was no fooling after an eight-
hours’ movement among the Protestants in the
cells of the Inquisition, cither—at least, if there
was, we never heard of it, and they didn’t form


a Trades Union, and demand that they shouldn’t
be burnt after two o’clock on Saturday, or object
to put in overtime at the stake. But, then,
there was no flabbiness about Governments in
these days. They had a certain unobtrusive
vigour about them instead, which has since died
out, and when a man wasn’t satisfied with his
pay they concluded that he was trying to under-
mine the Throne, and they hung him up by the
oar till he felt that his income was really larger
than he wanted. It is rough on the Tory editor
when lie looks back on these good old days, and
then thinks sadly about the flabbiness of the
present age, and the way people rise up, con-
trary to the dictates of Providence, and object
to live all their lives on dry bread, and then die
at last in the workhouse.
All Toy.
Ah Toy, the common heathen from the Hoang-
ho, who has successfully demonstrated in all
the Victorian courts that no Chinaman who is
a British subject can be .poll-taxed under
British law, is about to demonstrate the same
fact over again before the Privy Council in
November next; and considering all the proba-
bilities in his favour it is time for Australia to
consider what is to be done after that. What
Ah Toy has proved so far is that, owing to the
patched and murky imbecility of the Constitu-
tion of these “ colonies,” no law to exclude the
yellow leper will ever hold water, and until
the Constitution is mended the heathen may
come in thousands and millions at his pleasure.
Also the Constitution can’t be mended until the
British Government lends a hand, and the
British Government can’t lend a hand until the
British Constitution is altered and re-fixed to
meet the case, and how to re-fix it no one
exactly knows. Therefore, if Ah Toy comes out
uppermost, every Chinaman who ever paid a
poll-tax in this country will have an action for
damages, and among them they hold out a fair
prospect of national insolvency; and every
Chinaman in the Flowery Land may come in
without any tax or other obstruction. Or else
the British Constitution must go, on the ground
that it is too slow and unwieldy to be of any
use at such a crisis, and Australia must inti-
mate that even though tbp Privy Council should
break into eternal smash and the silken ‘ ‘ bonds ”
should be consigned to the dust-heap, the
Mongol has got to stay out. If the British legal
system has tied itself up into such a hard knot
that it can find no adequate remedy for the
difficulty—and it is one of the peculiarities of
the British code that it seldom can find a
remedy except after a hundred years of hard
consideration—then it is too old and exhausted
and rheumatic to be of any use in Australia.
When Ah Toy’s case is settled, the probabilities
are that some legal remedy for the Chinese
agony will be wanted in a great hurry, and
there will be no time to wait while the Imperial
Government sits down to contemplate the situa-
tion. It would be an ignominious fate for the
Empire to be wrecked on one common one-horse
pagan from Asia, but should that saffron-hued
alien win his case it will be necessary to put
some unexpected velocity into practice, or else a
political breakage may be looked for at an early
day. The British Constitution has been bragged
about, and advertised, and wept over at
banquets and loyal gatherings, and sung and
gxoaned and snuffled about for ages, but if it
isn’t large enough to keep out one Chinaman then
all the poetry that has been spent upon it was
wasted energy which might have been better
employed in carrying bricks.
Sydney Daily Telegraph, the organ com-
mercially figure-headed by Mr. J. R. Carey
—also president of the syndicate which
lias organised the acquirement by a private
company of the public tramways of Syd-
ney—prints an eloquent apology for black-
legging, and plaintively speaks of the manual-
labour and other traitors to the workman’s
cause as “ Men who have done no wrong in the
eyes of the law.” True, O Carey & Cos. ! ad-
mirable are thy reservations. The present laws
of Australia, of the world, simply constitute
a gigantic conspiracy against Labour. Mr.
Carey aforesaid is a director of the Port
Jackson Steamship Company. The ordinary
deck-hands of that corporation’s passenger-
vessels, we are told, work all day on every day
of every week, and each man’s average week’s
work means—ye gods !— one hundred hours.
One hundred hours at 6d. per hour, the London
docker’s wage, would be 505., but the Port
Jackson Co.’s deck-hands (who, in addition to
their other duties, embark and discharge cargo)
don’t, so far as we can hear, get 50s. for
100 hours’ work. They only get £9 a month,
or say £2 Is. 6d. a week. They are allowed no
holidays—when a man takes a day off he has to
pay a substitute, which usually costs him 4s.
over his own pay, as no casually-employed sea-
man can be got to accept less than 10s. for, say,
14 hours’ work (some days’ work means 18
hours—from 6 a.m. to 12 p.m.) Now, the
question is this : Will Mr. Carey, when
the syndicate over which he presides gets
the control of the public trams, do his
best to arrange that they are not worked
on the £9-a-month-100-hour-a-week principle?
There can be little joy in the life of a steamboat-
hand who gets £2 Is. 6d. a week, has to snatch
his meals as best he can, and has to stay away
from his poor home two or three nights a week.
People were afraid the other day that the
steamboat-hands would strike. If these are
really the conditions under which they work —
and so far as we can ascertain the men’s case is
not overstated—who is there that would not
sympathise with them? The Bulletin looks
forward with pleasurable anticipation to an
announcement that Mr. Carey has, as the
initial step of a big reform, induced his fellow-
directors of the P.J.S.S. Cos. to grant the com-
pany's deck-hands one holiday out of every seven
days, thus letting their weekly “graft” stand
at the respectable total of 84 hours or there-
abouts, which will be the ordinary trades-
unionist’s weekly 48 hours upside-down.
It is interesting to learn, apropos of the “spon-
taneous ” offer made by seven or eight hundred
Sydney warehousemcn’s-clerks to work on the
wharves in order to defeat the union manual-
labour, that in one warehouse where a dork of
independent mind was asked to “spon-
taneously ” sign the paper, he declined ; where-
upon he was given to understand that he might
leave the office. Therefore, he ultimately said
“I have a wife and family, sol may as well
sign,” which he did. What is the value, as a
declaration of opinion, of a document signed by
miserables who figuratively live in a copying-
press while the fat boss stands by with both
hands on the screw ? Competition is Jo keen for
employment in soft-goods houses (many of the
biggest of which are run by a few men and four
times their number of small boys) that three-
fourths of the warehousemen’s assistants are
morally in the condition of Brough Smyth’s
now historical clerks, who, when their stern
taskmaster rang his bell, jumped from their
seats in terror and thought of God’s dread
summons on the Day of General Judgment.
*
A Sydney morning paper having in a leader
recommended the “upholding of the law at
all hazards,” that is, without consideration as
to whether it be just or unjust, the little organ
of the strikers thereupon remarked :
Bad laws were made to be broken, and the community
which respects unequal inequitable laws is not fit to
enjoy—never can enjoy—the privileges of free men.
Whereupon the organ of capitalism enquires
solemnly;—
VVhat is this but the wild insensate gibbering of anarchy,
which demands that every man shall be a law unto him-
self? Bad laws were not made to be broken by anyone
who chooses to consider them had, and a people At to
enjoy the privileges of free men will deal with them in an
altogether different fashion. Good or bad, the laws of
this country are the free expression of the will of a free
people, and as such they must be respected until the same
power which enacted them repeals them.
The labour sheet has since foolishly withdrawn
the word “ broken,” and asserted that it should
have said “'bad laws were made to be revoked.”
That is sheer balderdash. Bad laws, our bad
laws, are not ‘ ‘ the free expression of the will of
a free people ” by several billies-full; they are
in many cases but an expression of the tyranni-
cal will of certain long-decayed debauchees and
autocrats, whose names live now only as themes
for execration, or were made in the interest of
the enfranchised few for the oppression of the
many. Such laws will never be revoked until
they are first systematically broken and trampled
under foot. It is not enough that they he allowed
to lapse into desuetude, for so long as they remain
on the statute-book any fanatic may imperil the
liberty of any reformer for the purposes of sec-
tarian or class-oppression. For instance, what
has the “ free expression of the free will of the
free people” of this country got to do with its
present Sunday laws ? Would it be possible
nowadays to pass such repressive enactments
through any of our legislatures ? We repeat:
Bad laws were made to be broken in order that
they may be revoked.

Social economics are divisable under three
heads : —Wealth, Capital, and Labour. The
first represents the staples of life, such as wool,
grain, metals, timber, live-stock, land, &c. The
second comprises artificially-developed organisa-
tions of wealth which, scientifically used, in-
crease the wealth, comfort, or conveniences of
humanity at a saving of proportionately in-
creased labour, such as tools, plant, railways,
ships, or instruments. The third defines the
means by which all wealth or capital is made
valuable. To be possessed of capital without a
reserve of wealth or labour in the case of a
strike means ruin to the capitalist when all bis
operations are suspended. A wealthy man can
live on his reserves ; a labourer can produce for
himself; but the capitalist lies between the
Devil and the deep sea, as he has no assets
enabling him to maintain life, neither has he
the knowledge or power requisite to obtain
them himself. His wealth consists of capital
which ceases to be wealth the moment that its
productive properties are stopped. More and
more risky are becoming the chances of the
individual non-co-operative capitalist ventures,
and before long private investment and specu-
lation must come to a standstill. It will
then be time for the community to take
over the business of the capitalist and
work the same on collective principles. The
railways are already treated on this plan, and
the shipping and other transport agencies for
passenger and goods distribution must follow
suit. Then Wealth and Labour—the present
intermediary having been removed—will con-
front each other for the final struggle. It will
not take long in deciding, however. The
wealthy once left to live on their amassed means
will speedily consume them and find themselves
on the verge of positive starvation. The labour-
ing class in the meantime, having the control of
capital and an organised system of production,
will have earned more wealth than the wealthy
owned at the time of their entering upon the
contest for supremacy. The moral is obvious.
Somewhere or other among the laws of New
South Wales is, we believe, an enactment
against * ‘ truck, ” specially providing that no man
shall he paid any part of his wages in liquor or
in a public-house ; and yet, we are told that the
truck system has long been in force at a certain
Sydney hotel run in the interests of a large em-
ployer of labour, who, it is claimed, has paid
his hands with cheques which they found great
difficulty in changing, except at this special
public-house—the natural result being that the
workmen usually went home to their families
with more grog than money. In this alleged
fact may be found a key to the solution of “a
disgraceful episode ” which recently caused
much tribulation among the champions of the
divide et impera trick/
What is the matter with the Sydney daily
press that it practically failed to report the
proceedings of the meeting of provincial news-
paper proprietors the other day ? Can it be
that the Sydney newspapers have a special in-
terest in seeing the country press of the mother
colony continue in its present abject position ?
However that may be one thing is certain—that
unless the journals of the provinces form a
strong combination in defence of their own in-
terests, which are, in many cases, quite opposed
to those of the metropolitan newspapers, they
will continue to have advertisements offered
them at a halfpenny an inch, they will be de-
barred from a fairly - apportioned share of
Government advertisements, and will remain as
poor as church-mice. All country pressmen
should read the Inland Printer (Chicago),
which shows the position occupied by the
American provincial press as set fortli at its
periodical reunions, the solidity of its organisa-
tion, and the independence (resulting purely
from combination) of the men who run inland
American newspapers. The present isolated
position of the New South Wales country-
pressman too often means misery and drudgery
and hopeless dependence to the end of the
chapter.

A will-case with 12 barristers and 12 solicitors
all hard at work eating up the estate on behalf
of 34 defendants, is in progress in Victoria. In
any community which had brains enough to
know a horse from an egg it might have been
settled by sending the disputed document to the
judge in a large envelope, and asking him to
read it and state his opinion, but Australia is
not built that way. Yet if commerce and law
were run on the same plan it would be necessary
to send 12 men and a four-horse dray to carry
home a pound of meat, and every man would
have to receive his instructions in writing and
spend two days in reading up the case and con-
sidering the question of food in all its bearings
before he started, and then it would be neces-
sary to appeal to a judge to decide whether the
meat hadn’t got so bad in the interval as not to
be worth delivering.

It is cabled that the London Daily News, a
journal which, while professing to be “Liberal,”
is an organ of the fat bourgeoisie and the idle
official classes, and therefore a more formidable
and insidious foe to Labor than, the hopelessly
stupid Tory section of the British press, in
effect tells Australian workmen to be careful
lest their places be taken by imported coolies
and cheap white European labor. With the
latter we shall deal by adopting the United
States law against labour-agreements made
beyond the seas ; but were any serious attempt
made to import blacks wholesale, the manhood
of this country would, if necessary, take up
arms. But the contingency last mentioned is
too remote to be seriously discussed. The only
effect of such a cable message is to expose the
woeful ignorance of the British press concerning
the temper and power of the Australian worker.

Another long-felt want has been filled by the
publication of the Agricultural Gazette oe
New South Wales, the highly-advertised
organ of the new N.S.W. Department of Agri-
culture. This work is a perfect treasure of
useless knowledge, and the plain, unvarnished
tales which it unfolds should secure for it what
John Haynes calls “a nick” in the “hood-
war ” of every cockatoo-farmer. Farmer
Chumpkins, for instance, will be delighted
with the chapters on “ Tylenchus Arenarins,”
while all doubts which have worried the
troubled brain of Farmer Gubbins will be set
at rest when he reads that the common, or
garden, variety of the potato-bug is readily
recognisable by the fact that these industrious
pests possess “ transverse strise, hardly hetero-
cephalus, head concave-truncate, ellipsoidal
median bulb or median sesophageal bulb ellip-
soidal ; cardiac column broad, porus excretorius,
bursa small, tail conoid, vulva prominent or
otherwise,” Portions of the articles on “ Mac-
rophallus ” and “ Askanasji ” will be regarded
as obscene language by the Unscientific selector.
Since 1870 New Zealand lias had no less than
30 millions in loans, which have been expended
on public works, many of them unproductive
and some useless. Wildcat railways were built,
harbours constructed which there was no ship-
ping to fill, “ boom ” towns laid out only that
people might gamble in corner lots. In 1874
the imports of the country amounted to
£8,373,111, against exports reaching only
£5,433,000. This condition of things has, how-
ever, been gradually changing for the better ;
for last year the imports did not exceed
£5,998,224, and for the present they will not
exceed £6,270,000. The change, further, is
still more striking when we remember that in
1873 the population of the islands did not ex-
ceed 295,966 inhabitants, while now it is more
than double that number. The exports, again,
of New Zealand are a still more significant
evidence of her growing prosperity. Last year
they were some three and a-half millions in
excess of her imports. Her last Budget shows
a surplus of £115,974, which ought to go a long
way in relieving taxation. A country which,
with a population of only 600,000, has exported,
as New Zealand has done, to the amount of
£10,000,000 in one year, may fairly he considered
as having sown her wild oats and started on the
high-road to prosperity.

Like most rechauffe's “ Boccaccio,” as now
played at Sydney Royal, lacks its pristine
piquancy. Contrary to prior anticipations
Marian Burton played the name-part on the first
two nights, when her rendering was embued with
more dash than could have been expected from a
lady of her proportions and temperament
Though Von Suppe’s bright little trifle has been
produced on three or four prior occasions in
Sydney when Kate Chard, Emelie Melville, and
Annette Ivanova donned the garb of the giddy
Florentine student, this is the first time he has
walked the Royal’s boards. For the time the
company has been much strengthened by the en-
gagement of _ the Misses Ivanova, Royal and
Salinger, Knight Aston and Edward lire; yet
though Tilly’s “ Tis the Prince ” was, per-
haps, the vocal gem of the first night, on
the whole the performance does not compare
to advantage with that of the Dunning company.
On opening night, in two of the principal duets
the singers failed to chord throughout and seemed
to be carrying on a triangular duel, in which the
conductor represented the apex. The comedv
business of Johnny Gourlay’s Lotteringhi is excel-
but Signor Snazelle’s Lambertuccio is spas-
modic and blossoms chiefly in the moment of gag.
The opera is, of course, well staged, and Gordon’s
opening scene is very beautiful, the second is
merely passable, and the third —a night-scene in
the Palace gardens, over which the dailies have
gushed—is to us a weird and incomprehensible
mystery.

William Rignold received a magnificent
reception on his return to the Sydney stage in
“ Now-a-Days,” at Her Majesty’s. The play is
in the hands of the original cast, but scarcely
seems to go with the dash and completeness of
the first production. As previously intimated,
“ Macbeth ” will be produced on a scale of great
gorgeousness on Saturday evening at this house.

There is a slight decline in the attendance
at the School of Arts, but the change of billon
Saturday will -doubtless compel Manager Leete
again to hang out “ house full ” on the outer walls,
Pianist Rhodes’ artistic handling of the ivories
deserves recognition, and Juggler Provo contrives
somehow or other to trot out new tricks weekly.
* -:if *
After a long delay, caused by the refusal
of the instrumentalists to accept from 10s. to 20s.
as remuneration for attendance at each concert
and the two rehearsals which necessarily precede
it, the first of a series of ten orchestral concerts,
under the leadership of Signor Hazon, took place
in Sydney Centennial Hall on Saturday last, in
presence of an audience that looked scant in
that big building. The first violinist’s seat,
vacated by Mr. Allpress, is now occupied by
George Hall, the conductor of the bright little
Criterion Orchestra. The acoustic properties
of the Centennial Hall are far from good,
for reverberations always mar any note that closely
follows another, nevertheless the concert was
highly successful. Wagner’s “ Entr’ Acte to
Lohengrin,” a shimmer of weird notes culminat-
ing in a magnificent crash of melody, was enthu-
siastically encored, and had to be repeated, and a
similar compliment was paid to Handel’s minuetto,
“Berenice,” a grave, yet sweetly tender melody
arranged for modern orchestration by Mr. W. T.
Best. Pass-out checks being unobtainable at half-
time, it was impossible for ushers to distinguish
re-entering auditors from those attempting to get
in “on the nod.”
** * *
On the last night of “Paul Jones.” at
Sydney Royal, towards the close of the second
act, just when the “mariners bold ” expected to
receive another gigantic wreath, they were hit
hard by a large consignment of greengrocery
directed to their care by the occupants of the
gallery. The late premier baritone of the Carl
Rosa Opera Company quickly fled the stage in a
great state of indignation, which has since been
mollified by the statement that in Australia the
heaving of carrots and cauliflower is deemed a
compliment. The Insect followed more slowly,
and, eyeing a very fine cabbage, was understood
to mutter that the strike had made vegetables
scarce at his boarding-house.

The illness of Marian Burton, on the third

together, but the lady, hearing of his success, was
on hand promptly during the rest of the season.
The change confirmed us in our opinion that love-
making between two women is an unsatisfactory
business; Paul’s scenes with Yvonne went twice
as well with a man in the namepart.

George Darrell is still allowed to roam at
large round the stage of Melbourne Royal, and on
Saturday that unnatural phenomenon commenced
a second series of gyrations in his own dramatic
perversion of “Potter of Texas.” The ghastly
absurdity of George’s latest concoction beggars
description. There is, perhaps, no other man in
Australia who could dream such a drama, not if
he devoured a sack of cold potatoes with a
hundred-weight of underdone pork and then
dropped asleep on a tombstone. The authorities
will be wanting in a proper sense of duty unless
they order the Mounted Rifles to fire off their 40
rounds of ball-cartridge at one pop, and blow the
inane riot into everlasting space. The diphtheria
outbreak, and the typhoid epidemic at Toorak,
are blessings in disguise compared with “Potter
of Texas.” It is beyond a joke. The fact of live
men and women drawing salaries from such a
show when they might be stokers at another kind
of gasworks is the strangest feature of the present
struggle between labour and capital. At this
stage of the notice a burst of demoniacal
laughter conies in. “Potter of Texas,' like
that previous atrocity, “The Lucky Lot,”
consists almost entirely of George and Mrs.
Darrell, who absorb the limelight and large
capitals, and generally behave as though all sense
of the ridiculous was lost to the family. George
is an ever-present calamity under the name of
Charles Errol (the Australian), while Mrs. D. is
generally there in the capacity of Lady Annerley,
a tragic female possessed of an iron will and a
drivelling secret wrapped-up in a paper. In the
first act she gives George this miserable penny
surprise-packet and tells him to keep it in his
pocket until they are both buried or something, after
which he falls under the influence of an Alexandria
cigar, and the lady has to sit on his carcase and
thump him back to consciousness, whereupon he
kills 320 Arabs by shooting them in the big toe,
and the British troops roll-up just in time to bury
the heaps of slain. For hard shooting this act
takes the cake; and when—as happened on Satur-
day—George’s revolver occasionally gets tired
of the massacre and refuses to go off,
the Arabs fall down dead just the same.
Later on it transpires that George has
forgotten all about his heroism and the prize
packet, and the rest of the drama is devoted to a
paper chase of no interest whatever. At the finish
nothing appears to be settled for certain, not even
George’s marriage, and if anything were settled it
ought to be accomplished by the aid of a brick.
As an actor of heroes George grows more hopeless
every week. His calmness in the midst of perils
is irritating to the last degree, and his self-conscious
walk reminds one of the old gentleman who “wants
to sit down. ” His chief characteristics are a strange
ability to walk in a sitting posture, and an extra-
ordinary capacity for wearing top-bots and no
waistcoat. Mrs. Christine Darrell outrages possi-
bility with much force and some indications of
dramatic talent misapplied, and the rest make
minor objects of themselves, Edith Blande, in
the part of Ida Potter, being the best of an
afflicted gathering. The Trades Hall Council are
earnestly requested to call “Potter of Texas” out
on strike.
«$ * &
“ Alone in London,” which filled the
Alexandra on Saturday, is always certain of a
respectful hearing when its lucidly - constructed
story is well told. The Bland Holt company,
being rather stronger than usual this journey, .are
carrying matters through with impressive effect,
and the great scene in the old Sluice-House —one
of the most legitimate sensations ever created in
melodrama—grows in awesomeness. Mrs. Bland
Holt’s performance of Nan has acquired strength
these last two years, Maggie Knight is above the
average Gipsy Tom, and Bland Holt’s picture of
Jenkinson developes fresh bits of humour as time
goes on. The others seem to be first-class, or good
enough.
-*• -M -#-
Although the first night’s promise of
crowded houses for “ Hero and Leander ” has not
been realised, yet business at Prin-
cess’s is sufficiently creditable to public intelli-
gence. A lack of length, rather than strength, is
the only drawback to the complete financial suc-
cess of as pretty a stage poem as ever exerted a
refining influence upon a degenerate age of stove-
pipe hats and dull respectability. Words are
wanted. The present meagre allowance of blank
verse improves on acquaintance, and the scenery
and situations would carry far more talk than the
nameless author had the temerity to write down.
He has neglected a splendid opportunity in not mak-
ing the virtuous High Priest come a-wooing Mrs.
Potter when Bellewis swimming, after the manner
of Brian do Bois-Gilbert with Rebecca. A stormy
scene between Mrs. P. and the inflamed ecclesi-
astic would add interest to the play, and fill in
that allowance of gas without which modern audi-
ences think they are being sent empty away. At
present, “Hero and Leander ” may be compared
to a graceful, slender form that appears weak to
people who judge female proportions by dress-
makers’ padding. In truth, it is a delightful pro-
duction, and, but for the now general disbelief in
Mrs. Potter, would have become the rage of Mel-
bourne.

*
There is a painful sameness about modern
melodrama, and the dire necessity of doing some-
thing to lighten-up the gloom and impart variety
to the proceedings leads us to offer Mr. Bland
Holt a friendly suggestion. We have no desire to
hinder the tragic end of the heavy villain, for vye
recognise the necessity of killing that person off in
the last act, somehow or other, and as he has
already died by every recognised means, except
spontaneous combustion and being eaten by wild
beasts, we don’t wish to interfere with the disease
that carries him off. But we have noticed that
during his career it has become a fixed habit of
that gentleman to embezzle • a gigantic sum
in gold, and hide it somewhere, and it is
also imperative that it should be missing
when he comes to dig it up again, and then
it, is required that he should tear his hair, and
go on in a dreadful manner. Also, now and then
there appears to be a difficulty in making the
plunder vanish in a feasible way. Therefore we
would suggest that a good effect might be arrived
at if the depraved monster could be induced to
bury the coin in a district that is subject to earth,
quakes, and then, just as heis coming to look fo r
it, a volcano might open on the spot and blow the
whole pile at the moon. After which the scene
might close with the prize ruffian groping round in
the dust, and finding only 3s. 6d. left as the re-
ward of a long life of crime and bloodshed, and he
might be heard asking himself if it was worth his
while to swim in gore during 45 years for a half-
crown and two sixpences, and a bushel of dirt and
grit and bricks.

Sass was taking his morning shave in a
Melbourne barbery, when a customer enquired of
the operating professor—“ What’s this Hero and
Leader they are going to play at the Princess’s ?”
Whereupon another man explained that Hero was
“a cove as used to swim across the Hellpond to
see his tart.” This is a fact, or nearly so.
