W. 0. Wills is dramatising “Esmond” for
the Kendals. Mrs. K. will impersonate the
matronly heroine who forms such a sweet, spiri-
tual attachment for an innocent many years her
junior. The Prince of Wales, they say, has now
quite forgiven Kendal for breaking out into a
giddy comic song at the Marlborough House
Sunday dinner-party, some years ago. Kendal’s
crime consisted not so much in the giddiness of
the song as in the singing of it without being
called upon by his Riled Highness.
-*- -:K- «-
At the Royal General Theatrical Fund
banquet the other week Mr. Leopold Rothschild
had the cool stupidity to say that he didn’t appeal
to the actors for subscriptions, but “to the public
they did so much to amuse.” This contemptible
cant is peculiar to the theatrical profession, and
its wealthy hangers-on, who seem to insinuate
that a theatre should be run on the financial lines
of a church, where, after paying for your seat,
you are expected to give a trifle extra for the
parson’s private purse. The Bulletin does, or
tries to do, something to amuse the public, there-
fore every subscriber is requested to send along an
annual donation for the staff. No thanks.
** * *
A London critic, after praising Melba’s
performance of Elsa, in “Lohengrin,” says “on
the whole, however, if I were called upon to
choose between her and Miss Macintyre I think I
should prefer the Scotch lassie.” Perhaps he
happens to he Scotch himself, which would fully
explain matters.

The false-imprisonment action of Price -v.
Santley was settled by the defendant paying £250
and his own costs. So that £SOO, about the sum
raised by the two big concerts got up for the
famous English baritone in Sydney, went into the
pockets of Melbourne lawyers. This is very
wrong. Price ought to have brought his action in
Sydney, when some of the Devil’s brigade would
have got their money back.

On the first night of “ Hero and Leander, ”
at Melbourne Princess’, the disclosure of Heil-
nings’ gorgeous scenic temple of Aphrodite
evoked a loud and long-continued call for the
artist. That modest individual stood quietly in
the wings, however, and was easily -dissuaded
from facing the footlights by the lithe Leander
who whispered hurriedly: “My dear f’lah;
doncherkhow, present costume (looking at a well-
worn slightly-stained suit of dittoes) quite in-
congruous; in fact, anachronism would disturb
dramatic unities, s’xnother time,” and so forth.
John didn’t go on, but at the close of the
last act he saw something that made his
old eyes wink. Leander, drowned in swim-
ming the Hellespont, had been thrown up
by the cruel billows at Hero’s feet on the pebbly
beach of Sestos, attired apparently in naught but
a pocket-handkerchief or a lady’s veil. There were
loud calls for “ B’lew that worthy couldn’t very
well go on clothed only in a loin-cloth, so donning
a “ cold snap,” blue cloth, Inverness cape, ho took
the call. As he stood bowing before the curtain,
with his B.C. legs sticking-out beneath his
twentieth-century toga, he looked like a clown
on a cold day outside a booth at a fair. But, no
matter, the play was over and the unities were
past outrage. Hennings ought to be on hand
now every night during the rest of the run, attired
in mosquito-netting—in case he gets another big
call.

The liquidation of the Commercial Bank
of S.A. has cost over £30,000, of which £IO,OOO
went in lawyers’ costs.
The Shipping Gazette, a London class
paper, rails at Labor Unionism, which, it declares,
is driving commerce from London. In one con-
sentrated howl, it alleges that “ the dock laborers
are now not only better paid than formerly, but
they do considerably less work.” That’s partly
what unionism sprang into existence to achieve.
On Tuesday last Mr. W. K. Peirce was
presented with a gold chain and locket, suitably
inscribed, by the staff of Messrs, Gibbs, Bright
and Cos., of this city. Mr, Peirce, who has been
promoted to the firm’s Brisbane house, carries
away with him the good wishes of his employers
and brother-officers.
The procession of strikers, bound for the
Exhibition Building, marching at the quick-step,
took nearly half-an-hour to pass this office last
Saturday, and it was estimated that at least
10,000 men thus showed the strength of combina-
tion. Headed by the stalwart Delegate Mooney,
of the Shearers’ Union, on horseback, they looked
an intelligent, well-nurtured, cleanly body of men,
and, watching them closely, we only saw one of
their number the worse for liquor. By the
way, that they look fairly well-fed and art;
decently clad, seems to convince the ordinary
hide-bound worshipper of Mammon that they
have no just cause of complaint. “ Look at ’em,
he says, triumphantly, “What have they got to
strike about?” But moleskins and a skin-full are
not the chief end, nor yet the sole object, of the
man who wants to get as much happiness here
below as other people will let him have.
Moreover, the unionist who waited till he had tc
hobble on crutches and hide his nakedness with
an evening newspaper, ere demanding alterations,
would then discover as little willingness on the
part of the dominant classes to concede them, as
now. The sight was a cheering and ennobling
one, a proof that community of interest can recon-
cile differences of opinion, and that after all the
proper study of mankind is man. A pathetic note
was touched by the disorderly marching of a
number of boys, led by a poor canvas banner-
unionists in embryo—at the tail of the pro-
cession. They were, we imagine, the lads
employed in tapping off the rust with little ham-
mers from the inside of boilers, and their presence
is a convincing proof, if further proof be necessary,
that the ramifications of unionism are wide-spread-
ing and that the Socialism which is feared by tin
uninstructed of this generation will be familiar as
household words in the_ mouths of the next. A
still larger procession will march next Saturday,
when the Domain will be the People’s Forum.
Rev. Andrew Gardiner, whose wife re-
cently got a rule nisi in divorce against him, has
returned to Sydney, and says he wishes the utmost
publicity given to the proceedings of the eccle-
siastical court about to try his case. From this it
may be inferred that Mr. Gardiner claims that,
though the decree nisi of the legal court has been
made against him, there are still two sides to his
case. Who knows? As an instance of how
innocence may co-exist with the most suspici-
ous appearances, we may point out that in the
witness-box the other day Lady Dunlo (who,
lung before her marriage to Lord Dunlo, had had
an illegitimate child to one Weston) deposed that
immediately after her marriage—Lord Dunlo
having decamped to Australia —one Wertheimer
(with whom she had previously travelled alone to
Paris platonically) came and rode out with her
He never visited her after the theatre, and they never
had supper together. She told him not to come to the
house, as she was being watched. She had never
kissed Wertheimer in the presence of any person.
Wertheimer kissed her once when he went to America,
saying that he might not return. He had never kissed
her since her marriage. It was not true that he ever pul
his arm round her waist. Since her marriage he had
always treated her with respect, and expressed strong
affection for her, which she never returned. There was
never any familiarity whatever between them. Being
cross-examined, she said that she had never kissed
Wertheimer at all He had often asked her for a kiss, but
had never kissed her except on the occasion mentioned.
He was very kind to her. She saw him every day and had
lunched, dined, and supped with him. He used to
buy everything she required. Her parents were
unaware that she was with him in Paris or Trouville.
Wertheimer took a house solely for her, and made her a
present of it. Housed to keep his evening dress there,
and slept there about twice a week. She had nothing to
do with him, and lived by herself. She had four horses,
a well-furnished house, and a private carriage, and ser-
vants to wait on her, all at Wertheimer’s cost. She re-
ceived cheques of considerable sums from him to support
the house. Lord Dunlo often called at the house, and
used to bring her home from the theatre and concert-
hall, and stav late. Wertheimer gave evidence denying
that Lady Dunlo had ever been his mistress. From the
first time he knew her he had a great admiration for her,
and on many occasions asked her to marry him. He
suggested that she should go to Avenue-road
because Weston forced an entrance to her rooms
and attacked her. He gave her the house before
her marriage, and had never given her money since mar-
riage. He never went to the house without a third person
was present. The statement that he had been in her bed-
room was absolutely false. At the hotels on the Con-
tinent he gave his name as Wertheimer, andhers as Miss
Bilton. The furniture for the house cost £500; He also
gave her horses. He had kissed her twice, and had put
his arm around her, much to her annoyance. He admitted
that his conduct was a mistake ; but he wanted to marry
her.
And yet, despite all this, the jury returned a ver-
dict in favour of Lady Dunlo, the case against
whom, as here stated, appears a thousand per cent,
more ugly than that made out in court against
Parson Gardiner—who, we are told, means to
appeal.

The Governor of Barataria.
AN HISTORICAL SKETCH.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Tt is not, I hope, necessary, in these
enlightened times when the book-canvasser is
abroad, to give a geographical account of the
great colony of Barataria. If, however, there
should be any person in ignorance of its where-
abouts I have much pleasure in referring them to
the standard work on the subject by the accom-
plished historian, Don Miguel Cervantes di
Saavedra.
# * *
Never, since the golden age of Barataria,
when it was ruled by the illustrious Sancho Panza,
did Governor ascend the vice-regal throne of that
famous dependency with better intentions than
Don Adriano de Speranza—as the rest of his titles
would take up too much valuable advertising-
space, I regretfully suppress them. He came of a
noble family famous for knightly deeds and de-
sperate razzias by the misty light of the moon, ere
cattle-branding became degraded to the level of
an exact science, and when every man was his own
policeman and Justice of the Peace. He was
allied by marriage to the haughty old Firbolg
house of De Muffins, whose chivalrous motto was
“Pride before Potatoes.” Was ever high career
started under happier auspices? Alas for earthly
vanity ! Alas for Hope ! The very intensity of
his well-meaning became the cause of Don Adri-
ano’s downfall, and the perfervidness of his devo-
tion to duty a fatal pit in his proconsular path to
Slory. # # #
The Baratarians were a generous people in
their relations with their Governor. They gave
him a princely income to live upon, and an exten-
sive though somewhat lugubrious palace to live in,
and asked little beyond affability and condescension
in return. But they were particular in insisting
that ho should be a grandee. No ordinary hidalgo
would satisfy them. The Baratarians were not
different from other democrats in this respect.
Don Adriano was a grandee of a very fine type—
too tine some people said who believed in nobility
of the l.arge barbaric sort, with huge thews and
sinews and a tremendous raptorial beak. Cer-
t duly Don Adriano was small—an aristocratic
epigram instead of an epic. But, though small, he
was good. The stowage space that in patricians,
built on a more prodigal scale, is chiefly occupied
by a varied assortment of hereditary vices, was in
Don Adriano crammed with conscientiousness.
He recognised the fact that in the utilitarian age
in which his lot was cast it was not sufficient to
have come of a family whose members have done
no work for centuries—not sufficient, that is, for
one who aspired to Fame and a high place among
those who are called Great by their contem-
poraries. Therefore he determined to do all that
was asked of him, and more, and do it with all his
might. He became affable and condescending to
an extent that sometimes seriously alarmed his
medical advisers-, but he still kept on smiling like
the Spartan boy, for he saw his reward in the dis-
tance. To smile, and smile, and smile, and be an
honest man is a hard task, but Don Adriano suc-
ceeded tolerably in it. If he had any natural in-
born contempt for the Baratarians as a semi
barbarous folk who hung by their eyebrows to the
Cimmerian Edge of the great Empire lie repre-
sented, he was painfully careful i n concealing the
fact.
# # =*
Not so his Lady, the Donna Pomposa de
Speranza. She exulted in showing her scorn for
the poor Baratarians, and never lost an oppor-
tunity of slighting their wives. This became such
a mania with her at last that she was avoided by
all the native nobility, and had none to pay her
court but a few fawners and grovellers upon whose
prostrate forms she wiped her feet in intervals of
yawning. It was poor sport, but she had been
accustomed to it, and could not do without it.
Many people endeavoured to discover a reason for
her supernatural hauteur, but were unsuccessful in
their search. The Donna Pomposa was neither a
great Beauty nor a Queen of Fashion, and her
ancestral House, old and proud, could never boast
of having had kings of its name, as was the case
with many fine old Firbolg families, whose
descendants are at present reigning over small
public-houses, or carrying bricks up the giddy
ladder. It is said that it was in reference to an
ancestress of Donna Pomposa that the ancient
Firbolg sarcasm, “ Aisy, O woman of three cows!”
arose. However that may be, it soon became
evident that the indefatigable affability of Don
Adriano was almost neutralised by the arrogance
of his Lady. An eminent Baratarian colonist
from Hibernia said “it was a “neck-and-neck
race between them—only they were running in
opposite directions.”
# # #
Don Adriano —who pathetically admitted
that he “ was not a brilliant man”—slowly became
aware of this, and redoubled his efforts to gain
popularity. Although he came of a race who
“joked with difficulty,” he set himself seriously
to learn the construction of the freaks of language
called jokes, and took several specimens to pieces
in order to study their inner works. When he
thought he knew enough about them, he ventured
to make a few on his own account, in order to
amuse the Baratarians, but they received them in
such a cold and clammy manner that he became
discouraged, and relapsed into his natural condi-
tion of amiable melancholy. Shortly after his
arrival in his pro-consulate, he was called upon to
take the leading part in certain high functions
wherein the former Governor —a grandee with an
imposing beard and large presence—had greatly
distinguished himself. One of these was the
opening of the Baratarian Parliament. The
former Governor, in carrying out this ceremony,
looked like a civilised Moses in gorgeous uniform
reading from the tables of the law ; Don Adriano,
with his small, beardless face, looked like a dis-
guised girl whose head had been shaven, and who
was ashamed of the fact, and wanted to hide
herself under an enormous gold-laced cooked-hat.
He was a disappointment as a national spectacle.
There was no pomp about him and he knew it’
It was the same when he got on horseback to re-
view the army. People said he reminded them of
a Baratarian shrimp —a species of Crustacean
which, curiously enough, was only to bo obtained
in Barataria in a tinned state. But Don Adriano
was ambitious, and did not lose heart because of
these failures. He endeavoured to make up for
his want of size by his ubiquity, and rushed to and
fro in his province, opening everything that came
in his way, from a church to a watch-house and
smiling epileptically. He listened heroically to
the_ speeches of fat Alcades with suet-pudding
brains who first patronised him on account of his
youth and then grovelled at his feet in their own
slaver because of his rank. But the stress of all
this was telling upon him. His fairy soul was
visibly fretting its way through its little tenement
of clay at too fast a rate. He was growing to look
like the ghost of an obituary poet.
# * #
Don Adriano returned to his palace one
evening after a day laborious of affability and
found two eminent doctors, who had been sent for
by Donna Pomposa, waiting for him in the hall.
“We must keep those people in their places,” said
the haughty Eirbolg heiress.
“Come out of the draught, gentlemen,” whis-
pered Don Adriano apologetically, and led them
into a side-room. “We shall not be observed
here,” he added, closing the door. “Now, may I
enquire what your business may be ?”
“ Will your Excellency,” said one of them, “ be
good enough to open ”
“With pleasure,” interrupted Don Adriano,
smiling wearily, “ It is my business. I have done
nothing but open things since I arrived here.
What do you wish me to open ? ”
“Your mouth, my Lord,” replied the leading
doctor, sternly.
“Certainly, gentlemen,” said Don Adriano,
MELANCHOLY RESULT OF THE MELBOURNE GAS STRIKE.
“ but, as I am tired, I hope you will excuse me
from opening it with a speech.”
He opened it —and the doctors gazed, awe-
struck, at the ruin within.
“Your Excellency will either have to give up
trying to become popular or give up your Go-
vernorship,” solemnly observed both of the medical
men, after they had somewhat recovered from the
shock.
“ Neither,” exclaimed the Governor, firmly. “ I
will die at my post, smiling to the last—so.”
He slowly uncovered his teeth in his “No. 7
smile for mixed audiences.” The doctors gazed,
shuddered, and fled.
* * #
Don Adriano grew worse. One day the
postman brought a dirty envelope to the palace,
which contained the following letter :—
Mud Puddle.
To His Rile Hiness the Guvner.
Dear Lord, —Ime goin’ to start as a pubblikin in this
risin’ township to-morrer, and want you to cum andhopen
the ’otel.—Yours trooly, Bob Peters.
P.S.—Don’t fale, and bring your kilts. There will be
dansin’.
Don Adriano went, and returned with a large
cold that settled upon his small chest and caused
him almost to cough his little head off. But he
stuck manfully to what he considered to be his
duty, and, when he crawled out of his vice-regal
bed next morning, went into his reception-room
to attend to the usual levee of persons who came
there every day to ask him if he would open some-
thing or other. He sent them all away with an
amiable reply in the affirmative till only two were
left.
The first of these —a fat man, in broad-cloth—
advanced, and said : “ Your Hexcellency, I repre-
sent the firm of Hitch and Sons, the great Insecti-
cide Importers. We have received a large con-
signment of a noo brand, and would feel deeply
obliged if your Hexcellency would come round to
our establishment to-morrow and hopen a tin. A
silver tin-hopener will be specially provided for
the occasion.”
Don Adriano murmured that nothing would
give him greater delight than to _ assist, in his
humble way, the noble crusade carried on by In-
secticide-importers, more especially as he came
from a country where a great nobleman had
become immortalised among the people for his
hostility to insects, in the famous phrase, “ God
pleas the Duke of Argyle !”
The fat man went away beaming, and told the
head-clerk of Hitch and Sons, when he got back to
the establishment, that “those who said His
Hexcellency was not a born horator were basses !”
The second was a young man with a sodden
complexion, furtive features, and a general air of
gaudy decay. He came forward, and, stretching
out an unwashed claw in which was a small box,
remarked, “Beg yer Lordship’s pardon, but I’m
uny a pore orphan just arrived in this collarny,
an’ bearin' that you do all the hopenin’ business
’ere I thort I wud cum and arst you to hopen this
’ere match-box. I’ll bet you five quid you don’t,
and if you win I’ll shout for the bloomin’ aidey-
congs. There, now—that’s a sportin’ offer from
one gentleman to another !”
“This is the last straw,’’ gasped Don Adriano,
and fell down in a dead faint.
* * *
Don Adriano did not die, after all. He is
still Governor of Barataria, and still pitter-patters
around in his amiable little way, but all who know
him say that he will never be the same carefully-
mended ruin he was before receiving this shock.
He is growing more spectral every day, and will
probably fade away till there is nothing left of
him but a hollow cough before his time of office is
up. Adios, Don Adriano de Speranza !
CREEVE ROE.
The mistress of a French rustic landowner,
having proved unfaithful to him, he called
up his servant, informed him that it was his inten-
tion to kill himself, and requested, after his
death, that he would make a candle of his fat, and
carry it, lighted, to his mistress. Then he shot
himself, and the faithless rascal of a servant
refused to boil him down.
Dr. Hodgkinsou, of the N. Z. Parliament,
is not a celebrated man, but he seems likely to
become so at an early date. His latest exploit
was to enter his indignant protest against all
legislation for the protection of woman, on the
ground that she was and always had been suffi-
ciently protected by “the innate chivalry of man.”
Our opinion of the chivalry of man or any other
animal never was very high, and Hodgkinson has
not done much to elevate it. We have read about
a period not long past when females were hanged
for stealing a loaf, and when that branch of chi-
valry died out, man developed a capacity for
making woman sew shirts in an attic for seven-
teen hours a day, and paid her at the rate of Bs. a
week for her exertions, and if she didn’t keep on
sewing shirts till she died it was because she had
the good fortune to marry some honourable citizen
who probably pounded her almost to death with a
poker. No—the chivalry of man never amounted
to very much, nor did the chivalry of woman
either, for that matter. To see a respectable
Christian female, with a feeling heart, wallop a
tired horse along uphill, where the roads are stony
and rough, is a sight to make gods and angels
weep, and when she has banged the last hair off
the unhappy animal she will go and shed tears
over an idiotic maiden in a book, and only wake
up to realities in time to find that the second
youngest child is on fire, and the baby is sitting
down in a plate of hot hash.

“Please, God, Untie My Bonnet-Strings.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
One day in pensive mood I walked
A-musing over many things,
When on my ears there fell a voice—
“Please, God, untie my bonnet-strings !”
A little maid, with puckered face
And troubled air (for trouble wings
Its way, alas ! to little hearts) —
“Please, God, untie my bonnet-strings!”
Down by the dusty road she knelt,
Outwearied by her wanderings ;
Small hands were clasped, a small voice said,
“Please, God, untie my bonnet-strings !” ’
She turned her head to hear my step;
The trace of childish sufferings
Was in her eyes : she spoke once more—
“Please, God, untie my bonnet-strings !’
My stronger fingers soon had loosed
The knot which caused her ;
And off the maiden gaily ran,
While I mused over many things.
Surely, I thought, her childish faith
A more than childish lesson brings;
Can older heads learn nothing from
That little maid’s imaginings ?
Were it not well sometimes to say,
When tired of life’s meandering,s,
“Father, wo cannot find the clue—
Please, God, untie our bonnet-strings”?
A. G. STEPHENS.
Gym pic, Q,
Albuminuria.
or Bright’s Disease, is one of the most deadly
complaints that attack debilitated constitutions.
Owing to over exertion and over consumption
of nerve force, the system becomes gradually-
weakened and relaxation sets in. The thin mem-
branes of the kidneys are the first to go, and
medical authorities state that 50 per cent, of the
deaths, nowadays, occur indirectly from Bright’s
Disease though the patients and their medical
advisers never even suspected the presence of such
a deadly foe. The function of the kidney is to
separate the waste fluids from the blood, to purify
it, and this process is carried on by what is termed
by physiologists, “osmosis.” In weakened systems
the membranes become relaxed, and the albumen
of the blood finds its way with the waste fluids
through the kidney walls, and passes from the
body with the urine; the patient’s health and
strength thus gradually failing. Beyond the
presence of albumen in the water, Albuminuria
has not any positive symptoms; but general weak-
ness, which always leads to these serious disorders,
always signifies its presence by special signs, you
may get headache, spasmodic toothache, torturing
neuralgia, pain in small of the back, disinclination
to work, heavy, aching limbs on waking in the
morning, bloodshot eyes, poor appetite, or con-
stipation. These warning symptoms must not be
neglected, or as sure as a rat hole will sink a ship,
so sure will the patient’s life bo sacrificed The
remedy indicated is Clements Tonic, this will
restore all organs to a proper, healthy state,
and ensure correct performance of the processes of
digestion, assimilation, perspiration, secretion,
and elimination. Clements Tonic has a record of
cures effected such as no other medicine ever
secured, and we arc willing to prove our rep-
resentations by outside evidence.
Mr. Thomas Garrett, M.P., in speaking of our
remedy says; “I have taken Clements Tonic
with very great advantage.”
W. H. East, Esq., “Stanthorpe,”Dulwich Hill,
says: “I took three bottles of Clements Tonic
and am wonderfully improved, and have had no
return of the excruciating headaches since.
Mr. W. D. Braham, the crack tailor of King-
street, Sydney, speaks of it in the highest terms
of praise.
Sydney Cunningham, Esq., 71, Watkin-street,
Newtown, says : “I and my family have used
Clements Tonic with great success, and strongly
recommend it.”
Mrs. George Hilliard, King-street, Newtown,
writes that she has given it a fair trial, and can
testify to its great value.

names and addresses of scores of our patrons who

The Bulletin has a complaint to make
against Illustrated Bits (London). In the first
place, it objects to be credited by Bits with hog-
wash which has never appeared in The Bulletin
columns, while its good, original matter is either
quoted unacknowledged or credited to other papers.
That Kyneton (Vic.) hospital patient who
had two ounces of his brain removed is getting on
favourably. Possibly if the rest of his brain had
also been removed, the result would have been
much the same. It is an open question whether
brains are a necessity after all. There is a goblin
tale of a Spanish physician who chloroformed a
criminal, kindly lent to him by the Government,
and scooped out the whole of his thinking organ,
leaving his skull a mere dry shell like a hollow
Salvation drum. The patient never bothered him-
self about anything after that. He never thought
or listened or saw or tasted or smelt —he simply
lay on a sofa and breathed for twenty years, and
his proprietor blew his victuals into him through
a blow-pipe, and used to open the silver lid which
formed the top of his head and allow his scientific
friends to inspect the empty void within, and
sometimes he would heave a pound of dry peas
into the cavity to hear them rattle. Finally, how-
ever, the doctor died and left the ruined auto-
maton on the sofa to his successor; and that
gentleman grew tired of it, and rolled the pro-
perty downhill one dark night, and it was never
heard of again. Possibly it is lying at the bottom
of that hill and breathing right ahead to this day.

in N.S.W. Assembly years ago, has just died,
wealthy, in England. He was more a Victorian
than a New South Welshman, and was one of the
founders of the old Melbourne Club.

to avert revolutions.

Rev, Mr. Marsden, says a contemporary,
filled the incumbency of St. John’s, Parramatta,
from 1789 to 1838; Rev. Mr. Booart from 1838 to
1854, Rev. Canon King from 1855 to 1867, and
Venerable Archdeacon Giinther from that date to
the present. Thus the parish has only had four
incumbents in the course of a century,

The late Judge Williams, of the Victorian
Supreme Court, father of his present Honor of
that name, was one of the laziest and most im-
patient men who ever sat upon a Bench., Once he
had before him in a long argument H. 0. Chap-
man (afterwards a S.C. judge in N.Z.) “You
have stated that point before, Mr. Chapman,”
said Williams, “but it is so long ago you may
have forgotten it!”
Rip Van Winkle Frencharn, who has beeil
lugged up in the Victorian Legislature as Bendigo
discoverer, has managed to secure a committee.
Just upon 40 years ago did Frencharn benefit the
world to the extent to which he now wants to be
paid. But M‘Lellan says gold was found on the
station of Fenton, the squatter, long before
Frencharn, and kept dark, as they tried to do on
Suitor’s ranche, in California.
When Queen Victoria was about to marry
she was anxious to have the rank and title of King
Consort conferred by Act of Parliament on Prince
Albert. Lord Melbourne thought the proposal an
unwise one, and tried to pass it off. The Queen,
however, persisted, and at last Lord Melbourne
said : “ Let us hear no more of it, Madam. If
you once get the English people into the way of
making kings, you will get them into the way of
unmaking them.”
Tom Playford, the new S.A. 16-stone
Premier, may be likened to Cincinnatus, the
Roman. Tom was a market-gardener, and is an
authority on pumpkins. Brawny and healthy,

LAMPS, BUT THEY ARE VERY SCARCE, AND TO GET ’EM
HOME SAFELY I HAD TO BUY A REVOLVER.”

of even a stonewalling session, and has some
few brains. His friends call him “Honest Tom,”
but his honesty isn’t any more conspicuous than
that of the average man. Probably he earned
the title because his bluntness and coarse force of

Frawncis Habigle is now on his return
journey to Horsetrailyer- Says a Dublin paper :
An Orange soiree was given in the Round Room of the
Rotunda on Monday to celebrate the “bi-centenary of the
Battle of the Boyne.” The Grand Master of the City of
Dublin Lodge presided. The majority of those present
were ladies. The room was decorated with orange lilies
and representations of skulls and crossbones, &e. A
selection of brethren forming parallel lines crossed hands
and a silly-looking procession, headed by the chairman,
carrying a closed Bible and a lily, marched between the
lines. Speeches followed from Colonel Waring, M.P., the
Rev. R. R. Kane, and a tourist named Abigail from New
South Wales. The last-named speaker described himself
as a member of Parliament from a self-governing colony,
condemned ’Ome Rule for Ireland, eulogised “lor and
border,” and moved a vote of thanks to Lord ’Artington.
That speaker was evidently Frawncis.

Society last week fined an employer £5, for break-
ing the rule of Wednesday half-holiday.
The cost of the Premier Building Assurin'

to have been only £4500 after all. W hy, we have

of David Syme, who has the hang of the Tln.u.n
circulation, even in boom-time.
POLITICAL POINTS
The female workers of Brisbane propose to
form a union.
Ex-Treasurer Holder will be the leader of
the Opposition in S.A. Assembly.
“The working man’s paradise and the
employer’s hell,” says a Melbourne man.
One of two things must soon be faced by
S.A. Parliament; Coalition or dissolution.
There are in N.Q. twenty-one newspapers
in favour of Separation and eight against it.
In the strike, the clergy are, of course,
unanimously on the side which uses Heaven’s
artillery.
Mr. F, A. Wright M.L.A., has votes in
twenty-nine electorates in N.S.W, Universal
suffrage, close up.
Yates writes that a certain gentleman is
not qualified for the House of Lords, because he is
deaf only in one ear.
Sir Benjamin Benjamin, as a candidate
for the Victorian Legislative Council, throws a
new light on the Lady Loch diamonds.
The alleged ladies range against the women,
and the alleged gentlemen against the men. One
class, it is plain, must be abolished. Which?
The Bulletin told its readers months ago
that Flayford and Kingston would part. And
now it has come to pass. Adelaide papers please
copy.
“Divorce” Shields is looked upon as the
Attorney-General of any coming Victorian Go-
vernment that may supplant the cunning barnacle,
Gillies.
Melbourne democratic prejudice against
the Employers’ Union was aggravated by the
news that the sharks of the Law Institute wished
to join in a body and protect the sacred rights of
capital.
There is a big scandal in the Victorian
Lands Department. Civil servants are taking up
laud, and refuse to take action against non-com-
plying lessees, fearing their own little plots may
be endangered.
The N.S.W. Government has resolved to
barricade Circular Quay, Sydney, in order to
facilitate work by non-unionists. From a French
point of view it is very bad to set the example of
erecting barricades.
The Playford Ministry is certainly not
wanting in variety. It contains two lawyers, a
market gardener, farmer, brewer and the usual
journalist. No Ministry can live without a
journalist for ballast.
The N.S.W. Trades and Labour Council’s
offer of a thousand special constables has con-
siderably incensed those who hoped that the
leaders of the trades-unions would incite their
men to break the peace.
Murray Smith says : “There was a time
when the cause of Labour was righteous.” This
to the Melbourne Employers’ Union. Now when
was the cause of Labour righteous ? During the
centuries and ages that it was ground under the
! i oel of Capital ?
Judging from what fell from the lips of the
delegates at the Railway Conference dinner, Vic-
toria and South Australia are satisfied with the
rule of their Commissioners, while N.S. Wales
and Queensland are not.
Preparations for the social revolution are
quietly proceeding in England. One of the great
objects to be attained by the short hours’ cam-
paign is the finding of time to enable workmen to
be drilled in the volunteer and militia forces.
Dillon, M.P., upholds the boycott and
defies the Pope. Healy, M.P., says that Irish
tenants who pay their rents and allow their fami-
lies to starve, are assassins; also that Australia is
at present really only helping to fill the landlords’
pockets.
At the forthcoming elections in Western
Australia, the widening of the new Constitution
will be prominently brought before the electors.
Already a number of candidates are advocating
the introduction of manhood suffrage and the
abolition of the property qualifications for candi-
dates.
Lieutenant-Governor Sir Alfred Stephen’s
Bill, punishing with fine or imprisonment the act
of “endeavouring to induce men not to work,”
constitutes a piece of impudent, but impotent,
tyranny, which would have adorned a lag-time
proclamation by Governor Darling. It simply
smells of Port Arthur.
Recent statistics show that the greatest
number of women, in proportion to population,
employed in industrial occupations in European
countries is found in the United Kingdom, the
number so employed being 4,500,000, per cent age
12T. Italy comes next with a per centage of 11 'O,
and France shows 9 'B9.
The latest mutiny in the British army has
broken out among the Hussars stationed at Canter-
bury, where Thomas a’Beckett lies buried and
Henniker Heaton enjoys their votes and interest.
We expect to hear that Henniker has been sent
down to the old Cathedral city “ to infuse a spirit
of loyalty ” into the troops.
The Employers’ Union has asked the press
to discontinue calling blackleg labour by its pre-
sent name, and demands that in future it bo
termed “ free labour,” Any paper which calls
blackleg labour “ free ” is lying by inference, in-
asmuch as it is lending itself to the insinuation
that to be a trades-unionist is to be a slave.
In a congratulatory letter to Mr. C. Teece,
on his election for Goulburn, that fine, old fisher
of men,” Premier Parkes, says : “ I earnestly hope
that your service in Parliament may be a long
service of honour and usefulness.” In other
words, Parkes hopes that Teece will form another
joint in his tail. An old dog for a hard road.
The Australian Intercolonial Railway Con-
ference among delegates from the various railway
associations have declared that “48 hours per week
should be the maximum hours of labour for all
positions of continuous work, and that every hour
worked over and above that number should be paid
for at overtime rates, such rate not to be less than
time-and-a-half. ”
Little Lord Hopetoun, playing the role of
bucolic magnate at the Melbourne Agricultural
Show luncheon, twanged forth: “My pursuits,
my pleasures, my business, my means of supply,
have all come from the country.” Hopetoun’s
business is something we now hear of for the first
time, but there can be no question as to his means
of supply coming from the country ;
O lordship, it can’t be denied
(Except with a lie)
The country is doomed to provide
Your means of supply.
’Tis plain, as you languidly ride
In a chariot by,
You never were built to provide
Your means of supply.
Your game it is putting on “side”
And snubbing small fry
Whose duty it is to provide
The means of supply.
A democrat’s temper is tried,
He asks with a sigh
“ How long are poor folks to provide
Those means of supply ?”
From morning until eventide
One cannot tell why—
Men labour, Lord H., to provide
Your means of supply.
Two halves of this universe wide
Are joined by a tie,
For each has to help to provide
Your means of supply.
Yourself and your nice-looking bride,
With the bright, merry eye,
Can laugh while the others provide
Your means of supply.
In plenty and peace ye abide,
Though things go awry
With them who have got to provide
Your means of supply.
A landlord or Gov. may be “ guyed ”
For his stupidity,
Yet still do the scoffers provide
His means of supply.
The cause of old-family pride.
Your Excellen-cy,
Is the fact that it doesn’t provide
The means of supply.
A son, when your spirit is skied,
(That is, if you die)
You’ll leave, for our sons to provide
With means of supply.
Unless busy bees shall decide,
In the sweet bye-and-bye,
No longer the drones to provide
With means of supply.

FREETRADE or PROTECTION.
Some vote Protection, some Freetrade,
As best for this young country’s weal.
But ask the youth or blushing maid,
And each will answer —Pemell’s Meal !
As best, because it has been tried,
There’s nothing half so good beside.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap, I wean.

Society
There was lately in the N.S.W. force an
enthusiastic constable who holds the record for
the arrest of drunks, of whom in one day he
unassistedly “ ran-in ” 27. His mode of procedure
was invariably to “give ’em a heavy fall and
knock the wind out of ’em.” After a most
hilarious career he resigned. This industrious
constable, by the way, used to carry a revolver !
* * *
A statement appears on page 4 of Lloyd’s
Weekly Newspaper, dated 13th July, 1890, on
the authority of the British Consul at Brussels,
that no M.D. diploma has been issued from the
University of that city for 29 years. Surely this
cannot be true, as quite a number of medical men
are practising in Australia upon, among other
qualifications, diplomas purporting to have
emanated from Brussels University within the
last ten years.
* * #
Melbourne Exhibition turns out a frost
and a fraud, with £241,000 deficit. Cowen’s
orchestra concerts cost £30,000, and realised
£9,000. Deakin declared in Parliament, before-
hand, that the Exhibition could not cost more
than £20,000 ! The official reason for the failure
is “deadheadism.” But then it would take
4,820,000 shillings to represent £241,000 deficiency.

Out of five recent Sydney defaulters, three
were Sunday-school teachers, and one was the
secretary of an Orange Lodge, in which the Bible
was regarded as “ the secret of England’s great-
ness.”
Is H.R.H. Tummy once more in the thrall
of the shent-per-shenters ? London “ Sassiety ”
is shocked at the fact that Albert Edward honours,
above all others of his set, Baron Hirsoh, a vulgar
foreign financier. Which reminds us of an old-
time Melbourne reporter, who used to shine at
social festivities, such as the Mayor’s ball, or the
Masonic annual, as representative of the Age.
The day before any such function he would wait
upon a celebrated Hebrew usurer, who had
social ambitions for his daughter, and plead,
“ My dear Mr. Fahcksrnore, have I your permis-
sion to take Miss Fahcksrnore to the ball to-
morrow evening ? I have tickets for two, and can
introduce her to all the people present worth
knowing.” The financier would “ Gott-bless-you-
my-poy,” and cordially assent. Then the scribe
would put in a good time, behave most affec-
tionately towards the young lady, and in a day or
two wait on the smiling father to discount his bill.
And, what’s more, he pulled it off every time.
When, one fine afternoon, Mr. Fahcksrnore heard
that his gallant young literary acquaintance had
skedaddled to Queensland, leaving many lament-
ing creditors, he burst into tears and said, “My
poor monish ! Tam tose reporter coves, dey vhas
all schwindlefrauds, und ven der hat vhas on der
roof vhas covered. ” The gallant rep. has since
developed a middle-aged embonpoint, but the Mel-
bourne hawks have never seen him since. Queens-
land is still his home.

Here is a little gratis hint for any one who
happens to want to make £5O without earning it.
In an issue of a week or two back, a Sydney
daily inadvertently published a “lost and
found ” advertisement about an overcoat, stating
that if the thing was returned “no questions
would be asked,” or something of the sort, and
thereby became liable for a £5O penalty, to anyone
who likes to sue for it. Now, here is a chance for
the Sunday-informer class to organise a little
syndicate.
# # #
A fast young squatter was not long ago
settled by an indulgent mother on a neat little
Riverina station-property. Finding himself hard-
up some months before shearing he negotiated with
a well-known wool-broking firm to sell the clip on
the sheep’s back. An agent was sent-up to inspect
with power to close, a price was agreed upon,
whisky and cards were introduced, and ere the
daylight next morning the young squatter found
himself minus the wool and the £4850 he had
obtained for it!

* # #
The Victorian railway officials dragged up
a mechanic, a few days ago, on a charge of owing
them three-halfpence for a railway-ticket, and
when the bench inquired why they hadn’t also
brought up the 150 merchants, clergymen, and
capitalists who were found defrauding the revenue
in the same way some three months back, the
prosecuting lawyer suggested that most likely that
gang had all given wrong names and addresses
and so couldn’t be found. Any one who can fancy
Parkes sitting conspicuously on the Treasury
bench, and no one able to find him because he had
given a wrong name and address, will understand
to the full magnificence of this explanation.

Miss Gresley, the daughter of a banker, and in
her virginal days was an attractively exuberant

disco verable pocket of hers ?
* * *
One of the things which want to be put
down is that person who signs her name in the
visitor’s-book at .Government House, and then
proceeds to enter her husband and her father and
mother, and all the family, and the baby, and the
dog, and her married sister-in-law, and all their
joint and several ancestors back to the Deluge.
Asa rule, she thinks nothing of keeping eighteen
people waiting while she writes up all these par-
ticulars, and all these people use strong language
about her, and wonder if the book will prove large
enough to hold the details, and the flunkey in the
background glares at her with derision. And then,
when she has finished her labours, and gone off
with a firm conviction that she will receive twenty
cards of invitation to the next ball, an official steps
for ard with a hoarse chuckle and draws his pen
through the entire list. Many a stout, combustible
female named Stubbs or Thompson would save
herself a lot of tears and mortification in the
future by casting her inflamed eye over this para-
graph.

• * #
The efforts of the employers to have their
blackleg-labourers called “free” labourers, have
been already anticipated by the other side. Down
on the Oastlereagh occurred the following dialogue.
A Sydney man fresh from the town asked a
bronzed old shearer : “ Are there many non-union
shearers in this district?” “There ain’t any,”
was the reply. “No non-union shearers !” said
the other; “I thought there were plenty of them
hereabouts.” “ Look here, sonny,” said the
ancient impressively, “ there’s only two sorts _ of
shearers —Union and Scab. If a man ain’t Union
he’s Scab. There’s no such thing as a non-union
shearer.” i

«• * *
Time out of mind a tradition has existed
among educated people to the effect that professors
scarcely ever wash themselves all over. When
this arose it would be hard to say—perhaps in the
days of the Scotists and Thomists when professors
were too much interested in arguing whether
names were things, or merely names, to spare time
for personal ablutions. No doubt there was some
foundation for it. The great Person is a case in
point, not to mention the greater Immanuel Kant,
who saw no reason for washing himself, as he
never perspired. Professor M‘Coy, of the Mel-
bourne University, is, however, a living contra-
diction to the truth of this tradition. He lives at
Brighton, and takes a bath in the sea every morn-
ing in the year. As Professor M‘Coy has never
been suspected of fanaticism on any point, other
reasons have been assigned for this extraordinary
conduct. Some people say that he is carrying it
on for a wager; others that he performs it as a
penance for some unconfessed crime, such as killing
a rival naturalist, who had discovered anew kind
of beetle, and appropriating the discovery to him-
self ; and a yet larger division hint that ho is
financially interested in Brighton as a bathing
resort, and bathes every morning as an advertise-
ment.

The discontent in Trinity College, Mel-
bourne, which arose out of a student being “ sent
down ” for alleged “disloyalty” to the Warden
still continues to smoulder in the breasts of the
students. The seven seniors, expelled by Warden
Leeper for assisting at the burning of his effigy
have since, owing to the boycott instituted by the
others, been reinstated. The unsatisfactory
quality of the food is responsible for much of the
discontent. The students have refused to attend
the proposed enquiry because of the Warden’s
declared intention to preside,

I tried to heat my hands
At the great fire of life, so warm to touch,
And closer crouched before the blazing brands,
Nor felt| their genial radiance over-much.
But, bending nearer to the beckoning brands.
Upleapt they treacherous and seared my hands.
I bared my heart to life’s cool, tempting breeze
To fan upon the passions it had known,
The tears, the terrors, and the secresies,
With all the sear of sorrows, borne alone.
But the cool breeze turned icy in its part
And froze the life-blood in my bare, hot heart.
I shut my heart to it and clasped my hands ;
No breeze should harm me now, nor cruel brands.
But loud laughed life in fiendish mockery
And echoed —echoed —all it knew of me.
What wot it that I hide my burning palms ?
What wot it that love’s debt is never paid ?
Beneath the mask life knows the storms and
calms,
And ridicules the cynic she has made.
Ina M. W.
# * *
Governor Hopetoun has opened up a great
deposit of solemn thought by the statement he
likes Australia, and has “no desire to go home.”
We expect he has not. At home he is only a
peer, and not a very large one either; in Aus-
tralia he is a deputy-monarch, and feels as if h e
weighed a ton. Also, he is the Fount of Mercy,
which probably agrees with him, and he has a
palace .and a salary, and the provincial mayors
entertain him, and take him round the bone-
crushing establishments, and show him 50 tons of
old scrap fat and bad eggs that are waiting to be
boiled down, and read him addresses, and he has
a good time generally. Governor Bowen, how-
ever, struck a brighter inspiration still; at every
town he visited he used to state that the one thing
that he longed for above all other earthly con-
siderations was to be buried there, and sleep in
the picturesque local cemetery till the day of
resurrection. And the tears come into our eyes
when we think that this humble ambition missed
fire. He hasn’t been buried yet.
Some ridiculous person advertises in a
Northern paper that he has “A Good Home for a
Christian Youth who has been to sea and can make
himself useful about the house ; C.E. and English
preferred.” Likewise, small appetite will be
regarded with favour, we presume ; furthermore,
a contempt for filthy lucre in the form of wages
will be hailed as an outward evidence of inward
Kerristianity. Christianity, also Religious Fer-
vour, is a good excuse for meanness, and a never-
failing substitute for ready cash. Amen !
Bookstall Smith, first Lord of the Ad-
miralty, is to be elevated to the peerage. The
British peerage is pretty nearly all Smith now,
except that part of it which is thinly-disguised
Jones. Some of it made his pile out of pills, and
some of it deait in coal and old bones, and another
section was in the slop-clothing industry, and the
rest got into the aristocracy on account of its
Grandmother's immorality. But the legend still
liolds out that it was descended from some pic-
turesque baron who came out strong in ancient
times, and the British sycophant, who worships
old descent, holds it in reverence on account of
the pedigree which it hasn’t got, and bows down
oefore the peer of ancient lineage whose mother
was named Jemimar Harm, and manufactured
pies. And so when Smith attains to the peerage
it will presently be rumoured that he is lineally
descended from Johannes Smithianus, who was
short in his cash in the days of Julius Caesar and
had to clear out in consequence, or else it will
transpire that he is directly descended from
Balaam’s Ass, and he will be respected accordingly.
Dear Bulletin.—l have seen your “ par.”
that Millar Bros, have tired of Chinese miners’
labour and have replaced them with Euro-
peans. That is hardly correct. The Euro-
pean miners lately introduced to the Northern
Territory only relieved the former workmen,
who are now down your way along with their
manager. Millar still hangs to the yellow man,
and must have 50 to 00 of them doing various jobs
on his ground. The claims are looking tip-top so
far, and I fancy the idea of the Millars is to split
them up into sections and float in England. I
may be wrong in this, but circumstances point to
that belief. It will probably suit the owners, but
for the country I, for one, will regret such a course.
We have had numerous companies working ground
here, but none have held out, and the last has just
gone bung. Too much money goes to London for
“officers;” the management is done from Lon-
don ; cables cost money; and the whole system
has been proved by us to be rotten as Royalty
itself. Millars have a remarkably good thing, and
no doubt they will ask big figures when they part
with it. You must understand that up to now
their expenditure cannot have been less than
£20,000, and they show good work for it.—Yours
faithfully, G.M. Fort Darwin, N.T.

A Rambling Complaint.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
You can talk as you may,
And think as you like,
I mean what I say—
I’m sick of the strike ;
Which side is the wrong,
Or which side is right,
I’d care not as long
As they left me a light.
I work with my hand—
At the end of a pen :
I belong to the band
Of the labouring men ;
I don’t use a spade,
Or perform with the pick,
And I am not afraid
To admit that I’m sick.
It isn’t my lot
To go stoking up coal,
Yet I graft till I’ve got,
Ah ! a pain in the soul;
My ballad to-night
To comrades who stoke
Is give me a light—
I’ll give you a joke.
I’ll stand, as I’ve stood,
By the side of each mate
Who works for the good
Of himself and the State;
But I likewise remark
To each Billy and Dick—
To be left in the dark
Makes a journalist sick.
Trades-Union hands
Don’t monopolise toil,
The mainstay of lands
Are the tillers of soil;
If those hai’dy sons
Of Labour, says I,
Didn’t stick to their guns
We should jolly well die.
If all struck at once
At a Trades leader’s shout,
It is plain to a dunce
We must soon peter-out;
I am tyranny’s fo3,
Be it little or great
’Tis a duty I owe
To the good of the State.
You can talk as you may,
And think as you like,
I mean what I say—
I am sick of the strike
(Though probably right
At the time it began)
If it puts out the light
Of a labouring man.
Melbourne, August 29, 1890.
E.F.
Turkey wants England to lix a date for the
carrying-out of the frequently-sworn promise to
evacuate Egypt. Salisbury coolly replies; “It
would be premature for England to evacuate
Egypt at the present time.” The British bond-
holding Shylooks have not yet got their “ pound
of flesh. ” It will always be ‘ ‘ premature ” for
J. Bull to do justice towards the weak.
A speaker at the N.S.W. Railway Con-
ference the other evening cracked the old chestnut
that “Parliaments are what the people make
them.” As a matter of fact, while plural-voting
exists Parliaments can only be what the allotment-
voters of the community make them. The speaker,
perhaps, dimly apprehended this when he said
later on that he hoped the day was coming when
Labour would be more fully represented.
What is the matter with the Northumber-
land (N.S.W.) miners that they don’t unite to
establish an evening daily newspaper of their own?
That 10,000 of the most sturdy men under Heaven
should be such fools as to support newspapers
which either sit on the fence or are avowedly their
deadly enemies passes all comprehension. New-
castle and district is quite big enough to hand-
somely maintain a small, but live, Radical evening
daily.
The Land of the Crocodile.
Two native blacks recently walked osten-
tatiously into a public-house in Roma. Both had
on clean shirts and new pants. They marched up
to the bar and ordered “ two fella rum,” with the
easy grace and professional confidence of the
highest type of civilised shearer. The landlord
thought they were two troopers out of uniform, or
a couple of regular station-boys come to town with
a little surplus cash. They drank three rums in
succession, and then slowly sauntered to' the door.
The landlord called out, “ Hi! come back ! Baal
you been pay me ! ” And the elder darkie
replied, “ 'Bright, old man! pay be damn ! We
trust you!” Then the jovial myalls marched
gaily off, serenely happy in the knowledge of the
Act which imposes a heavy penalty on publicans
supplying liquor to aboriginals. And yet the
Presbyterian Church of Australia cherishes a mis-
guided belief that the Queensland blacks are
deficient in the first principles of modern
Christianity.
* # *
The first Queensland fossils of extinct
fauna were found in King’s Creek, on the Darling
Downs, in October, 1847. Since that time many
new specimens have been added by the Legislative
Council, the latest addition being found in Rock-
hampton and presented solely because it was a
personal friend of Mount Morgan Pattison.
There is a rapidly-growing desire among the
people of Queensland to replace these post-pliocene
A UNIONIST MAKES OVERTURES TO A “BLACKLEG."
fossils of extinct nominee mammals with new
varieties selected by the people themselves from
the period when live men appear upon the scene—
the Quaternian epoch, in the dawn of Intelligence.
# * *
The first caucus meeting of the Liberal
party, after Griffith selected his Ministry, was one
of the liveliest on record. The union of the two
knights, and the happy burying of the old steel-
rail war hatchet, was a rude shock to the partisan
bigots among Griffith’s followers. He was at-
tacked by the bigots from motives of bigotry, and
by others who were mad because their transcen-
dental qualifications for office had been overlooked.
Groom was one of the most relentless critics of the
Coalition. Only national disaster could follow the
omission of his name as Minister for Lands.
Griffith surveyed him with unspeakable thoughts,
and an et tu Groome expression. And at the close
of the caucus, Griffith, in atone remarkable for its
intense sincerity, consigned the Toowoomba schis-
matic to a region where molten platina is usually
mistaken for ice-cream.
* * *
The Whip of the Coalition Ministry is
“Jack” Annear, one of the members for Mary-
borough. Jack would likely have been Minister
for Railways but that his sympathetic good nature
was not deemed proof against the unscrupulous
assaults of deputations, and melancholy men, and
lovely women in distress. He is quite satisfied
with the position of whip, as it entails no neglect
of his own business. His political income is re-
presented by £3OO as member and £350 as whip—
nearly three-fourths the salary of a Minister.
Jack‘Hamilton, member for Cooktown, will still
act as whip for the party now gone into opposi-
tion. Jack is one of the best boxers and best
swordsmen in Australia. He was the “ best man”
among ten thousand at the Palmer rush. Both
“ whips ” are honourable men, not in the least
likely to take any mean advantage, and each is
the best man his party could possibly select.
* * *
Not vei’y long ago a love-sick officer in the
North Queensland native police sent a specimen
of what he believed to be a new plant to Bailey,
the Colonial Botanist, with a request that it be
named after a beautiful Northern girl to whom he
was engaged to be married. He had a romantic
desire to pass his darling on to immortality as
Achyranthes Louisa, or BulbophyUurn Annie, or
some other simple poetic autograph that his
posterity would fall over in the dark and would
make them believe their grandmother was a
Greek princess or an ex-Queen of Madagascar,
sister of Ranovalomanjaka. While Bailey
was critically examining the new specimen
to ascertain the jaw-cracking order to which
it belonged, the girl, with the cold and
heartless fickleness characteristic of so many
daughters of Eve, forgot the absent lover, who was
away avenging the murder of a Oftinarnan among
the Northern myalls, and basely married a man
from Cooktown ! On the day this disastrous in-
telligence reached the police officer, and just as he
was about to rush off to wire to Bailey to call the
plant after his horse or his dog, or one of his black-
boys, a letter arrived from the Colonial Botanist
to say that the “ new specimen belongs to the
order Eygophyllcce, trihulus cistoides, a well-
known perennial procumbent plant, said to be
fatal to stock when eaten on an empty stomach.'
This second blow was fatal! The unhappy lover;
in the sudden frenzy of despair, called his faithful
troopers around him and plunged into the depths
of the forest, breathing slaughter against all forms
of life on this planet. No blacks have been seen
in the district since that eventful day, nor have
even the balance of the Chinamen been restored
to the bosom of their cabbage-beds.
# * *
Queensland is not a colony where the
Chinese leper enjoys a long life. His existence
here is not leased on any fixity of tenure. If he is
discovered in the interior of the colony, he rarely
ever reaches the coast. He starts for civilisation
in charge of a policeman who generally loses him
on the way down. When camped in some lonely
spot, the leper escapes in the night, rambles away
into the scrub and can never be found again. The
manner in which a clumsy, long-heeled China-
man, whose toes have unanimously dropped off,
manages to conceal his tracks and defy the eyes
of the keenest trackers, is a mystery not yet lucidly
explained. I can remember two lepers who
vanished in this remarkable manner. They were
in charge, on each occasion, of a powerful police-
man and two black troopers. Yet both escaped
in the scrub country, and successfully baffled their
pursuers ! Probably they fell down a stump-hole
or were carried off by a cassowary. While some
poet composes a little poem to the memory of
those poor lost Chinamen, I may record my
earnest appreciation of the many valuable services
rendered the State by common-sense Queensland
policemen.

The English miners are striking fur eight
hours. The employers offer to recognise ten hours
as a day’s work. The miners have a most righteous
cause.
London Daily Chronicle has ceased to
support Unionism since Edward Lloyd died. The
young Lloyds have lots of energy, push and money
and are going to make their paper boom.
Cholera in a virulent form has started in
China. There is some relief in hearing this, but
then unhappily the Chinaman has been allowed to
increase and multiply to such an extent that the
biggest epidemic in the world couldn’t shift them.
It is like trying to remove a boa-constrictor with
an egg-spoon.
By the amalgamation of the “ Man about
Town” and the “Bird of Freedom” the British
public are doomed to have additional weekly doses
of drivel fired into them about the “ Master,” and
the “Shifter,”and the “ Tale-Pitcher,” and all the
other dull-witted drunkards with whose gin-
scented breath and pimply eruptions the “Pink
’Un ” has for years endeavoured to familiarise
London cab-drivers and noblemen of the Kim-
bolton-Dunlo type.
Book - hawkers in Kansas have been
earning an honest penny of late by travelling
round armed with a double-fountain pen and two
kinds of ink which are used thus : An agreement
is made out with a writing-fluid that fades, and
signed by the victim with ink that does not fade.
Then in the sacred seclusion of his own home the
book fiend substitutes an entirely different agree-
ment altogether, and the dupe finds himself
confronted with a promise to pay for articles he
never heard mentioned before.
A deputation of faded women of uncertain
age—past mistresses of the Social Gimlet-hole
Purity Society, and the Women’s Christian Tem-
perance Union, &c. —brought the severe batteries
of their prim charms to bear on Duncan Gillies,
the Victorian Premier, the other day, and asked
him to abolish all railway refreshment-rooms in
Victoria. Duncan blushed more redly than his
own hair, and sucked his thumb with cherubic,
infantile innocence. But the “ dears” didn’t got
there all the same, though Gillies is an inex-
perienced bachelor. Duncan, as a man who can
appreciate nectar on occasions, declared that there
were many people who believed they needed
“ better refreshment than water” on a journey, and
he wasn’t going to stop their travelling tipple.
“ Better refreshment ?” shrieked a staid young miss
of 69, “what can be better than God’s pure
water ?” And the sisterhood clung to each other
in mutual support, and told Duncan he was a
“ker-uel man” —“a nard-’earted ’edge’og” -“a
tempting fiend, ” Sic. Pearly tears, like dead fishes’
eyes, trembled in Gillies’s orbs a second, and then
fell with a rattle on his luncheon-tray. He could
only sob —“Boo-hoo, f’givc me, ladies ; I’m a lone
bachelor; boo-hoo, put your demands in writing.”
Then he fled from the room and rushed to the
Chief Secretary’s quarters, saying—“Alf., you’ll
have to receive those dogmad tabbies next time.”
Deakin peaked his goatee and said he didn’t mind.
Dear Bulletin, —ou tsaturuay, .me .Loth
August, Thargornindah (Q.) laughed until the
tears came. Sub-inspector Stuart arrived from
Charleville on the previous evening, and early on
this day rumour floated about that we might
expect to see some complicated military evolutions
in the police-enclosure; for Mr. Stuart’s wide
reputation for accomplishments in strategy and
tactics —a continuation of his renown as a com-
mander of blackfellows some years ago—had
preceded him here. Accordingly we were all on the
qui vive for a gaudy display, when about ten
o’clock we observed the saynyer-constable and
another policeman drawn up on the very cramped
verandah of the police-quarters. The inspector,
assisted by a sub-inspector, “sized” these two
men, then formed them into line, and opened the
ranks for inspection. He put them through small-
arms drill and, having marched the- pair down
from their perch on the verandah, through com-
pany and battalion drill also. One blackfellow
and half-a-dozen surveyors’ boring-rods were called
into requisition at this stage. The most ludicrous
episode was when the two police, with the black-
fellow and five boring-rods, formed as half
battalions in columns of double companies, with
the sub-inspector as right-marker to dress by.
One word of command we made note of was,
“ Column on the march, half-left-turn ” (this vyas
to steer clear of a certain small wooden erection
which was in the way). Another command was,
“From open intervals close upon the right of No.
1 Company.” Remember, through all this the
parade strength was five souls —the inspector and
his aide two policemen and a black-tracker, whose
pants were most rudimentary. Inspector Stuart
promises to visit us every three months unless the
exigencies of the State cab him to another sphere
of action. We look forward with expectancy to his
great November review.— Charleville.

There was a remarkable tracing of typhoid
fever last week near Toorak (Melb.). Nightsoil,
put in a fruit-orchard, flowed to a creek where a
dairyman’s cows drank, and 23 of that dairyman’s
customers were seized with typhoid in a month or
two.

A mob of special constables, a genteel
mob, almost a swell one, has been sworn in at
Melbourne. No working-class people are wanted,
the staple is young warehousemen, and the like,
from Manchester-laue. They are fine young
fellows, who would take a delight in cracking, jf
they dared, the heads of the vulgar. The militia
have not been called out, as the great majority are
trades-unionists.

The Referee.
A number of rabid Australian patriots are now
Attempting to attribute the Seventh Eleven failure to the
unfairness of English umpires, while a few of them go so
far a» 66 say that previous teams have been cheated out
of victories now and again. The Bulletin— which has
had reason to feel satisfied with its arguments and predic-
tions concerning Anglo-Australian cricket for some years
past—-is disgusted, rather than astonished, at the folly of
newspapers which encourage young simpletons to “put
it down to the umpire ” and print their balderdash under
the impression that the public at large desire such a
poisonous decoction of “ blow” to soothe their wounded
feelings. English umpires may, in some cases, show a
leaning towards their own people, but they are probably
as honest, take them altogether, as folks on this side of
i'uo water. At any rate, they don’t deserve such
asinine insinuations as we find published in the
Argus- A man writes to that solemn periodical:—
“ Another curious thing I noticed was the £25 given by a
sporting paper for the best batting and bowling average
to professionals, and where it was stated that five of the
fiowlers had formed a syndicate. It seemed to me that it
would also be an easy thing for the umpires to form a
syndicate in connection with the bowlers, and as the Aus-
tralians are a common enemy this may account for the
many bad decisions given, and in the majority of
oases where these leading howlers are in the syndicate.”
This is the merest schoolboy hogwash. The five bowlers
formed a syndicate because, playing on different parts of
the country, one might be working on a wet wicket while
the others were at a big disadvantage on dry ones. Seeing,
then, that either one of the five might be good e nough to
win the “pony,” with the assistance of a little bit of
luck, what they did was to agree to divide the luck and
the money with it. The idea of five bowlers, and Lord
knows how many umpires, forming a syndicate to “ cut
up” £25 is too ridiculous. There should he no occasion
for The Bulletin to point out once again that the alleged
Australian supremacy in cricket never did exist except in
heated young imaginations. The earlier of Murdoch’s
teams had “soft” things on as a rule, and seldom or
never met really representative English elevens. They
woke up cricket in the old country, however.
The professionals, long regarded as lazy fieldsmen
and mechanical bowlers, pulled themselves together,
and have kept together ever since. It is useless
to attack English umpires for the altered complexion put
upon Australian cricket. Two or three years ago we had
a couple of English elevens out here together, and each of
those teams played the Sixth Eleven on their native heath
and beat them decisively. Four or five years ago
Shrewsbury’s crowd came out here at the same time as the
returned Fifth Australian Eleven, and heat them, on the
Melbourne and Sydney grounds, in three successive
matches. Within seven years the writer has watched four
English teams play Victoria and “ Australia” on the Mel-
bourne C.O. ground, and has never yet had the satis-
faction of seeing the Britisher beaten, or nearly beaten.
Evidently Australian umpires must have favoured the
visitors. Let us put it down to the umpire.

Melbourne bookies are indulging in new luxury
small page-boys, resplendent in uniform and buttons,

Even the victory of Lord Hopetouu couldn’t
excite a “loyal” demonstration at Caulfield the other
Saturday, although the horse’s namesake was present in
the vice-regal box. Which suggests that the owner of
this very promising colt should take the hint and call it
Discarded Idol, or Forgotten Fiction.

The Worship of Backers —the Melbourne Cup.

It was last week stated, on respectable Chow
authority, that the Chinese lottery hanks in Victoria
scoop an average daily profit of £225 amongst them.
£70,000 a year absorbed by these leprous syndicates, while
Sunday-school children are contributing their unwilling
pennies towards the conversion of pore heathen !
9 * *
Lord Londesborough’s team, who start a match
(weather permitting) against the Australians to-day
(Sept. 4th), might better be described as “All England.”
With Shrewsbury in the place of Woods, the eleven would
seem the strongest that could have been chosen.
* * *
During a match with Denstone College, on the
Stafford County Cricket ground, a Mr. Ward hit the ball
into the Birmingham train. It was proposed to hire a
“ special” for its recovery, but the matter was eventually
compounded for 40 runs.
* •# *
Nickalls, the winner in three successive years of
the Wingfield Sculls-the English amateur championship
rowed from Putney to Mortlake—was beaten easily this
year, his right arm having given way, by Gardner, the
man whom he defeated for the Diamond Sculls at Henley.
* * #
Surrey holds the English county cricketing
championship for the year ’9O, Lancashire has second
place, Yorkshire and Kent tie for third, and Notts comes
next.

It is said that G-riffo is a blood-relation of cham-

# * *
’Frisco for some years has had the boxing boom
all to itself, but it looks as if the venue is to be changed to
Sydney. The Sydney Club have been treating their
members to some first-class contests for months past.
Vankee Matthews brings off a genuine contest every
Saturday night at his hall; and now the N.S. Wales
Olympic Club has started in the old Darlinghuvst Skating
Rink, with Larry Foley and Jack Molloy as boxing in-
structors. The latter club offer a purse of 200 sovs. for the
light-weights, Mace, Dawson and Burge—or any trio of
men—to battle for.
* * *
An English writer says that had he been em-
ployed to select the All England Eleven that encountered
Australia at Lord’s, not one of the amateurs included
would have been chosen. Grace, he says, is now paste,
Walter Read is no longer at his best, and M'Gregor, the
most useful cricketer that Cambridge University owns, a
clever wicket-keep, against Woods or Blackham is little
more than a child.
* , # *
Pettengell, the New Zealand boxer, made a poor
show with Jack Molloy, of Sydney, at Sam Matthew’s
Hall on Saturday night. When Pettengell met the chain-
pion amateur, George Seale, he was merely a chopping-
block for the clever amateur. On Saturday night Molloy
fought for “ the point ” and artistically knocked b'“ man
out early in the second round.

It is said that Dreadnought is going to England
after the Melbourne Cup meeting. His full brother,
Wentworth, went there with the last batch of youngsters 1
and it will be interesting to learn definitely how his and
his companions’ nominations stand in the face of the death
of their nominator.
# # #•
The first two to finish for the Sir Joseph Banks’
25th Handicap were James Tasker (33’> yards) and Thomas
Elliott (36J yards). It is said that Britisher Elliott has
only been on Australian soil since the beginning of the
year, and that the clever people at his back put him on a
baker’s cart at Wollongong ere bringing him out in several
“ Novice ” handicaps. However, the “ plant ” just missed
coming off, and the typical Queenslander, lean and long,
finished ahead of Elliott, a typical Britisher, round and
rosy.
* * *
Though the Harvard crew won the eight-oar
with Yale this year, they were by no means the champion
college crew, seeing that they, like Yale, persistently re-
fuse to meet Cornell University, “a farmer-crew from
the backwoods,” who won the inter-collegiate rowing
championship in ’75. Since then these Ithaca men have
only been defeated three times, their eight going under
once at Lake George, then to Philadelphia University at
Saratoga, and their four having been beaten on the
Thames. Cornell without doubt holds the eight and
four-oared amateur championships of America, although
Yale and Harvard think it politic to ignore them in their
annual contest.
* * *
The new English amateur walker, H. Curtis,
said to be the finest and fairest walker since the days of
Baby, and likely some day to do his eight miles inside the
hour, has beaten the Eiurlish amateur records at 5,6, and
7 miles at Aston, Birmingham.

M'Auliffe is favourite for the Slavin-M‘Auliffe
match at Ormonde Club on September 22.
* * *
__ The final match of the Australians with All
England, to have been played at Manchester, had to be
abandoned on account of rain. Our men have since de-
feated Staffordshire by an innings and 28 runs. The score
is now—Lost, 13 games ; won, 11; drawn, 8.
* * *
Psotta, the American amateur sculler, did a
manly thing in his heat for the Diamond Sculls at Henley.
The swivel of Kennedy’s right-hand scull opened and he
fell out of his boat, but Psotta, who had about £4OO on the
race, refused to row over the course, and waited until his
opponent was ready to start again. Kennedy beat him,
but the American received a document bearing tribute to
his manliness with the signatures of all the vowing digni-
taries, which, he said, made more than amends for the
beaten defeat.

The Surrey professionals are, as a class, entirely superior
to the paid men who some years ago worked for the county
and the public-house at the same time. Men like Lohmann
and Maurice Read are better gentlemen than very many
of our present-day amateurs.

* # *
The N.Z. steeplechaser Sir Maurice, of whom
we spoke last week as once a member of Bland Holt’s
company, used to come on in the sale act of “New
Babylon.” He was named after Sir Maurice O’Rorke,
Speaker of the N.Z. Assembly, was owned by a cabman,
and valued, as a discarded racehorse, at £2O. Now that
he has forsaken the stage and rehabilitated his racing
reputation, his present owner, the boss of a hash-foundry,
wouldn’t take, it is said, £5OO for him.
The most awful picture we have ever seen
is in Paris L’lli.cstration, by last mail, repre-
senting an actual scene of only a month or two ago,
when the police broke into a wretched Parisian
attic, and found a whole family (eight persons)
dead; they had suffocated themselves with char-
coal fumes under pressure of starvation. It tran-
spired afterwards that they put it to the vote, and
were unanimous for death.
In a case of overcrowding in a village near
Bridgewater, England, it was given in evidence
that the defendant, an old labourer, with his wife,
two daughters, and eleven illegitimate children
belonging to the daughters, were herded in a hovel,
containing one room 14ft. by 9ft., and 6ft. 6in.
high, another 12ft. by 9ft., and a garret above,
reached by a ladder. There was no ceiling to one
of the rooms, and no casements to any of the win-
dows ; and the only water supply was from a
neighbouring ditch, into which the sewage of the
premises ran. Such is life in some of our country
villages, “where health and plenty cheer the
labouring swain.” —London Truth.
Sir Geo. Bowen writes that the captain of
the Kearsage told him that Capt. Semmes, of
the Alabama, could easily have been captured, as
he floated away on a spar; but the Kearsage’s
captain did not want to take Semmes to the
States, to be hanged. This leads a correspondent
of the London Spectator to publish the record
of a long conversation he had with Mr. Lancaster,
of the yacht Deerhound, which saved Semmes,
and 40 others. It was a little nine-year-old girl of
Mr. Lancaster’s who caused the Deerhound to go
out of Cherbourg. Lancaster had all his family
on board ; but Mrs. Lancaster, and the minority,
didn’t want to go because it was Sunday, and the
numbers were equal, till the little giiT plumped
for the fight. When they got there Mrs. Lan-
caster became so excited that she exclaimed, “Go
in a little closer!”

and mental vigor.

a personal First Cause, simply declares that such a thing
has not been made evident - nor is its necessity apparent.
(3) Huxley invented the term “ agnostic ” to describe his
mental attitude towards religion. (4) The Bulletin
