What’s the difference between a special
constable and the system under which he’s let
loose? One is sworn in, the other’s worn out.
Duncan Gillies, the Victorian Premier,
has a vast acreage of wrinkles on an open counten-
ance bordered with saffron herbage. When he
exposes all his facial furrows, pouts out hjs in-
carnadine lips, and lets all the reefs out of his
nether and sub-nether chins, you know he is
smiling. Poets then call him “ wreathed in
smiles.” And that condition of Gillies is always
a sign that there’s going to be a political cyclone.
The successful forgery of a £SOOO-cheque
on a Melbourne bank recalls how neatly an astute
Maitland manager was ‘ ‘ had ” a few years ago.
A sleepy-looking bushman entered the bank with
a £9OO cheque. “ How will ye ’ave it?” “ Short!”
And short it was. Three days afterwards it was
discovered that the cheque had been altered from
nine to nine hundred. The quiet-looking bush-
man, who had meanwhile been painting the town
red, was arrested, and on him was found £6OO of
the money. He had been “lambed” to the tune
of £3OO.
Messrs. G. H. Smith and Son, of 302
George-street, the well-known hatters of this city,
have been made sole agents for Henry Heath, the
celebrated London hatter. The following is taken
from The Lady, an English fashion paper :
Mr. Henry Heath manufactures all his own silk felt hats,
together with those of many other hatters, and in addition
to inspecting his show-rooms, I made a tour through the
manufacturing and store department. The number of
hats in the latter department strikes any ordinary observer
with astonishment. The whole of the basement, in addi-
tion to several other large rooms, were all crowded with
shelves, filled with hats from floor to ceiling. The ship-
ping department is kept distinct from the rest of the
house, and, judging by the samples of the linings, Mr.
Heath’s foreign trade must be an enormous one.
The Victoria Market, Melbourne, is partly
built over the old cemetery. In sinking a post-
hole, lately, they found a skeleton in a red shirt,
with a rope round its neck; but whether suicided,
lynched, or hanged legally, nobody knew. Under
the market pavement-flags are about a thousand
bodies awaiting the Resurrection, while over them
goes on the chaffering over cabbages, turn-ups,
tomatoes, spring inyuns, butter, and strawberries.
The caretaker's ducks play over a lot of old flat
tombstones, inscribed in Hebrew, for everlasting
remembrance. This cemetery is rapidly becoming
a mere bare field, and in a few years there will be
scarce a remnant to show that there the rude fore-
fathers of the hamlet sleep.
COWBOY’S TOOTHACHE
Facing a Dentist Foolishly. How Suffering
Could Have Been Avoided.
Col. Metcalf, of Denver, Colo., U.S.A., was
once a cowboy, and tells a good story. ‘ ‘ Tin
winter of 1831,” he says, “ was a hard one, and I
never saw anything like the rain that spring.
When June came we were truly a drowned-out
set. I never was so worn out. I made up my
mind to go to Denver. I hit upon the toothache
for an excuse. My employer was at the home
ranch far away, as I thought; and so having
pressed my excuse on the boys I started. 1 got
to Denver in the morning. After breakfast I
lighted a cigar and stood on the steps of the hotel)
and standing there came face to face with my em-
ployer. I whipped out my handkerchief, and
whipped it up to my cheek, ‘Toothache—terrible,’
I muttered. ‘ Toothache, eh !’ ho said, looking at
me keenly, ‘come with me and have it out.’ I
hadn’t the courage to say a word, and followed
him meekly round to the office of the dentist. I
sat down in the chair in a state of utter collapse,
pointed out a back tooth, and groaned ‘Pull it.’
The dentist had hard work to get that tooth out,
for it was one of the best teeth a man ever had. I
had to pay two dollars for the operation too, and
when the tooth was out, and the dentist had
soothed my wounded jaw, my employer took me
down to the station and saw me off on the next
train for Kit Carson, bound for the camp. I
never dared to tell any of the boys how I got
beaten at ray game.”
To lose a good, sound. molar in that sort of
fashion was simply stupid cowardice. Any sort
of quick-witted man would have spoken of St.
Jacobs Oil, which everybody knows to be an
absolute cure for the toothache, and for all kinds
or any kind of ache or pain. How easy then, if
he must fib, to say that he had come to town to
get the great remedy, and he would have been
believed. Thousands testify to its efficacy, but
one or two instances will suffice to prove it. The
Hon. Billa Flint, Life Senator, Parliament,
Dominion of Canada, says: “It acted like a
charm in the cure of face neuralgia arising from a
defective tooth.” Mr. Edward Walters, 87
Bourke-street West, Melbourne, writes, “That
members of his family have used it most effectually
for toothache.” Mr. H. H. Hoermance, Lawrence-
burgh, Ind., U.S.A., August 17, 1888, states that
he suffered with toothache for two days. “1
tried,” he says, “ St. Jacobs Oil, and it cured me
immediately, and there has been no return of
pain.” A most remarkable cure was that of Mr.
Samuel B. Shiplor, Paragon, Ind., July 30, 1888.
“I suffered,’ he says, “with pain in my head
from sunstroke for twenty-two years, and was
cured by St. Jacobs Oil; no return of pain in four
years. ”
A PRACTICAL OPINION.
THE AGENT OF THE WATERBURY
WATCH COMPANY.
Sir, —As I have carried a Waterbury Watch for
several years, and it has proven a most reliable
timepiece, I trust it will not be out of place for
me to express my opinion concerning the well-
known watches. I am a teacher, and could not
satisfactorily use a watch which could not be de-
pended upon for correct time and durability. 1
have used at different times several other watches
of different manufacture ; but have frequently re-
turned them for the best of reasons—they would
not run. Finally, my father (A. O.
jeweller, of Chateaugay, N.Y.) obtained for me a
Waterbury, “series J” (short wind), saying he
knew from experience that would give satisfaction
in every way, and it has. The watch has not had
the best of care, I confess; yet it is, without ex-
ception, the best timepiece I have seen. I have
no desire to exchange it for any other. * * *
Very sincerely,
Metta E. Prescott,
Chernbusco, Clinton Co., N.Y.
April 29, 1880.
AT A TRIFLING COST
you can obtain a Waterbury watch, which rarely
gets out of order, and never constitutes itself a
permanent tax on the owner. In the event of
injury befalling it, by taking or sending it to the
Company’s Repair Depot, 50 Margaret-street, it
can be efficiently repaired at a nominal cost, rang-
ing, according to the extent of damage, from 6d.
to 2s. Cd., but never exceeding the latter amount
except by mutual consent. The genuine Water-
bury may be obtained from any respectable store-
keeper or dealer. Price, series “ E ” (the old
favourite) 13s. 6d.; series “J” or “L” (the new
marvels), enamelled dials, 22s. Gd. each.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under : —
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-heard.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
S. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
It. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be pref'erred,
A ll Contributions must he Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of 4 the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name find address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped, and, addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

Cornwall, January, 18S>0.
Dear Sir,—l write to inform you that your-
“ Allcock’s Porous Plasters ” are excellent—a real
blessing. Whatever I may say, I feel 1 cannot
praise them sufficiently. I have given away
hundreds amongst the poor and suffering, and I
cannot recall a single instance where they failed
to give relief. They have been used by me for
complaints in the chest, liver, kidneys, and spine,
with marked results, in fact, I know of three
special oases out of many (one of chest, one of
liver, one of kidneys) where the sufferers had been
to the infirmary, tried many doctors, without any
benefit, and were told that nothing more could be
done. I gave them your plasters, and after a fair
trial of them the result was really wonderful.
The sufferer in the chest no longer complains of
weakness, has lost all distressing cough, and is
quite strong. The sufferer from liver complaint
no longer complains of indigestion and pains in
the side and between the shoulders, but is quite a
different person. The sufferer from kidney com-
plaint no longer lies in bed nine months in the
year in agonies, but is able to get out of doors
every day, and is free from pain. I shall recom-
mend these plasters wherever I go.—With all
good wishes for the New Year, yours very grate-
James Arthur Kemp, Minister.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888,
To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq. .
Dear Sir,---This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took tt great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. _ At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever. KEENAN
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, m the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smvthb, J.P.

THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
Th® Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literarj
Journal, is the only paper which has a con-
sistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
TH® Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form—printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
of national progress: it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege—the direct cause of
half the misery which now afflicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised, The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the peari-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more than a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The

able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official—that, of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
Dther paper, because its vast mass of original and
apecially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

“ The malign visage of the employer,'
said a socialist, amid the gathering at Melbourne
Athenaeum the other night.
“ Let there be light,” is the motto printed
on the back of Stanley’s “Darkest Africa.”
Stanley means, of course, “Let daylight be put
into the nigger.”
Mr. Bray, a Sydney naturalist, appears to
have proved that some Australian venomous
snakes have more than two poison-fangs. Thus
evaporates another cherished article of faith.
The other day the following advertisement
appeared in an Australian morning paper : —
TXT ANTED, smart Man and Wife. Man as Coach-
’» man, Groom, Gardener, and generally useful,
must have own livery and be good whip ; Wife as
General and good Plain Cook, for private family.
Apply &c.
[Copy of Application.]
Mr. ,
Street West, Melbourne.
Sir, —In answer to advertisement, I beg to apply for the
situation.
Am 35 years of age, Cft. 1 Jin. in height, dark com-
plexion, good-looking. Have been used to horses, cattle,
etc., from infancy; am first-class horse-breaker; have
driven for years six-horse coach on the roughest part of
the Rocky Mountains, America ; some years circus-rider,
acrobat, etc., with Cooper and BaileyVcircus. Am “ Al”
harness-maker, can shoe a horse equal to any blacksmith,
good coach-painter and trimmer, also wheelwright. As
gardener, am member of Botanical Society. London prize
taken for choice collection of flowers at the Crystal Palace
Show. Have several certificates, medals, etc., from
various pastoral and agricultural societies in the
colonies for varieties of vegetables, etc. Can cut
wood, milk, thorough expert in poultry ■ raising.
Do any odd jobs in the way of carpentering,
plumbing and gasfitting, repolishing furniture, paper-
hanging, upholstering, &o., &c. Was at one time pro-
prietor of first-class comedy company, and having re-
tained a most expensive wardrobe, can supply liveries for
coachmen, in various shades, from bright-scarlet to sombre
black. Can also attire as German Prince, North American
Indian, Spanish Count, Esquimaux Seal Hunter, Italian
Brigand, Samoan Native, &c., &c , at a few moments’
notice, as occasion may require. My wife holds diplomas
from French, Italian and London schools of cookery, is
first-class clear-starcher and ironer, has studied dress-
making and millinery under first-class Parisian artistes,
is an accomplished pianiste and vocalist (contralto),
understands dairy management, making of butter,
cheese, &c., &c.; can in spare time teach children (if any)
English, French, German, Italian, Russian, Dutch, or
Spanish, painting in oils and watercolours, fancy needle-
work of all descriptions ; has own sewing and knitting-
machines. Is first-class nurse, and holds English and
colonial certificates in practical midwifery. Having
enumerated these few accomplishments of wife and self, I
hope to receive a favourable reply ; wages no object. We
are both staunch teetotallers or blazing razzle-dazzlers,
as occasion may require. Religion, wherever the carriage
drives.—We are, sir, yours most obediently,
Theodore Vespasian St. John Courtenay and Wife.
P.S.—As you will see by this document, written by self
with “Caligraph” typewriter, am proficient in the use of
the same. Am also an expert shorthand-writer.
P.P.S.—Practical electrician, can erect and maintain
signalling and lighting apparatus. References from Lord
Caringtdn, the Right Rev. Bishop Moorhouse, Sir W. J.
Clarke, Bart., and the late Archbishop Goold.
The Professional and Literary men of London

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

A writer in a West Australian newspaper
says:—“Whatever be the cause, it is a fact teat
in years gone by the officers of the Crown in this
colony secured a larger proportion of convictions
to trials than is obtained in these times.” T his
recalls a story of the early days of West Australia
which, taken in the ab uno disce omnes sense, will
tend to explain the cause of the large pro-
portion of convictions referred to. In one of
the country districts of the Swan River Settle-
ment, late in the fifties, the scales of justice
were held by an old thick-brogued gentleman,
with a keen eye to business. For performing the
duties of magistrate he received a fixed salary,
but he had an excellent private income from a
timber-mill, which he owned, as well. He used to
hold court once a fortnight. Sometimes a shep-
herd’s hut would serve as the Temple of J ustice;
at other times the majesty of the law would be
vindicated under the shade of a wattle-tree ; and
not unfrequently, in the winter time, could be seen
the impressive spectacle of the prisoners on one
side of a river, and the magistrate on the other,
passing sentence on them. The following is an
illustration of the dialogues which generally took
place between the Beak and the accused :—
Magistrate: “Robert Johnson, what are yeh
afther doin'? ” Johnson : “I ’ad only a drop too
much of tanglefoot, yer Wuship. I got it from
yer own men.” Magistrate: “Soilence in the
coort, Johnson, or OiTl put yeh where the
pigs won’t root in the dooer on yeh. Shu re
it’s meself that knows yeh, Johnson, and the likes
o’ yeh, yeh thavin’ villian ; ye’r too lazy to work,
and it’s meself that offered yeh tin shillin’s a
month and yer boord only a week ago, yeh good-
for-nothin’ scoundrel. Ye’r brought here by a
galoot of a peeler charged wid bein’ drunk, when
it’s thavin’ yeh ought to be charged wid ; for Oi
saw yeh wid me own two oise prowlin’ about shep-
herd’s huts for the last fortnit: and Oi now give
yeh six months’ hard labour on suspicion of com-
mittin’ a robbery. And go and do it at my timber-
mill, yeh limb o’ Satan.” No wonder the W. A.
officers of the Crown used to secure a larger pro-
portion of convictions than is now obtained.

Somebody told Sir William Robinson lately
a story about a N.S.W. Western celebrity who
took a couple of newspaper-men to see a splendid
monument he had erected to the memory of his
second wife, and the foundation-stone of which,
he calmly explained, had been laid by his third
and still living spouse. On hearing this tough but
true yarn, Sir William observed that “ the only
thing needed to finish-off that story was a five-
pound note and a letter of condolence from Lord
Carington. ”


Use Batho's Baking Powder.
The elections in Western Australia, under
the new Constitution, will probably take place in
December. There are 30 single electorates. Owing
to the restricted franchise, the new House will be
very similar to the old Council, all the old menu
bers, almost without exception, being certain of
their seats. Stephen Henry Parker, a native ot
the colony, will be W. A.’s first constitutional
Premier.

846 Maoquarle-street North, Sydney, September 3, 1890.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
A lady at the recent Women’s Fund hall had rather an
unpleasant “ find ” in one of the galleries during the even-
ing. She was being led to dance, when her satin-shod foot
kicked against an obstacle which, when rescued, proved
to be an artificial shoulder-blade ! It was eventually
restored to the owner, who was dissolved in tears in the
dressing-room, hut who really courted the mishap. Why
a lady thus renovated should wear a Y-shaped corsage
and so give her anatomy the chance to escape isn’t
apparent. But it always would slip right off, since there
is nobody living brave enough to step up to a woman with
the remark, “ I beg your pardon, madam, but your
shoulder-blade is coming unpinned.” Now, is there ?
And, speaking of stepping up to people that is
generally what the incorrigible small brother calls “ a
mug’s game.” A woman told another woman at the
theatre one evening that her shoulder-strap was slipping,
and met with the angry response: “ And what is that to
you, pray?” The retorts lady No. 1 could have made but
didn’t almost tempt me to make one for her.
The benefit concert in aid of Sydney City Mission,
engineered by Miss Naylor, assisted by Miss Colborne
Baber, Kowalski, Lemmone, Allpress, Gehde, and other
musical lights, was held in Centenary Hall, on 29th ult.,
and was a distinct success. There was a large and well-
dressed house, and several handsfull of garden-produce
were hurled at the ladies. Colhorne-Baber (appearing
again in Sydney after a silence of a year) sang well in the
dainty “Bel Raggio,” and for encore gave “Way down
upon the Swan-ee-ee,” &c. —fortunately not in the
vernacular. Lemmone, of the dark and liquid eye, tootled
seraphioally, and Kowalski alone actively responded to the
second encore, accorded the duet “ Bohemienne,” played
with much brilliancy by Miss Naylor and the enthusiastic
Frenchman.
It would appear as if Bach’s music did not agree with
“the Biggest Organ in the World.” Recently the great
instrument gave a long, wheezing, hellowy sound, and
subsided in the middle of a Bach passage, and the other
afternoon, also during Bach, it became apparent that the
organ had a stitch in its vox hmnana, for presently, with
a windy snort, it “ struck” in the middle of the movement,
and sulked for an hour by Shrewsbury clock. All things
considered, a taste for organ-music isn’t easy of cultiva-
tion in Sydney, and it is rarely that Mayor Burdekin (who
must surely attend the Town Hall for the purpose) hasn’t to
advance and tell the crowd that our pet’s lungs have
gone wrong. Mr. Best doesn’t enjoy unfailing health,
and what with the capriciousness of the organ, and the
Sims-Reeves-like uncertainty of the talented musician,
the person who buys a ticket for an organ orgy must be
prepared for the worst.
Sydney crSohe or day-nursery, at 21 Maoquarie-street
South, was to be opened without any gubernatorial gush
on Ist inst. The reasons for the vice-regal withdrawal
are well understood. Whether because of its fall from
“ society ” grace or because the proposed fee really was
exorbitant, the creche has reduced its charge from 6d. to
4d. per diem.
Quite a posse of spectators, on rows of chairs placed in
the open air, assembled at Sydney Prince Alfred Hospital
on 27th ult. to assist at the laying of the foundation-stone
of the new Home for the nurses, by Lady Carington,
attended by Lord Carington, Lady Hamilton and Captain
Trench. But the chief item of the programme was the
unveiling of a bust (by Sir John Steele) of that nurse of
nurses, Miss Nightingale, which Lady Carington scientifi-
cally denuded of its bagging after a few biographical re-
marks from Sir Alfred Roberts. A procession of nurses in
grey and lilac gowns, each placing a posie of tributory
violets at the base of the bust was sentimental and pretty,
and the warbling of “Home! Sweet Home” by St-
Cecilia’s Choir would have given an almost tearful turn to
the proceedings if it had not appeared that the choristers
had left their breaths in their luncheon-baskets. For the
arduous task of gazing fixedly at the foundation-stone
while a son of toil heaved it into place, Lady Carington
(who carried a charming committee-bouquet) was pre-
sented with a handsome paper-knife and paper-weight in
the form of a silver mallet and trowel, the handle of the
former being the figure of a nurse in solid silver.
At the laying of the Sydney Nurses’ Home foundation,
stone. Governor Carington amiably remarked that “ The
young gentlemen of the colonies would not allow the
nurses to grow old in their avocations, but took them
away.” It is currently reported that another foundation-
stone will have to be ordered, owing to the number of
ladies now desirous of enrolling as hospital nurses.
It is Sir Alfred Roberts’ opinion that “ there are nurses
in N.S.W. equal to any in Europe, and that many amongst
them would meet any emergency with efficiency.” There
is one emergency to which all the ministering angels,
without exception, are equal. That is when the bachelor
medico with the waxed moustache squeezes her hand
across the operating table, or surreptitiously hugs her in
t he dead-house.
A novel feature at the second series of orchestral con-
certs, commenced at the Centennial Hall on 30th nit.,
was that the daughters of one of the secretaries acted as
usheresses and were indefatigable in showing people
where they belonged, both upstairs and downstairs,
thereby making things very easy for that portion of the
audience which had booked its resting-place beforehand.
There was a large attendance; headed by Mayor and
Mrs. Burdekin, in a sage-green gown, the toque hat
relieved with touches of red, but there was no dress,
ing worth comment—the same absence of style which
characterised the Otton series being very noticeable.
One gets so accustomed to sidling through a dense mass
of humanity at Sydney Royal, during comic opera seasons-
that repeating the experiment, on 30th ult., at the
redivivus of “ Boccaccio ” didn’t excite yours ever to even
the smallest extent. There were lots of society people
dotted about the house, though owing to the Mayoress’s
post-organ recital supper-party, a few “regulars” were
missing. Wigs and legs are vanity; but quail-on-toast is
an enduring thing.
Sydney Bicycle Club sports at the Association Ground
on 30th ult. were well patronised everywhere but in the
ladies’ reserve. The concourse there mostly consisted of
the suburban girl in her Sunday frock, and among the
garments one looked twice at were a heliotrope cashmere
mingled with a mauve chow-oheok and pansy toque, a
cream delaine figured in mauve, hat in violets, mignonette
and wax roses, a flecked putty-coloured tweed topped by
t four-little-hlackbirds-haked-in-a-pie sort of hat; and a
dawn-grey combined in a Brown-Potterish manner with
black velvet, black felt hat and feathers. The races were
of absorbing interest, and the movements of a man in a
tall, black, glossy hat, who rode recklessly about on top
of a spidery hike, were breathlessly followed.
Wail of a lonely soul per a Sydney newspaper :
I\/rATRIMONIAL. —Lady, 40, first-class Villa,
LVL grounds, all appointments requisite, freehold,
income £3OO, desires introduction gent., good social
position, income not less £5OO year; references ex-
changed. Genuine, P. 0., Park-st.
“All appointments requisite ” is a neat way of alluding to
the chairs and tables and things without which matrimony
is a hollow snare, and connuhiality a vain thing. Which
reminds me that, despite its air of comfort, not to say
luxury, this advt. has appeared three times, while the
two-line remark of a young lady who wanted to “ view
matrimony ” at the age of 23 appears hut a fitful onoe>
having apparently evoked an offer. ’Twas ever thus.
The woman of 40, with “all requisite appointments,’
hasn’t a chance beside a blue-eyed juvenile with one frock
to her hack.
The Women’s Fund ball, on 26th ult.—the first festivity
of the kind held in the completed Centennial Hall-
attracted a large and varied assemblage; over one
thousand people were present, and every society person
who could attend was there. Lord and Lady Carington,
who brought with them Lady Hamilton and Miss Walenn,
stayed to a late hour and took much interest in the ball,
for the success of which Lady Carington had worked like
a Trojan, as president of the fund benefited. Music,
supper, and floor (it was rather humpy-up, owing to the
prevalence of midshipmen, %ho apparently regard their
partners as marlin-spikes) were good, and a programme
of twenty dances was formally opened by a quadrille in
which Lord Carington danced with Lady Hamilton and
Lady Carington’s partner was Sir Frederick Darley.
The dressing was remarkably good. Lady Carington
was fairy-like in a herthe bodice of white satin striped
brocade, with skirt of white gauze striped with silver, a
garland of blush roses coming from the left shoulder
on to the skirt and ending in a plaque of roses
on the right side, with white aigrette on the
left shoulder, necklace, bracelets, and tiara of dia-
monds, white shoes and Suede gloves, the bouquet
“presented,” &c., &c., of pink cream and white with
ferns and streamers. Lady Hamilton was rather plainly
attired in a trained gown of crushed strawberry and
ivory brocaded silk over a jupe and bodice-front of white
chiffon, and wore no jewels at all, while the dressing of
the vice-regal looks suggested a strike on the part of the
vice-regal maid ; Miss Walenn wore pale-grey brocade and
Valenciennes lace ; Mdlle. Roderiguez appeared in yellow,
and Mrs. Burdekin in pale grey-blue and sunset-pink
silk with wide stripes florally designed, draperies of
pleated sunset-pink crepe, diamond necklace ; the Misses
Cox wore black with silk-headed bodices and skirts o*
black Russian net relieved in the one instance with
yellow and in the other with cardinal, hair d la chignon ;
Miss Nellie Hill, eau-de-nil and salmon pink crepe and
silk ; Lilian Seccombe, black lace and silk relieved with
yellow roses ; Marian Burton, white satin and clusters of
pink roses, the plain skirt front designed in gold and
pink tinsel thread ; Frances Saville, white corded silk and
chiffon ; Miss Burdett-Smith, pale-blue tulle with diamond
star and necklet—and so on ad infinitum ,*Homo of the
floral dresses coming out splendidly indeed. A white tulle
with the water-fall pelted with white daisies was
deliciously fresh and pretty, and Miss Halliday’s pale-pink
crepe, front studded with tiny pink and green flowerets,
was a most admirable costume.
The wisdom of economy in connection with charity balls
is too obvious for comment, and if gingerbeer and buna
were the refreshments provided, I’m sure nobody would
audibly cavil. But a bad light in the dressing-room is a
thing no woman, however charitably disposed, can for-
give, and the lighting in the “repairing-room” at the
Women’s Fund ball made many a butterfly regret that she
didn’t carry matches. A twilight dressing-room is really
a very treacherous institution. The effect of the shadowy
looking-glass was cruelly apparent in the glare of tlu
ball-room, one lady having wiped the rose-de-beauth off of
one cheek while the other resembled a “ mulberry
sprinkled with meal,” and a long, disconsolate-looking
lock of false tress bristling with hairpins which lay perdu
on the floor of the ball-room (and which, oddly enough,
nobody ever claimed) doubtless owed its easy detachment
to the subdued half-light.
And another mistake in connection with the ladies’-
room at the Fund ball was that it was yards up a corridor
down which a stream of peeled people in all their glory
met an incoming tide of spectres arriving in ulsters and
clouds and every other abomination that women bundle-
up in after sunset. Wrapping-up suits a woman in pro-
portion to the glories of her outer-covering, but to he met
in an ulster of last year’s check, with her gown pinned
up round her knees, making her five feet in diameter, and
carrying her floral adornments in a paper-bag, doesn’t
put a woman in a good temper with herself exactly
Then the arrangement was that ladies met their escorts
“ in the ball-room,” and as the ordinary she doesn’t fancy
beating her way through a festive crowd without the
moral support of a dress-suit in her vicinity, lots of
muddle resulted—men waiting patiently in the ball-room
and decolette females with anguish on their brows
dodging flatteringly about the corridors.
What rational parents mean by allowing young girls to
cavort at Bands of Hope and things of the sort, standing
on platforms before crowded houses, and screeching,
thumping and declaiming with the aplomb of profes-
sionals, is a mystery. A local mamma, addicted to making
a public character of her daughter, with a view to induc-
ing hopeless drunkards to take the pledge for the
seventeenth time, or else buy socks for the heathen or
something, was brought up with a short, sharp shock
recently. There was a sacred spree of some sort on in the
tabernacle, at which Jane was going to sing, and as
mamma came softly in to superintend the toilet, Jane
observed laying on the carmine like a coryphee. The
true explanation, “I really want it, stuck up on the plat-
form, ma,” didn’t suffice, and now the Hope of the
Heathen has to bury her talents at afternoon Sunday-
schools.
Yours affectionately;
SAPPHO SMITH.
All preliminaries for establishing the Sydney Gover-
ness’ Home are now arranged. The fee for board and
lodging at the Home will be from 18s to £1 per week—
double-rooms, 14s each occupant—which includes free
registration and, in fact, everything hut washing.
Associates may join the Home for 5s per annum, for which
low figure they may treat it as a club where they may
write letters, rest, wait, &e., &c. There will be a matron
at £3O per annum, a housekeeper and a charwoman, and
with a very strong committee and the prevailing
Governor’s wife as president, the “Institoot” will have
quite a “ society ” interest. Subscriptions are only
required to set the ball a-rolling in the matter of furni-
ture, and the Home will not he ventured upon ere the
committee have a year’s rent in hand.
Sydney “Women’s Literary Society” is, in sporting
phrase, getting its second wind. A room has been secured
and rules drafted, and the rate of membership has been
fixed at the low figure of 10s. per annum, associates ss.
Miss Windeyer is the hon. secretary.

At Poverty Point.
They don’t think much of Cavmencita at the American
watering-place, Narragansett. Says the local critic: —
“ Carmencita is in no sense a surprise or a stimulant to
the inhabitant of this locality, whose regular life is filled
with visions of woman in her choicest guise, and whose
senses are never free from the champagne of her most
passionate moods. While the New Yorker, who has never
visited this neighbourhood, is vulnerable to the soft fleshy
snap of Carmencita’s slender body, the wise habitues of
this salt-blown paradise can be fanned into ardour only by
such magnificent artistry of the feminine shape as our
own ladies reveal so fairly. The Spanish dancer’s red and
rather insinuating mouth, her drowsy, languishing eyes,
her rollicking swing of arms and hips were at first re-
CARMENCITA
(The World-famous Danseuse).
garded with much favour, hut considerable adverse
criticism was heard concerning the length of the
dancer’s yellow satin dress, which reached quite to
the top of her slippers. This concealment of her limbs
was a grave mistake on the part of Carmencita. We are
a colony with strictly original ideas about beauty and its
uses, and when a lady is too prudish to warm the gaze of
man upon her knee-pan she is too good for our section of
the world ; however, as she began to move on the wings
of the music she lifted her feet, allowing the really valu-
able part of her to come curving out of the cloud of lace — •
and then the audience became calmer. But with all the
swift glimpses of this reasonably graoeful section of her-
self, and with her entire series of cerebral twists, Carmen-
cita did not approach, in point of maddening freedom of
body, any of our regular prize beauties, who have only to
tumble over a wave of medium size to show the perfect
plan of all that is feminine. By the side of them this
Spanish woman is no better than an iron hydrant. Vale,
Carmencita! Come again, when you have not so much
on ! ”
Playing Othello once, Barry Sullivan, while vainly
clamouring for the handkerchief made sacred by Des-
demona’s use, was met by the startling adjuration from
the gallery: “Nevermind the wipe, sir, blow yer nose
any’ow, and git on with the play.”

Wybert Reeve, now resident manager of Adelaide
Royal, is thinking of publishing his theatrical experiences.
Wybert can tell some interesting stories of the old days.
His great subject is Irving, to whom Wybert appears to
have been a sort of theatrical godfather.- .
A Swede, in Paris, emulated the flea-trainers with a lot-
of rats, which went through a hanging ceremony and
burial service, hut one day all was broken up through a
spectator throwing them a piece of bun. Hunger was the
only incentive. It reminds one of Parliament.
The original production, in Melbourne, of “Hero and
Leander,” suggests a review of the pieces which have first
seen the light in Australia. The works of Akhurst,
Marcus Clarke, Cooper, Darrell, Walch, and Whitworth
will be chiefly recalled. Bellew doesn’t disclose whether
“ Hero and Leander” is English, American, or Australian.

Australia was “The Gladiator,” an ambitious classic
affair, by Jaffray, a Melbourne journalist of yore.

Richmond Thatcher is said to have arrived in Adelaide
en route for London.
Wirth’s Circus arrives in Sydney by the Mariposa from
’Frisco. They bring a Wild West show.
Old Charlie Wheatleigh, who brought “The Colleen
Bawn ” and “ Shanghrann ” to Australia, actsChristophei
Sly in Daly’s American Company, London.
For the information of journalists in other cities who
continually confound the two, Sydney’s Centennial Hall
is municipal property and contains the big organ ; the
Centenary' Hall, a sacred building, belongs to the Wes-
leyan body'and is only occasionally devoted to the worship
of Mammon.
Navarro, the proud husband of Mary Anderson, is said
to be suffering from a severe cold. He hugged the ice-
berg and now he reaps bronchitis.
Madame Melba says that the Prince of Wales “ has been
the kindest of friends ” to her ever since she first “ came
out ”in England. Yes
It’s a rummy little,
Dummy little,
Tummy' little way',
It’s a funny little way he’s got.
She also declares that Tummy is “ the most tactful, in-
telligent, and courteous of men.”
G. K. Sims is said to have an income of £20,000 per
annum—all made by his pen.
J. C. Williamson advertises that he will not be respon-
sible for any debts contracted without his written
authority.
The great American actress, Ada Rehan, has electrified
London audiences as Rosalind in “As You Like It,” now
being played by the Daly Company, at the Lyceum. One
critic says; “She is the great Rosalind of our time, and I
don’t believe there has been a greater among all the
famous Rosalinds, from Peg Woffington to Mrs. Jordan,
and from Mrs. Jordan downwards.”
Lilian Tree, who is a pupil of Sir Charles Hall 6, intends
to give pianoforte recitals in Sydney soon.
Another member of the lately-dissolved Braoy company
—little Flora Graupner—has been attacked by diphtheria
in Melbourne. Is there anything in the vocally exercised
throat that renders it peculiarly susceptible to this
insidious disease, or do the theatre dressing-rooms of mal-
odorous Melbourne foster its germs ?
Johnny Solomon Is now in business as an auctioneer in
George-street, Sydney. Selling, the other day, after
crying out for at least ten minutes: “Now, gentlemen,
what will you hid me for this elegant article ? Any bid ?
Will anyone hid?” and receiving no response he said*
a little annoyed, “Well, you might hid me‘Good-day,’
anyhow.” “I’ll bid you good-bye and go,” said a
theatrical manager, whe el come in for a laugh, as he
quitted.
Hubert O’Grady, Irish comedian and dramatist, arrives
in Melbourne shortly. He opens at Melbourne Royal on
the 13th.
Martin Simonsen’s opera company opens the new Sydney
theatre with “ Fatinitza” on Boxing-night.
Marian Burton leaves for colder climes, we hear, on the
expiration of her six months’ term with the Nellie Stewart
company.
Emma Chambers is still running a company in South
Africa. So is Emelie Melville, assisted by Verdi.
Toole has enjoyed a pleasant Bendigo experience, very
different to that of his old friend, Charles Matthews, there.
Matthews raised prices, wherefore the curtain was rung up
to half-a-dozen people in the house, and the gay' Charles
refused to shake a leg. The worst sell known in that line
was Arthur Sketohley’s (Mrs. Brown) at the Melbourne
Athenaeum, when he came up, with much side on, to
lecture, but hardly a soul attended. Poor Arthur de-
parted with his plumes sadly draggled.
Drury Lane Theatre, 78 years old, is to be pulled down
and re-built.
Patti had the cheek this season to ask Gus Harris £BOO
for appearing one night in opera. He declined.
On the close of his Broken Hill season, Grattan Riggs
received a gift of jewellery and a testimonial through the
hands of the Mayor. Jennie Lee followed him on Saturday
night in “Jo.”
A little pleasant variety has been supplied to *\e popu-
lation of Kimberley (South Africa) by the circumstance
, that a travelling menagerie has broken loose, and the
town has been rushed by four lions, two lionesses, two
tigers, three bears, two wolves, one hyena, two cheetahs,
four jackasses, one elephant, three camels, and sixteen
baboons. Most of the inhabitants are camped on roofs,
or on the tops of clothes-props and telegraph-poles, and
. generally a refreshing oasis has appeared in the great
desert of everyday life. The residents are gradually getting
used to the new arrangements.
Boucioault is said to have taken the incidents for
“After Dark” from an ancient drama entitled the
“ Bohemians,” and it was a crib from “ Les Gamins.”
Recent revelations make it evident that the professional
claqueur is becoming a power in British theatrical matters.
The latest novelty in the ranks of musical prodigies is a
certain Signor Filippo Marto, of Naples, who plays the
cornet solely aided by the breath of nostrils. What a
snorer he must he ! Poor Signora Marto !
One of the finest collection of acting plays in Australia,
perhaps the finest, is owned by a journalist who, in his
own person, unites Church and Stage by dragging out a
miserable existence on the staff of a religious paper, while
solacing his soul by collecting and hoarding dramas. He
purchased the bulk of Harwood’s stock from Cole, and
has on his shelves Dick’s, Lacy’s, Duncomhe’s, French’s,
and Oxherry’s publications, besides copies of many
modern plays.
Miln leaves Melbourne tor London in a few weeks, with
the good wishes of all. His Richard 111. will give more
satisfaction than his Hamlet, which is preachy ; but Ins
Marc Antony would, perhaps, catch on best. He will
likely, though, have to act at the East End, for one can-
not see an openingfor him in tile West.

THE DOLL HOUSE-WIFE.
Among his books he sits all day
To think and read and write ;
He does not smell the new-mown hay
The roses red and white.
I walk among them all alone,
His silly, stupid wife ;
The world seems tasteless, dead and done
An empty thing is life.
At night his window casts a square
Of light upon the lawn ;
I sometimes walk and watch it there
Until the chill of dawn.
I have no brain to understand
The books he loves to read ;
I only have a heart and hand
He docs not seem to need.
He calls me “Child”—lays on my hair
Thin fingers, cold and mild ;
Oh ! God of Cove, who answers prayer,
I wish I were a child !
And no one sees and no one knows
(He least would know or see)
That ere love gathers next year’s rose
Heath will have gathered me.
And on my grave will bindweed pink
And round-faced daisies grow ;
He still will read and write and think.
And never, never know !

of missing his career, this way. In 1849, he got
the billet of Principal of a very orthodox Hobart
Town college, but just then his book on the
“Nemesis of Faith ” came out, and the authorities
took alarm.

Now that Cardinal Newman is dead we
trust that some steps will be taken to suppress
the old-established sham who always awakes on
such occasions to state that the longevity of the
deceased was entirely owing to his habit of early
vising. Whenever a prominent man dies at a
o-reat age some plausible liar is certain to get up
and affirm that the deceased habitually rose in
up middle of the night, and went out and caught
dW d e?ght e ho3 hard ffia^worfq
St breakfast on a biscuit and a spoonful of hot
water After which he accomplished five hours
nor of tough mental exertion, and didn’t take
® lunch, and then he laboured on till dinner-
I me and went to bed without having any
Inner at all, and got up again in the
middle of the night as before. In other
words, he was a miserable, hungry, ravening
skeleton for eightv years, and died at last without
ever having a comfortable sleep or a square meal
n ail his blighted existence, and if he attained the
aveof a century, it is generally added that he wore
hardly any clothing in the winter time and in-
variably slept with his feet out of the window,
ami walked 35 miles on his ninety-ninth birthday,
a d hadn’t turned a hair when he got home.
There are few earthly troubles so aggravating
aT those anecdotes of great men who lived
to an immense age because they never ate any-
ing or went to bed, and something requires to
"done to the next man who fires that hoary He

When Labouchere first stood for North-
Hampton his Radical reputation was sufficient to
semre him the support of the embryo Republi-
pans, who are pretty numerous in that city of St.
Crispin; but to place him in the desired position
at the poll it was essential to obtain as well the
“ vote and interest ” of the Dissenting fraternity,
How to arrange this was, in the face of his widely-
known atheistical tendencies, rather a poser,
Temporising with them proved of no avail, and he
was at last forced to consent to meet a deputation
of Chadbands, upon satisfying whom depended
his fate. A public meeting had been called for
eight o’clock on a certain evening, and it was ar-
™nged that he should receive the deputation man
adjoining room half an hour earlier. The evening
arrived and with it the gentlemen m black. Labby
had never been in such “ good’’company, or at
he decently could, but however profuse his
cordiality it could hardly be made to extend over
the whole of the two mauvaises quarts dheure.
Let Labby himself take up the running: An
idea struck me. I surveyed my would-be tor-
mentors, and selecting the one whom I took
to be the best stayer, said : ‘Gentlemen
this being an occasion fraught with the most
momentous issues, I think we cannot do better
Rian commence with prayer, and I call upon
Brother to offer up a petition.’ This met
with unanimous approval, and I saw made an im-
pressiom We all knelt and my Ohadband pro-
oeeded in a, to me, delightfully slow and measured
®tmm, I, throughout, emitting the appropriate
groans, in which I had to take my cue from my
companions my whole attention being centred on
the clock. Ten, fifteen, twenty minutes went, oh,
so slowly! but still he held on; another ten
minutes and I should be safe. The crowd m the
adjacent hall we could hear was getting more noisy
and impatient every minute. (I had taken care
teat they should be admitted very early). But,
alas, when it still wanted five minutes to the hour
my man failed me and arose, and with him the
rest. Having come so near victory it would have
been hard indeed if it had then eluded me. I
played my trump-card. I remained on my knees
wrestling in private prayer! When eight o’clock
struck I rose and made for the hall, followed by
the now thoroughly satisfied black-coated gentry,
whose presence on the platform carried the meet-
ing with me and gained me the seat.”
TJ® " ow , f amous . y°«?g. Franco-Russian
arfcl f and autobiographer, Mane Bashkirtseff, we
read > Y as remarkably shrewd in spots, and had
fnewise notion, which is strongly recommended
to the young women whose tears are watering the
land because they cannot get some ring or some
bangle. She never wore jewellery! She said that
diamonds and such thmgs were made for old
people; the young girl who wore a diamond was a
fool. N°thing about a young girl should be so
bri S ht and sparkling as that young girl s eye. Iso
y°«ng girl s eye of course, could hope to compete
Wl . fc l tke s P arkle °J a diamond. Therefore, she
Baid > sbe was no .t such an idiot as to put a
d ' amond where it could putsparkle her eye.
Her own words on the subject were: “That
the point lumineux (luminous point) about a
§ irl should be the pupil of her eye
Diamonds and such things were good for old
f(dks who wanted some sparkle about them, and
whose eyes could no longer supply it.” She
had a P™ 1 - °Pm'on of Worth, the dressmaker
as an artis -at east, so far as the making of
young girls clothes w 7 as concerned. She used to
say that he could make things fit only for broidery
down the back. She never wore bonnets, of
course; for unmarried women under thirty in
Paris never wear them. The bonnet is as much a
sign of marriage in France as having your two
middle upper front teeth knocked out is among
certain savage tribes. Toward the end, Marie
began to regret that she had not married; not
that she thought that she was dying, but because
with her fading beauty, admiration was less plen-
tiful. For some reason she got it into her head
that young men did not come trooping for her
hand because they did not believe her wealthy.
She ought to have taken the high ground of
scorning to attract anybody by her dowry.
But she did not, because, as a matter of fact,
the other thing is all right in France.
She said one day to her Aunt Romanoff : “ They
think that vve haven’t really got money, because
our property is away off in Russia and not right
here in France; but I will show them. ” And,
unbelievable as it may seem, she packed her Aunt
Romanoff off to her lawyers with instructions that
she faithfully carried out. She sold for cash a part
of her estate in Poltava and brought the money
back to the bouse in numerous bags of gold. Marie
lay enthroned in state in bed, and the gold was
poured out around her in a yellow stream, out-
lining her form on the white counterpane. She
played with it for awhile with her fingers, then
thin and weak, for it was only a short time before
she died, and her whim being satisfied, Aunt
Romanoff picked it up and probably carted it off
to the bank. Perhaps Marie was pleased at the
thought that she was like Danas, whom
she must have seen illustrated in the Salon,
visited by Jupiter in a golden shower. The
Duke of Hamilton, alluded to with such fre-
quency, pathos, and silliness by Marie in her love-
sick early days, is still alive, and is thus de-
scribed :~He is fat and yellow, with a red face,
but withal a very good type of an English duke.
His name alone is quite an imposing affair, and
no doubt Marie knew it by heart and repeated it
over and over with great satisfaction. Here it is ;
—Duke of Hamilton, Sir William-Alexander-
Louis-Stephen-Douglas-Hamilton, K. T., Mar-
quess of Douglas and Clydesdale, Earl of Angus,
Arran, Lanark, and Selkirk, Lord Hamilton,
Avon, Polmont, Maehanshire, Innerdale, Aber-
nethy, Jedburgh Forest, Dare, and Shortcleugh,
Duke of Brandon and Baron of Dutton, Premier
Peer of Scotland. He also claims to be Duke of
Ohatelherault under an imperial decree of Napo-
leon 111. The young woman who did marry this
very desirable duke, and whom Marie so violently
hated in consequence, without having any idea of
whom she was, was Lady Marie Montagu, the
oldest daughter of the Duke of Manchester,
and the sister of the former disreputable
Lord Mandeville, now duke. The paid
of a much-worshipped young man, which
he was made to play in Mane’s journal, has
caused the yellow Duke of Hamilton a great deal
of annoyance and unlimited chaffing. Marie, it
is true, speaks of him only as the Duke of H ,
but this is no protection for poor Hamilton, as he
is the only duke in the English peerage whose
name begins with an H. His friends are very
fond of chaffing him, and particularly of quoting
that passionate exclamation of the little Russian
at the age of 12 : “ Oh, my God ! give to me the
Duke of H . I will make him happy !’’ It is
possible, also, that the Lady Marie M ontagu may
have had some observations to make to his grace
about Marie’s journal. It will be remembered
that this ingenuous young Russian speaks very
frankly and jealously of the Duke of H ’s
mistress, and of the latter’s habit of eating cakes
publicly in the windows of that immoral lady’s
house. The fact that the Duke of Hamilton, as
he has frequently and tearfully put it, “ never saw
the little brat,” meaning our dignified friend Marie,
and therefore, of course, never knew what a
beautiful being he had stirred to admiration,
makes it all the harder for him to stand so much
chaffing now.

Tales of “The System.”
No. VIII.: Miss Latchford’s Overseer.
[OR THE BULLETIN.]
Sergeant Derby stood, in the sunlight of a
November afternoon, at the garden-gate of the
Turnut Police-station. He was watching a tired,
drooping figure, clothed in a shabby black-cloth
Suit, coming up the dusty Jugiong-road.
“ Labour on pass,” he said to himself.
The figure drew nearer and nearer till it stopped
before him ; and proved the accuracy of his con-
jecture by saluting, and then holding out to him a
bit of paper, partly printed and partly scrawled
over with many “fists” in various stages of
legibility.
“ Sydney? ” ejaculated the Sergeant as ho took
the “pass” and examined it. The convict (a
young man of 23) replied that he had come from
the city.
“Ah, you’ll be pretty well knocked up after
that. Here, sit down on that verandah-seat while I
check your pass.”
The pass-holder hesitated.
“Aren’t you tired?” said Derby. “Well, if
you are not, you must be a well-plucked ’un after
that tramp—and (glancing again at the pass) just
off a four-months’ voyage, too !”
“Yes, sir, I am tired, ” the convict replied, “but
the Regulation”
“What Regulation?” sharply interrupted the
officer.
“No. 18, sir”—and the convict repeated the
sub-section “ordering all prisoners to stand when
under interrogation.” He recited it mechanically
as though it were burnt into his brain. As,
indeed, and not to mince matters, it was. The
System took plenty of care that its subjects should
have their memories exercised, however it might
treat their stomachs and their souls. It gave them
the Regulations with their morning meal of brown-
bread, and the noonday soup, and the evening
hominy. Sometimes, in order to spiritualise the
souls by afflicting the stomachs, the System
stopped the meals, or reduced their quantity.
But, never by any chance, did it omit the Regula-
tions. If a prisoner had nothing to digest, he had
always the Regulations; and on Sundays a double
dose with the clean shirts.
»“ Bother the Regulations !” quoth the Sergeant.
“Here, I order you to sit down while I get you
something to eat. Got grub with jrou, I s’pose ?”
He turned over the pass and, looking at the last
of the endorsements, resumed: “You were last
rationed, I see, at the Pigeon Hole—and I know
what that means—a quarter short and sour bread.
Wonder what the Government means allowing
poor devils to be robbed !”
From these observations the acute reader will
infer that the Sergeant was not the most ardent
admirer of the System, and the acute reader will
not be far wrong. You see the Sergeant had been
trained in the school of Captain Charles Sturt;
and though his teacher s conduct in the Sudds and
Thompson’s episode is apparently indefensible
(according to our modern notions), yet Sturt was,
as a rule, as humane in his treatment of “Gov.-
men ” as he was with respect to the aborigines.
Here and there, the System developed the noblest
qualities in its administrators ; but the Franklins,
the Wilsons, the Maconochies, the Sturts, the
Naylors and theCri uohes —the men who interposed
an elevated humanity between the System and its
victims—are to be reckoned by units only, and not
tens. Because they were so few, the greater share
of honour theirs, and the larger portion of shame
others’. In the day that Australia counts her
heroes she will do these few men justice.
It was because Derby was one of Sturt’s men
that he treated John Denham, newly-arrived
transport per Ocean, No. 38,328, with an unob-
trusive kindliness that damped Denham’s burning
eyeballs with a tender dew that had been foreign
to them since the hour when his mother—and
another woman—bade him farewell in the corridor
of Gdtspur-street, Compter.
Denham was “labour on pass ” to the employer
to whom j 1 ® had been assigned. In twenty-mile
stages from Sydney, reporting himself at every
police-station he had passed, and getting a ration
of bread (as often as not short-weighted) at each
place, he had tramped his way under a November
sun to Tumut—the nearest station to his destina-
tion. Fifteen “ reports ’’had he made en route; as
many times had his pass been examined, the des-
cription therein compared with his person, and
endorsed with the particulars demanded by the
Regulation in that case made and provided. These
were ; The authorisation to proceed, the number
and class of rations issued, the date of inspection,
the sobriety or non-sobriety of the holder, and the
signature of the inspecting officer. Fifteen times
had this form been gone through, and on no occa-
sion had the inspecting officer had aught but a
stern or harsh word for the pass-holder. He had
been given his ration, sometimes with a curse by
way of a sweetener to the soured or mouldy loaf,
and sometimes with a look that lacerated his feel-
ings more than the curse. The Establishment did
not pay for kind words or pleasant smiles; rather,
on the contrary, was it inclined to reward their
opposites. Not at all astonishing was it, therefore,
that, when Sergeant Derby, a quarter-of-an-honr
later, called Denham into his neat office and bade
him, with a rugged sympathy in his voice, sit
down to the meal that lay spread on a snowy nap-
kin on a table in the corner, not at all surprising
was it, we say, that “No. thirty-eight, three, two,
eight ” gave one glance at the food, another at the
Sergeant (who “ made-believe ’’not to see it), and
then placed his arm against the door-post, bent his
head on it —and wept like a child.
“ Tut, tut, man !” exclaimed the Sergeant. “ Be
a man !” He got up from his desk and pulled-to
the door that led from the office to the sitting-
room of his quarters. He had caught the sound
of a sob from behind the door, and guessed that
his little wife was glancing through the interstice
at No. 38,328, and was weeping out of the pity of
her heart for the poor chap, who was affected into
tears by a little kindness. At least, that is the
reason that he gave his spouse when at tea-time
she asked why he had shut the door. As she
boxed his ears she told him he was fibbing, that he
closed it because he did not wish the pass-holder
to see that he was within a barleycorn’s length of
crying as well. From which faint glimpse of
domesticity the acute reader will please draw
another inference to the effect that Mrs, Derby
did not admire the System any more than did her
husband. We shall see, one time or another, a
good deal of little Mrs. Derby, and we shall only
bid her farewell at the side of the grave in which
she has just laid her dear, dead Sergeant, slaugh-
tered by Bushraneer Tallboy. But that hour is
not yet.
#
We should bo fibbing like the Sergeant
if we said that Convict Denham made a hearty
meal. He didn’t; he just crumbled a morsel of
the sweet, white loaf and touched it with an atom
of the golden butter, and, trying to wash it down
with a drink of the new milk, failed miserably;
but he felt as refreshed as though it were a
draught of strong wine that coursed through his
veins, and not merely a dron of the elixir of a
brother-man’s sympathy. And while he pre-
tended to enjoy the evidences of Mrs. D.’s house-
wifery, the Sergeant—gazing all the time out of
the doorway and not at No. 38,328—discoursed to
him mildly :
“Look here, Denham,” he said, “I’m going to
give you a word of advice. I think you’ll take it
properly, otherwise I wouldn’t offer it, but I see
you are—well, not far gone in crime. Yet,
and .”
Denham broke in, with emotion, stiffing his
utterance :
“No, indeed, sir, I broke the law once —and
I’ve been punished for it, and I won’t go any
deeper—if I can help it.”
“ Ah, that's it—if you can help it. But this
damned System won’t let many a man help it. It
throws the youngsters hi sin into the company of
the oldsters. And that’s what I want particularly
to say to you. Keep yourself to yourself, the old
hands will make your life a burden to yon at first
if you do. They will try and trap you, and then
put you away, but never mind that. Don’t put
on any airs with them, but keep them at a dis-
tance. Do that and keep out of mischief, and
while I'm here I’ll stand your friend. That’s be-
tween you and me, you see. Don’t say that to
others. But lam Assistant Muster-Master of this
district, and one way and another have got a deal
of power. As for Miss Latchford (that’s your
Mistress, you know) you’ll find her difficult to get
on with at first. She is terribly proud—why,
you’ll find out from herself, perhaps—but she lias
a rare head for management, and, on the whole, a
just head. Her chief fault is that she has no
heart - .
The Sergeant stopped here—and glanced around.
Something like a muttered remonstrance came
from behind the parlour door. If the tiny wife
and the big man had any subject of chronic
marital and fference, it was this question of Miss
Latch ford’s heartlessntjss.
The Sergeant went on—-
“At least, I say so, but my wife says she has.
Anyhow, she has a just and'a strong head, and
I’m sure that when she s e you are different from
other Gov’ment men about here, she will make
your lot easier than it would be with others.”.
There was no doubt about it this time. Both
the Sergeant and No. 38,328 heard it distinctly !
The voice behind the door exclaimed, in a tone
that was meant to be very, very ironical, “And
yet he says she ha no heart !”
The Sergeant laughed, and continued—
“ She, M'ss Latchford, I mean, has told me to
look oat for you, and what she wants you to do if
you are competent. You will be storekeeper and
bookkeeper. I expect she’ll let you have quarters in
the store-room, if you are steady and don’t give
trouble—but I don’t think you will. Do your
duty and both she and I will help you, I am sure
to get your ‘ticket’ when it’s due, am) it’s only
a matter of time then for yon to make money for
yourself. I shall see you at monthly musters, and
perhaps, once or twice in the month besides, and
you can let me know how you are getting on. Of
course, I’ll hear of you too, from Miss Latchford—
she don’t go much among he squatters, and my
wife and I are pretty well the only fr ends she has,
for all her money—and talk of the devil, here she
is!”
As he concluded, the Sergeant moved forward
to open the gate. Denham followed, and as the
officer returned, escorting a lady who held up j le ,(
serge riding-skirt with a hand that, though i arr , 1
and guileless of gloves, was shapely, took off
cap, and stood at attention —as per Regulation
# # #
Miss Latch ford, of Hereford Station— tlm
young woman who looked him up and down with
a steady gaze—was in the first bloom of a brilliant
and beautiful womanhood. Her beauty was of
that type that might coarsen with advancing
years, but would not wither. The tinge of vivid
colour implanted by _ a brisk gallop harmonised
effectively enough with the slight bronze which
was her cheeks’ natural hue, and to which the
southern sun, and the mountain breezes had been
so lenient as not to deepen, notwithstanding
that their owner spent most of her time out n't;
doors. An exquisitely-shaped upper-lip, and an
under-one that expressed decision no less em-
phatically than beauty, when they parted their
carminecl curves displayed regular teeth ( ,f
ivory pallor; her nose was, perhaps, a trifle
too large, but the delicate roundings of the
nostrils supplied a compensation; and superb
eyes, with iris of a true purple, looked out reso-
lutely—too resolutely, indeed—from between
long lashes and from beneath a forehead almost
masculine in its sweeping height and breadth of
outline. Blue-black hair was coiled massively
under the hat of dark-felt; and a foot peepcd-out
from under the looped skirt that no one—not even
a fashionable bootmaker —would have termed
small, but to which no one would deny the attri-
bute of a fine instep. Tall, with a iitheness of
movement, that gave an additional grace to bodily
lines which fell little short of an ideal perfection
and with a stately poise of the head which
betokened a strong, self-centred individuality that
had, notwithstanding, nothing in common with
selfish or vain dispositions. Such was Kate
Latchford. Where she got her good looks no one
could guess, though the topic was a constant
theme of district speculation. Her father had
had no characteristics of manly beauty; } le ,.
mother none of feminine. Perhaps in Kate some
century-gone strain of physical excellence revived
under the stimulating conditions of a glorious
climate, ample leisure, and ampler wealth.
Whether that was so or no, Denham, as he looked
at her, and whenever he looked at her afterwards,
compared her with the high-bred women whom
he had seen crossing West-End pavements and
sauntering in the Park. Something also ho
thought now of the haughty gaze of the aristo-
cratic dames and damsels was visible in those
stern yet beautiful eyes, which examined him
much as they might do a yearling colt. He
thought, too, that the Sergeant must be
right. She must lack heart. There was a
hardness in her glance that seemed to indicate a
character unsusceptible to the finer emotions.
Nature had keyed her, perhaps, to intense chords
from which a mighty pa-sion might smite a
thunderous and sombre music, but the subtler
filaments that breathe a note of sweetness when
touched by the velvety fingers of homely and
friendly affection—these, assuredly, were missing.
She addressed the convict in a dry, business-like
tone.
“You are John Denham, assigned to mo—Miss
Latchford, of Hereford Station?”
“Yes, madam,” replied No. 38,328.
“My agent, Mr. Slyde, the Police Magistrate,
