at Sydney, selected you at Carter’s Barracks, be*
cause you said you were a clerk. That is so? ”
“That is so, madam.”
“ You are competent to keep station-books, give
out st res, and so forth ?”
“ I think so.”
“You must be sure before I put you on. If I
put you to the work, and you can’t do it, I shall
bring you before the magistrates for punishment.
I speak plainly. The last man they sent me as a
clerk was a swell mobsman, lagged for twisting a
sparkler.* I gave him two-dozenf and sent him
back. If you can’t do the work, say so. You had
better go back at once without punishment, than
return with your back tickled.”
Upon Denham’s spirits, which had felt more
buoyant after the Sergeant’s speech than they had
ever done since the Law touched him on his
shoulder in distant London, fell a deep gloom.
What manner of woman was this who talked to
him with an unimpassioned, almost cynically,
brutal tone? Who used gaol-slang, spoke of flog-
gings which cut marks on the skin as indelibly as
the iron of the French executioner brands “F”
tm the ToulonForcat, as mere “ticklings?” Good
God ! was she a woman ? And this was to be his
mistress ! For a moment he wavered. Would it
not be better to say that he could not perform the
duties required of him and to tramp back to
Sydney, than put himself under the rule of this
she-wolf? Very likely he would have done so
but the remembrance of Carter’s Barracks with
their attendant horrors rose vividly to his mind,
and with it the thought—“ Better than these,
Anything 1” So he said simply :
“ I can do most things in clerical work.”
“For what were you lagged ?”
Under the fine dust with which the last stage of
his tramp had covered Denham’s face, Miss Latch-
ford perceived a sudden flush and then as sudden
a pallor. But she was relentless. Before he could
answer she pressed another question upon him :
“ And for how long?”
“ Seven years, and for embezzlement.”
“You are innocent, of course ?”
Again the sudden flushing and paling. Denham
had looked his interrogatrix in the face up to this
moment. Now he let his eye fall to the matting.
“No !” he said ; “I was guilty.”
For the first time the features of the lady ex-
pressed an interest in the man. The face of Ser-
geant Derby, too, put on an aspect of surprise. It
was quite anew thing in the experience of both
to come across a transport who, according to his
or her own account, had not been wrongly con-
victed ; and the implied recognition by Denham
of the nominal justice of his sentence conveyed to
them an instantaneous belief in his substantial
truthfulness and trustworthiness which neither,
possibly, would have admitted.
# # #
By way of completing her scrutiny, the
Mistress of Hereford Station lifted the pass, which
was lying on Derby’s desk, and audibly ran over
the recapitulation of Convict Denham’s personal
prison characteristics ;
Name, Denham, John; Ship, Ocean ; Register, IVO
oS, 8%8; Age, ‘23; Occupation, Clerk; Education, C;
Religion, C. of E. ; Height, sft.llm. ; Features, Oval ;
Eyes, Hazel ; Forehead, Broad; Hair, Black; Nose, Long;
Teeth, Regular, complete. Marks, Mole on nape of neck ;
General description, Gentlemanly Appearance ami speech,
might y e taken for a swell-mobber, but isn’t. (O sapient
observer 1) Beard and whiskers allowed, by grace of
Burgeon-Superintendent, for good conduct, anil helpful-
ness on voyage.
She checked each particular with her eyes, and
then said: “ I think you will do; but you must
shave. I allow no privileges till they are merited.
You can earn them in three months, if you like.
Can you ride ? ”
As an occasional Sunday trot on a livery-stable
hack was all the equestrian exercise in which
Denham had indulged when a free man; he
thought it best to reply in the negative.
“ Well, we’ll even teach you. The sooner you
begin the better. I will give you a note to the
Woolpack people to tell ’em to saddle you up the
young mare. ’ She sat down, and was scribbling off
the note when the Sergeant, who, knowing the
girl’s ways, had listened" in silence while she had
put her new assigned man on the rack, bent down
and whispered ; “If he can’t ride, Miss Kate,
that skittish thing’ll break his neck. She's nearly
all blood, you know, and not more than half
broken-in.”
Miss Latchford made her rejoinder in her usual,
tones, so that Convict Denham, if he would, need
not have failed to hear her or to understand her.
“ What’s the odds, if he does ? Perhaps it will
save the Government from buying a half-inch rope
for him.”
Denham passed through the pretty garden to
the Woolpack Inn, and resolved as he went that if
the young mare took it into Vat graceful head of
hers (that spoke so plainly of the Diomed straip in
her blood) to break his neck, he would not cross
her purpose.
# * #
While he was about his errand, the Ser-
geant said a few words to Miss Latchford.
“Kate, you are too hard. Mark you, that
young fellow is not one of the ordinary hands. I
know a man of sensitive nature when I see him,
and this chap is one. Treat him kindly; don’t
let him feel he’s a mere brute, to be ordered here
and flogged there, and he’ll do good work for you
and for the world yet, ”
“Have you done your sermon, Sergeant?”
asked Miss Latchford, coolly; “ because, if so, I
want to see Mrs. Derby. ”
“No, I haven’t. I spoke before as a friend.
Now I speak as an officer. I shall make it my
especial business to watch how you treat Denham,
and report to Sydney.”
“Bah! if you have Sturt behind your back, I
have Slyde. Besides, I know Act 5, George IV.,
as well as you do. I know how far I can go with-
out risking the withdrawal of my assigned labour
—if I wanted to drive a man to the devil I can do
so, and still keep within the four corners of the
Acts and the Regulations.”
‘ ‘ Miss Latchford, I believe you’re a Mudie in
petticoats !”
“Derby, how can you!” exclaimed a voice in the
parlour.
“Oh, you’re there, are you, Jeannie?”—and
Miss Latchford turned and held out her hand to
to the Sergeant’s wife. “ The Sergeant says I’m a
feminine Mudie. But you know I’m not, don’t
you, dear? You know I’m simply a woman with
a grievance against Society for making me an old
lag’s daughter. Besides, Mudie is a mere bungler
—he went beyond the four corners ; I keep within
them.”
“I don’t know which is the worse,” growled the
Sergeant.
“ Sergeant,” said the girl in reply to this doubt-
ful compliment, “don’t let’s quarrel. You’ve
been too good a friend to me—you and the little
woman here—for that. Perhaps I’m wr ng--per-
haps, as you say, lam hard-hearted. But I strive
to be just, all the same. I’ll be just to this new
man, but favour him I will not—not even to please
you, for there would be no holding in the other
hands then. You must know that 1 have a hard
task in the management of the Station as it is,”
“I don’t want you to favour him,” said the
Sergeant, partially mollified ; “I want you to be
just. And if you are you’ll treat him kindly.”
‘‘-the little woman” came forward and joined
their hands.
“ There, you big people,” she cried, “ shake !
And if I find you quarrelling again I’ll take it out
of both of you !”
I his awful threat terrified the disputants so
much that they instantly sealed amity with a
laugh.
» # #
Not to go too much into detail, we may say
that the young Diomed mare did not break the
neck of John Denham, No. 38,328, per Ocean,
either on the first time ho bestrode her, or on the
many subsequent occasions. He proved his com-
petency to do the work Miss Latchford required
at his hands, and more. First as clerk and store-
keeper, then as manager and confidential man, ho
served her, as faithfully as (perhaps more faithfully
than) any free man would have done.
The station was one of the largest in the South,
and included both mountain country and a rich
champaign, so that both cattle and sheep were
grazed successfully. Old Latchford, Kate’s
father, had been one of Gov. Macquarie’s “ eman-
cipist _ petsand by thrift, hard work, and an
adaptiveness of faculty and fertility of resource,
rare even among British colonists, had delved and
bred a small fortune out of his 500-acre grant on
the Hawkesbury. With that small fortune at his
command, he had been one of the first to follow in
Hume and Morell’s footsteps as they trended to
the luxuriant South. He had taken up a big run
before the country became a “surveyed district,”
between _ the Tumut and the Bogongs, and had
stocked it with what capital remained after he had
made the few hundred acres immediately sur-
rounding his homestead-site a freehold, “A second-
fleeter ” himself, he had married, as soon as he
was “ freed,” the daughter of a fellow-” lag.” His
child, Kate, was born at the Hawkesbury Farm.
Educated in Sydney, she had not been brought to
the Station till after her mother’s death. She had
then been sent-for by her father, “to keep him
company,” as he said; he was so lonely ; and she
obeyed. A few months passed, and he, too, died ;
and she was left alone—owner of a great and un-
encumbered property, with, in addition to her
run, three maize-farms on the alluvial flats of
Tumut, and £SOOO at 15 per cent, interest—
mortgages in the “ Thirties” were worth holding—
with 30 assigned and 10 free servants at her beck
and call; with youth, and health, and exceptional
beauty—-and an unhappy spirit. Upon the golden
fruit which Fortune gave her, there was one fatal
speck which eat into its heart and robbed it—to
Kate Latchford’s palate—of all sweetness. She
was one “lag’s” daughter, and the grand-daughter
of another. Proud and sensitive naturally, her
disposition grew prouder and more hardened year
by year, as new vistas of life opened before her
gaze, only to be blocked by that one stain.
Brooding constantly on her darkened ancestry,
she exaggerated its effect on her own career.
Suitors came to her, of no purer descent, and her
reply was : “ What? Bring children into the world
to have the finger of scorn pointed at them—
never.” And to others, sons of families whose
ancestral skeletons had never worn the broad
arrow, her answer was the same. She was too
brave, too proud, to desert the field and seek
retirement in another land from scenes that daily
recalled to her mind thraldom (for so she regarded
it); so she trod her path alone, generally misun-
derstood, and, save for little Mrs, Derby, and
using the word in the exact sense, friendless.
One woman out of ten thousand so far as mental
capacity for managing a great and various pro-
perty went; this peculiar notion of hers, mono-
maniac almost in its intensity, made her daily
round one of acute torture. To have to do with
brutalised and devilised convicts inflicted upon
her an unremitting pang, for her morbid con-
sciousness would remind her that between them
and her there was one link, one mutual tie : they
were alike Children of the System. She hated the
necessity for employing assigned labour; hated
the free labour because she could not get enough
of it; hated the convicts for her kinship with
them; hated herself for hating them.
* * *
Denham’s trustworthiness and his ap-
titude for control which had rapidly mani-
fested itself ' was therefore a godsend to her.
Gradually, in token of his usefulness, she ex-
tended to him every privilege allowed under the
Regulations, and several that were not. She gave
him wages and permitted him to speculate in a
purchase of maize, and contemplated (though this
he knew not) advancing to him the money to stock
a 640-acre farm as soon as he was due for his
“ticket.” She would take his opinion, also, on
other matters besides those connected with the
the management of her business affairs. With
some of his first wages he had sent to Tegg,
the Sydney bookseller, and had ordered a few
books. These, of had come addressed to
her care and she had opened the parcel. Taking a
volume mechanically in her hand, she glanced at
a few pages. The book was an edition of Words-
worth. Fairly well-taught in the subjects included
in the course of a fashionable Sydney board ng-
school of the late Twenties, she knew nothing
of literature. Denham, for his age, was well-
read ; and the occasional talks between them
about books which arose did both of them
good. They helped to satisfy a craving of Den-
ham’s nature, while they stimulated a wholesome
desire for culture in hers, to which she had hitherto
been a stranger. But talks about business, about
the land (which was beginning to.be the problem
it still is), about politics generally and about
books, made up their intercourse. He was the
confidential man, and. of course, knew her position
thoroughly, but she never invited his confidence.
She franked the letters he addressed to his mother
in England, but never asked a word about his
relations or his antecedents, nor put a single in-
quiry as to the circumstances under which he was
transported. She never asked him to share a
meal with her; never asked him, indeed, to sit
down in her presence ; never called him by his
Christian name, but always “Denham.” She was
ever the Mistress, and he ever the assigned man.
She enforced the utmost respect of word and
gesture, even to the morning salute; and he granted
it, as became a Government man who had for-
feited his individuality when he became a criminal.
The maintenance of this disciplinary relationship
kept alive his sense of inferiority and forbade him
to forget that ho was, socially— not a cipher, cer-
tainly, but a number—No. 38,328. But for it, he
would have been as happy as was possible under
any conditions of penal servitude. He was
esteemed by his mistress and by Derby, in whom
he found a staunch friend and sound adviser ;
and with respect to the station-hands and to
the outsiders with whom Miss Latchford’s busi-
ness brought him into contact, ho was obeyed by
the one, and viewed by the other class with at
least the form of respect. With the assigned
servants he had many a tussle in his capacity of
manager, and been subjected to many a brutal in-
sult, but in time he boro down all opposition. His
success was the more notable as the neighbouring
station-overseers ruled their assigned labour
chiefly with the lash. Denham, when the reins of
management were in his hand, never reported a
man to the Tumut Bench for a floggable offence,
except once, and then only for cruelty to a horse.
But, suddenly, just as was born the New Year,
before whose close he would be practically a free-
man by virtue of a ticket-of-leave, there came the
end—or what seemed to be the end—to all his
peacefulness and hopefulness of mind. Like the
effect of a storm which, precipitating itself from a
smiling sky into the heart of an inland lake, beats
with a tropic fury the placid water into tempestu-
ous rage, so were the smoothly-rippling relations
between Denham and Miss Latchford fearfully
interrupted.
She had him flogged.
* * *
One of Miss Latchford’s assigned servants,
a mere lad, nicknamed “The Cockney Kinchin,”
was kept about the house to perform some non-
descript duties. Part page, part stable-boy, part
gardener’s-assistant, he had access to the whole
establishment, and being a born thief, preserved
his larcenous faculties in undiminished lustre by
constant practice. He could no more help stealing
than he could help breathing; all was fish that
came to his net, from the silver thimble of the
mistress to the groom’s stirrup-leathers. He rarely
escaped detection; he knew if it was his fate to
steal, it was equally his fate to be found out; and
he stole all the same. Society had punished him
when it should have studied him. Afflicted with
the criminal neurosis, he was less a law-breaker
than a problem in morals. Miss Latchford, how-
ever, looked upon him as simply a boy who, being
thievish, required punishment, and, milder reme-
dies failing to effect reform, had threatened him
on the discovery of his next offence with the lash.
The disciplining of the farm and station-hands
she loft to Denham ; that of the house-servants
she reserved to herself.
On an early January Sunday, the “Cockney
Kinchin ” stole a silver pencil-case, which Miss
Latchford had thoughtlessly laid down for a
minute in her sitting-room. The trinket was
found on him, and he was ordered to be ready at
seven o’clock the next morning, to be taken into
Tumut for report to,the magistrates—the Monday
happening to be the fortnightly court-day.
The lad begged Overseer Denham’s intercession.
He promised it, and was crossing from his office
to his mistress’ apartments when, by an evil,
chance, she came out on the back verandah.
Things might have been different had he met
her alone;
Saluting her and doffing his “cabbage-tree,” he
begged her to resign her resolve; and tried to
show her that, wliile kindness and education
might reform the “Kinchin,” punishment was
powerless to that end.
Now, Miss Latchford was one of those fatally-
conscientious persons who “stick to their word ”
at whatever prejudice to their better judgment,
and at whatever cost to her interests.
“Never,” she said, sharply. “I have said he
shall bo flogged, and he shall be !” Then some
demon prompted her to add: “And you forget
yourself—if you cross my will, I’ll have you flogged
as well. ”
She spoke loudly and the numerous hands about
enjoying their Sabbath rest, heard—and were in-
terested, yon may be sure !
“Oh, no,” rejoined Denham, with a smile,
“you would not do that!” He had meant the
answer as a respectful sort of joke. She read it as
conveying defiance and a subtle presumption, as
though he knew he was too valuable to her to be
treated as another assigned servant would be in
the case of disobedience.
Was she to be sneered at, flouted, disobeyed, by
her assigned man and in the presence of other
convict labour?
“Won’t I?” she said; “I’ll show you who’s
mistress, my flash chap.” (She lapsed into slangy
phrases in her passion.) “I’ll show you that if
I’m a lag’s daughter I’m also a lag’s boss. I'll
take you in myself to the beaks to-morrow and get
you fifty. ”
The Sabbath peace that penetrated even into
the kitchens and the men’s huts at Latchford’s
that evening was unusually peaceful and Sabbatic.
There was something so consoling in the fact that
the Overseer was “booked for the beak.” After
all, there must have been a good deal of “ human
nature ” in the assigned hands : they felt so very
virtuous over Denham’s downfall.
“He’ll get a lessen,” quoth She-Oak Jim, in
the stockman’s hut—Jim was an amiably-disposed
gentleman with 23 convictions—“ not to be so
and— —and uppish when he’s ’ed a taste o’ th’ cat.”
* s *
Faithful to her determination, Miss Latch-
ford took Denham and the “Kinchin” into the
Tumut Court next day.
Mr. Slyde, of Sydney, P.M. and Commission-
agent, mindful 6f possible exigencies on a station-
run by assigned labour, had furnished his fair
client with half-a-dozen sets of “darbies.”
At seven o’clock on the Monday morning she
stood on her verandah-step, and as Denham and
the “ Kinchin ” brought round the dog-cart in
which all three were to travel to Tumut, she flung
a pair of the shiny, steel things at the former’s
feet. Looking straight at the Overseer, she
ordered him to fix the one bracelet to tbe “ Kin-
chin's” left wrist. One mute look of protest he
gave her, and then obeyed.
She descended the step—and with her own
hands snapped the other on to Denham’s right
wrist. She motioned them to get into the veh.Vi
I hey scrambled up somehow, and she follow^
* * *
She timed, their arrival at the court-hous
just as the knot of customary loungers was at '•?’
thickest, immediately before the openin'* of h
sittings. Apparently she had made up her mi, ?
to spare Denham no single drop of humiliatin' 1
The chat and jokes of the loaf its merged hit
startled silence as they saw that the “ g o „° a
Beauty ” meant to flog her overseer. That tin?
was her determination was as plainly to be read **
her face as though it were printed in bold type ° U
■She called to the Sergeant to take the charge •
he was passing from his office into the court-romf
with the Police-Magistrate. Wonderingly j U
came forward, and touched his shako to her. 116
“ What is it, Miss Latchford ?” he asked.'
“ 1 charge the boy with larceny ; the man with
insubordination. ” 1
The Sergeant repeated, hesitatingly, “Insubor
dination ? You know that means flogging, Miss
“ I do ! ” she said ; and Derby knew her too well
to attempt to alter her mind when she spoke h
that tone. He said no more, and directed a con-
stable to remove the prisoners. With Denham"
he found a chance to speak and was informed ~e
the facts of the case. ' ‘
# # %
At this period, the bare and unsupported
testimony of an employer all but invariably
sufficed to prove any save a capital change against
an assigned servant. The only hope the accused
had was to break down the employer’s testimony
by cross-examination. The police never interfered
between master and man ; that is, on the man’s
side; but all that Sergeant Derby could do'for
Denham he resolved to attempt.
Miss Latchford’s evidence against her overseer
was. couched in general terras ; the substantial fact
she unconsciously exaggerated, and distorted in
her passion—but it served. He had interfered
to prevent the punishment of the boy, Harris (alias
the Cockney “Kmchen’.) charged with larceny, and
was thus insubordinate. Now, “insubordination”
was the most heinous offence within summary
jurisdiction; and Convict Denham, declining to
cross-examine the prosecutrix, was forthwith sen-
tenced to fifty lashes.
As Miss Latchford was leaving the box, Ser-
geant Derby addressed the Bench.
“If you please, your Worship, as Assistant
Muster-Master, I apply for the cancellation of the
assignment of the prisoner to Miss Latchford.”
The Mistress of Hereford Station had not
bargained for matters taking this turn. She had
never given a thought to the contingency that she
might bo deprived of Denham’s services. But
Derby was within his powers in making the
application. Though, as Sergeant, he might not
intervene between her and her assigned man as
Assistant Muster-Master, he was entitled, just
grounds Being forthcoming, to make such an
application as that he had. now preferred to the
court.
“What are your reasons, Sergeant?” questioned
the P.M. _ “Is the application made at the desire
of the assignee ? ”
“No!” said Miss Latchford ; “H, is against
my wish.”
The Court looked surprised.
Cor the Sergeant to have declared his real
reason would have been to have the Court against
him. But he was resolved that Miss Latchford
should not regain Denham’s services, so he
diplomatised.
“ The prisoner, your Worship, was Miss Latch-
ford’s overseer. Now, if he goes back after
punishment to her employ, and is re-instated, his
authority over the other Government men would
be gone. If ho is not re-instated, they will—l
know these men, your Worship—they will revenge
themselves upon him in his daily association with
them for having exercised a former authority over
them. They will act in such a way that His
Majesty’s peace would be endangered.”
Now, if there was one thing that the P.M. had
at heart more than another, or one phrase oftener
than others on his lips, it was “ His Majesty’s
peace.” Derby’s plausible argument told, and the
order was made, the Court courteously inclining
its learned head to Miss Latchford, as iie said, in
suave tones ;■ —
“I think it is best —er—-Miss Latchford, tu
relieve you—er —of Convict No. 38,328’s presence.
It would be very unpleasant—er—for you were
there any disturbance—er—on your estate.”
The mistress of Hereford scarcely heard the
words. She no sooner grasped the fact that she
had lost Denham’s services than the scales fell
from her eyes, and she saw (to do her justice) not
alone his money value to her, but those things
which no money could purchase—his fidelity,
intelligence, and zeal. Stirred by an irresistible
impulse, she moved towards where, a minute
before, he was standing, with some vague, ill-
defined intention of begging his pardon. But he
bad gone ; or rather had been removed, and was
at that moment stripping in the court-yard. The
spotlessness of Justice in N.S.W. tempux Will.
IV. was only equalled by the promptitude with
which her fiats were carried out.
# # #
Subdued, with strange emotions struggling
in her heart, and with a most unwonted desire for
that feminine panacea, “ a good cry,” Miss Latch-
ford thought she would pay Mrs. Derby a visit,
while waiting till the wretched Kinchin had
recovered from his whipping sufficiently to go
for her dog-cart to the Woolpack Inn.
She was making her way to the front of the
premises immediately occupied by the “little
woman,” when the Sergeant confronted her.
“You want my wife, Miss Latchford?” he
asked, with a stern gravity.
She could not trust herself to speak. She
nodded.
“ Mrs. Derby is not in—to you,” he said.

The Harvey Lamp has been adopted by the

mediums. Over 6000 Harvey Lamps are m use
in this colony, and for all lighting purposes are
giving universal satisfaction. These Lamps can
